The author of this book, having completed for you, my children, this book, which is the complete portion of the science of medicine, and having reached its peak in clarity and explanation, saw fit to complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of practical application.
Because practical application in our country and in our time is entirely lacking, so much so that its study has almost ceased and its effect has been cut off. But only a few traces of it remain in the books of the ancients, which the hands have corrupted, and error and confusion have affected, so that its meanings have become obscure and its benefit distant.
Therefore, I saw fit to revive it and compose this treatise on it by way of explanation, clarity, and abbreviation, and to provide images of cautery irons and other instruments of practice, since it is an addition to the explanation and an illustration of what is needed.
The reason there is no skilled practitioner with skillful hands in our time is that the craft of medicine is long, and its practitioner must first exert himself in the science of anatomy, as described by Galen. So that he may grasp the uses of the organs, their arrangement, temperaments, connections, and separations, and know the bones, nerves, and muscles, their number and exits, the pulsating and static veins, and the locations of their exits.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are numerous in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of the hand. We have mentioned a part of this in the introduction to this book.
For whoever is not knowledgeable about the anatomy we have mentioned is liable to make mistakes that kill people, as I have witnessed often among those who have pretended to this knowledge without study or understanding.
For I saw an ignorant physician who incised a swine-like tumor on a woman's neck, injuring some of the arteries of the neck, causing the woman to bleed to death before his eyes.
I saw another physician, attempting to remove a stone from an elderly man, who had a large stone, acting rashly and extracting it with a piece of the bladder tissue. The man died about three days later. I was called to remove it, and I saw from the size of the stone and the patient's condition that I could not have done it.
I saw another physician who earned a living practicing medicine among the merchants of our town.
A black boy, who had a fracture in his leg near the heel, with a wound, came to him. The physician, in his ignorance, hastily applied splints and bandages tightly to the fracture, over the wound, leaving no breathing space for the wound.
Then he indulged his desires, and left him for days, instructing him not to loosen the bandages until his leg and foot were swollen and he was close to death.
I was summoned to him, and I quickly loosened the bandages. He found relief and his pain lessened, but the damage to the limb had already taken hold and I could not prevent it.
The damage continued to progress in the limb until he died.
I saw another physician treat a malignant tumor. After a few days, it ulcerated, and the patient's condition worsened.  This is because, if the cancer is purely a black humor, it should never be treated with iron.
Unless it is in a limb that can be completely removed.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work can be divided into two categories: work that is accompanied by safety, and work that, in most cases, leads to injury.
I have warned throughout this book of any work that involves risk and fear, and you should avoid and reject it.
So that the ignorant may not have an opportunity to speak ill or find fault, take care, exercise caution, and treat your patients gently and with certainty.
Use the best path leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult-to-cure illnesses.
Keep yourselves away from that which you fear might lead to doubt in your religion and worldly affairs, for this will be more lasting for your reputation and elevate your standing in this world and the next.
Galen, in some of his precepts, said: "Do not treat a disease badly, and thus you shall be known as bad physicians.
This article is divided into three chapters.
The first chapter.
In cauterization by fire and cauterization by a sharp medicine, a categorized treatise from the heel to the foot, descriptions of instruments, cautery irons, and everything necessary for manual work.
The second chapter.
On incision, lancing, bloodletting, cupping, surgeries, removal of arrows, and similar procedures, categorized and arranged, and depictions of the instruments.
The third chapter.
On fractures, dislocations, treatment of sprains, and similar conditions, categorized and arranged from the heel to the foot, and depictions of the instruments.
The first chapter.
On cauterization.
Before we describe the procedure, it is necessary to mention the benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
To say that the benefits and harms of cauterization are a lengthy discussion, a precise science, and a hidden secret, many wise men have spoken about it.
And they differed, but I have summarized their brief statements, fearing to be overly lengthy.
I say that cauterization is generally beneficial for every bad temperament, with or without matter, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without matter, and the dry temperament without matter.
As for the dry temperament with matter, they differed. Some said that cauterization is beneficial in it, while others said the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable for a disease caused by heat and dryness because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to be cured of a hot, dry disease with a hot, dry remedy.  The one who says the opposite says that cauterization with fire may benefit from a hot, dry disease occurring in people's bodies. Because when you add the human body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the human body cold.
And I say as he says, because experience has revealed that to me many times, but one should not conceive of this matter except those who have diligently practiced and trained in the art of cauterization extensively. And he who stands on the differences in the temperaments of people, and the nature of diseases within themselves, their causes, symptoms and duration.
As for the other temperaments, there is no fear of them, especially cold, moist diseases.  All physicians agree on this, and they do not differ in the benefit of cauterization in them.
Know, my children, that the secret of cauterization with fire, and its superiority over cauterization with a burning medicine, lies in this: fire is a single element whose effect is confined to the organ cauterized, and it does not harm another connected organ except with a minor harm.
Cauterization with burning medicine may extend its effect to other organs, and it may possibly create a disease in the organ that is difficult to treat, and may even kill.  Fire, because of its noble and esteemed essence, will not do this unless it is excessive.
This has become clear to us through experience, due to the length of service and care in the craft, and through the understanding of the facts of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discourse.  Had it not been unbefitting this book of mine, I would have presented to you the secret and manner of fire's action on the body, and its eradication of diseases, using a philosophical and demonstrative argument that would be too subtle for your comprehension.
Know, my children, that they have differed regarding the time when cauterization is most suitable, and have declared the best time to be spring.
I say that cauterization may be suitable in any season, for the harm caused by the season is outweighed by the benefit that cauterization brings. Especially if the cauterization is for a strong, urgent, and serious ailment, which cannot tolerate delay, because a greater calamity might follow than the slight harm caused by the season.
Do not, my children, think what the general public and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization which cures a disease will never return and thereby becomes a blemish.
And it is not as you suppose, because cauterization is like a medicine that changes the temperament and dries out the moisture, which is the cause of the ailments.
However, cauterization is superior to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
The disease may return at some time in the future, depending on the patient's temperament, the strength and severity of the illness, the gathering of humors in the body, and the patient's negligence in acquiring proper nourishment and similar causes.
Unless the disease for which cauterization is used is a mild one, in a part of the body with few humors and moisture. For example, cauterizing a tooth for pain and similar conditions, the pain may not return, and this will be the least of the possibilities.
Also, the common saying that cauterization is the last resort in medicine is correct, not in the way they think, because they believe that no treatment will help after cauterization, and the matter is otherwise.
The meaning of "cauterization is the last resort in medicine" is that when various treatments have been applied to a disease without success, and then cauterization is used as the final measure and is successful, *then* cauterization is considered the last resort. Not in the sense understood by the common people and many ignorant physicians.
The ancients stated that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, saying this because of gold's moderation and noble nature. They said that the cauterized area does not become inflamed, but this is not universally true.
Because I have experienced it, and found that it works in some bodies but not others. Cauterization with gold is better than with iron, as they said, except that if you heat a gold cautery in the fire, you cannot determine when it is heated to the desired degree of gold's redness.
Because cold quickly affects it, and if you increase its heat in a fever, it melts in the fire and becomes molten, thus causing the craftsman to be occupied.  Therefore, cauterization with iron is faster and closer to correctness for practical use, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections, ordered from head to foot, to make it easier for the student to understand, God willing.
The First Chapter.
In cauterization of the head, a single cauterization.
This cauterization is beneficial for excessive moisture and coldness in the brain, which are the causes of headaches, frequent colds from the head to the eyes and ears, excessive sleepiness, toothache, and sore throats. And generally, for any illness caused by coldness, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and lethargy, and similar ailments.
The method for this cauterization is to first order the patient to induce vomiting with a purgative that clears the head for three or four nights, depending on the patient's strength, age, and habits.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor, then have him sit before you, squatting, with his hands on his chest.
Then place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes.  Wherever your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive oil cautery, which is of this form.
If the bone is exposed to the extent of a bodkin head or a carbuncle, lift your hand. Otherwise, reheat your hand with the same iron, or with another, if it has cooled. Until you see the bone as I described, then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a piece of cotton in it. Place it on the area and leave it for three days.
Then apply a piece of cotton soaked in ghee and leave it until the heat from the cautery subsides.  Then treat it with the four-fold ointment until it heals, God willing.
They say that a wound that remains open prolongs the pus, which is better and more beneficial.
Some have said to cauterize the skin to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the bone's thickness burns. Then, to remove the burnt bone, and then to treat.
Others said that one should exaggerate the cauterization so that it strongly affects the bone, causing it to fall off like a carat or a small coin.  They claimed that this releases the vapors of the head from that spot, and that the wound should be left open for a long time.
Then treat until it heals. I do not find these two types of cauterization at all beneficial except in some individuals, and by way of chance.  I prefer to leave them alone, and with safety, if it be true, as the head weakens when its natural connections are disrupted as we have seen in other limbs.
Especially when the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cauterization is safer and better in my view. Use it, and you will be safe, God willing.
The Second Chapter.
On cauterizing the head as well.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head, and this persists in the patient, and if the applications, poultices, flogging, ointments, and bandages have been used, and especially if the single cauterization we described did not help, then examine. If the patient's head is strong by nature, and not weak, and if he experiences severe cold, cauterize another small area above that one. Then cauterize each horn of his head with a cauterization until the thickness of the skin is gone and the bone is revealed to the extent we have described. And cauterize a spot at the back of his head, in the area known as the "axe" [or "the place where the axe lies"]. Be gentle here and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience intense pain unlike the pain of all other head cauterizations. I will mention this cauterization in its proper place.
The cauterizing instrument used for the horns and back of the head should be gentler than the one used for the middle of the head. This is its form/image.
Chapter Three.
Cauterization of the chronic sister.
If, in the cleft of the head, pain occurs with headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then have the patient purge with medicines that purify the head, and use all other treatment mentioned in the classifications of diseases. If this is not effective, then cauterization in this case is twofold. Either by cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
As for cauterization with the burning medicine, one takes a single clove of garlic, peels it, and cuts its ends off both sides. Then, with a wide scalpel, incise the painful area of the temple until there is a subcutaneous space large enough to accommodate the clove.
Then, insert it under the skin until it disappears.  Then, press firmly on it with a firm pressure. Leave it for about fifteen hours, then remove it and take out the garlic. Leave the wound for two or three days, then bandage it.
Place a cotton swab dipped in ghee on it, until the area becomes suppurative.  Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.  If you prefer, you may use some of the burning medicines which I have given in the 18th article on burning medicines.
As for cauterizing with iron, it is done in this manner: heat the cautery iron, which is depicted as follows:
It is called the nail-headed cautery because its head resembles a nail, with some serrations on it, and a small protuberance in the middle.
Then, place it on the painful area, and hold your hand while slowly rotating the iron, burning the skin to about half its thickness. And lift your hand so you do not burn the vein underneath and cause bleeding; then soak a cotton swab in salt water and place it on the area, leaving it for three days. Then, treat it with ointment by applying it to the cotton swab until healed, God willing.
If you wish, cauterize this vein with the sharp, knife-like part of the cautery, preserving it from cutting the vein in this chronic vein, especially.
Chapter Four.
Cauterizing the chronic vein.
If you have treated the vein using the aforementioned treatments and what we have mentioned in the classifications of diseases, and the treatment has not been successful, and you see a cause beyond what we have described, such as initial cauterization with medicine or cauterization by fire, do not proceed. You should heat the knife-like cautery until it whitens, after having marked the painful area with a line about half a finger long or similar.  Hold your hand once, pulling it firmly until the vein is cut and reaches the bone.
However, you should be cautious not to touch the jaw that moves during chewing, lest you burn the muscle or nerve controlling it, causing spasms.
Be cautious and vigilant about the bleeding of the artery you cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially if one does not know what to do and has not had prior successful experience; abandoning the procedure is preferable.
The treatment of accidental bleeding from an artery will be discussed in its proper place in the book, God willing, if you find a condition that this cauterization cannot handle.
If you find the patient's body able to withstand it, cauterize the center of the head as described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, use the cauterization we mentioned in the chapter on cutting an artery with the two-bladed cauterizing iron; it is a better and more effective cauterization than this one.
Chapter Five.
On cauterizing earaches.
If an earache occurs due to cold and is treated with laxatives and other treatments we have mentioned in the classification, but the pain does not subside, then heat the cauterizing iron, which is called the dot, which is its image.
Then, after heating it, apply it to the entire ear, moving it around, or around both ears if the pain is in both. Move the cauterization away slightly from the base of the ear after marking the location with ink. The cauterization should be about ten dots on each ear, or thereabouts. Then treat the locations until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Six.
In cauterizing the fistula.
The fistula that is treated by cauterization is the type that arises from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
One should avoid cauterizing the type that arises from dryness and nerve tension.
When you have treated this type of fistula with irrigation, massage, and gargles, and your treatment has not been successful, then the affected area should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly below the temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the opposite side of the affected area, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The cauterization should be made with one cauterization opposite the upper part of the ear, slightly below the crown of the head, and another in the temple, its length equal to the length of the thumb.
With the cauterization, apply your hand until you burn about half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cautery, which is a type of knife, but slightly gentler, as you can see. The knife should have a slight excess of thickness, then treat the area as previously described until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
On cauterization of chronic stroke.
If the stroke becomes chronic and you treat it as we have described, and your treatment is unsuccessful and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize four times. One cauterization on each temple, one cauterization in the center of the head as previously mentioned, and one cauterization at the back of the head, as previously described.
The cautery treatment, as previously mentioned.  A cauterization may also be applied to the mouth of the stomach, which would be more effective; then treat as previously described.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
One afflicted with phlegm should first be given large doses of laxatives and purgatives to clear the brain.
Then, shave his entire head and apply a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on dressings, several times.  For it is a form of cautery.
And do this in the same manner as previously mentioned. If he recovers thereby, do not proceed further; otherwise, cauterize him three times on the back of his head, in a row, from the top of his head to the base of his neck.
Leave the thickness of a finger between each cauterization, and then treat the cauterization as previously described.  If you wish to increase the treatment, and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle one.
If you wish to increase the treatment, cauterize on the temples, then treat him until he is healed.  The cauterizing instrument should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterization for paralysis and general relaxation of the body.
First, cleanse the head with the applications we have mentioned. Then shave the patient's head, and cauterize one cautery in the center of the head and one on each temple and one on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If, in a case of general relaxation of the body, more than this is required, and the patient can tolerate it and the illness is severe and stubborn, cauterize four cauteries on the vertebrae of his back. Cauterize deeply enough to burn most of the skin, then lift your hand and treat him as previously described until he is healed, God willing. The cautery should be olive-oil based.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterization for epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is due to phlegm should be cauterized.  First, his brain should be cleansed with large leeches and with all the other treatments we have described in the section, if the patient is strong and can tolerate taking the medications.
However, if he is a child who cannot tolerate medications, then for many days before, use gargles and mouthwashes that cleanse the brain, while also improving his diet.
Then shave his head, then cauterize with a single cauterization in the middle of the head, as described earlier, and another cauterization in his back. And on each temple, one cauterization. If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, cauterize with the cauterizations mentioned in the discussion of paralysis and relaxation of the body on the cervical and dorsal vertebrae, using an olive-oil cautery, in the manner previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this way.
Chapter Eleven.
In the cauterization of melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is putrid moisture and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauterizations that we mentioned in the case of paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess leaning towards black bile and the patient's body is moist and corrupt, purge what cleanses the brain as described in the division.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of flax like a circle, then place it in the center of his head, and the patient sits crouched, holding it from each side. Then take one pound of old sheep's butter.
Then heat it over a moderate fire until a finger can tolerate it when inserted, then pour it into the center of his head in the circle. Leave it until it cools.  Do this to the patient once a week along with his other good treatment until he is healed, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize him with many small, scattered punctures, without holding the cautery in your hand, but rather with a gentle touching.  This type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton pad soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter twelve.
On cauterizing water descending into the eye.
If you observe the descending water in the eye, by the signs mentioned in the classification, immediately treat the afflicted person.  Cleanse his head and protect it from all moisture.  Make him sweat in a bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize with a cautery in the middle of the head. Then cauterize twice on the temples, if the water is descending into both eyes, or if it is descending into one eye, cauterize on the temple of that side.
Then, using the cautery, cut all the veins and arteries under the skin.  Let the cauterizations be in the length and width of the temples, and be careful to prevent bleeding.
If you see anything of it, cut it at the appropriate place using whatever treatment is possible.  We will discuss the wisdom of dissecting and cutting arteries, and preventing bleeding.
One may also cauterize twice deeply on the back of the neck, under the bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the cauterization of chronic weeping sores.
If the eye's weeping is chronic and constant, and originates from veins and arteries on the surface of the head from outside the head, and you are certain that it is due to thick, cold, phlegmatic excess, cauterize it with the cauterization I described at the beginning of the descending water. One cauterization in the middle of the head, two on the temples, and two on the back of the neck beneath the bones. If more is needed, cauterize on each side of the eye's tear duct at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
On cauterizing a foul-smelling nose.
If treatment with what we mentioned in the classification was unsuccessful, promptly cauterize the patient's sinuses for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle area with an olive oil cautery iron. Then cauterize with a nail-shaped cautery iron, two cauterizations above the eyebrows, slightly beneath the hair, and be cautious of the artery, do not sever it. This is a depiction of the nail-shaped cautery iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid is relaxed due to disease or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize slightly above the eyebrows, two cauterizations in each direction, spaced from the temples, and the length of each cauterization should be equal to the length of the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cauterization with your hand, but only to the point where one-third of the skin is burned.  And the shape of the cautery iron should be of this type.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If the eyelashes turn inward and injure the eye, cauterization in this case is of two types. Either cauterization with fire, or cauterization with a burning medicine.  As for cauterization with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to allow the eyelashes to grow long and even if they are the type who pluck them.
If it is hurting when they are growing, bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so they don't move until they grow.  Once they have grown and are even, place the patient's head on your lap, then mark the eyelid with ink in the shape of a leaf.
Begin the mark near the eyelashes, then place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a basilisk under the eyelid.  Then heat an iron; this is its form.
Then cauterize it little by little in many applications, until the surface of the skin which is in the shape of the entire herb leaf is burned.  A sign of correct work is to see the eyelid has shrunken, and the hairs have risen from the eye's flesh.
Then remove your hand and leave it for three days, then apply a cotton pad with ghee until the scab is removed. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If any hair grows back after a while and the eyelid relaxes,
Then repeat the cauterization at that spot as you did at first. If the hair is on the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with a burning medicine, you order the patient to let the eyelashes grow long and even.
Then make a shape of an asafoetida leaf from paper, then take from the usual soap and quicklime, one dirham or similar weight of each, grind them thoroughly together, doing this quickly so that they do not dry. Then spread it out on the paper in the shape of the asafoetida leaf, and place it on the single or both eyelids. Place a cotton pad soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on the medicine and gently press it, moving it whenever the patient feels the burning of the medicine, because he finds a burning sensation like fire.  As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger.  Once the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And observe; if you see the eyelid has risen as it rises from cauterization by fire or cutting, then do otherwise, and reapply the medicine to the part where it has not taken effect and has not blackened, until you complete your work and the eye is cauterized. Then apply cotton with oil until the burning subsides.
Then treat it with palm ointment or similar until it heals.  You must be extremely careful during treatment that no medicine falls into the eye.  If the eyelid loosens after a few days and we fear there is something in the eye (referring to hair or debris).
Then reapply the medicine to the loosened part of the eyelid, specifically as you did at first, and treat it until it heals.  This is a picture of an asafoetida leaf.
Know that people's eyes vary in size and age, therefore adjust your cauterization accordingly.  The correct method is not hidden from those who have expertise in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
Cauterizing the fistula that appears in the eye's margin.
If you have treated the fistula with the methods described in the sections on diseases, and your treatment is ineffective, it is then necessary to cauterize it in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head on your lap, and have an attendant hold it firmly, preventing any movement or shaking of the head.
Then place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or the juice of a *barzquṭūn* (likely a specific plant) on their eyes.  Then heat the cautery iron, which is pictured in this way.
It should be hollowed out, like a bird of prey's feather tube, on the side where the cauterization will be done.  If you prefer, the other end can be pierced or solid like a rod; however, the hollowed one is better for your work, God willing.
Then, if the fistula is open, squeeze out the matter and dry it; or if it is not open, open it and remove the pus.  Then apply the extremely hot cautery iron to it, holding it until you reach the bone.
Keep your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose when cauterizing, so that your hand doesn't miss or the patient doesn't become distressed, and the cautery iron doesn't land on the eyelid and damage it. If you reach the bone on the first cauterization, then stop; otherwise, reheat the cautery iron a second time if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball with ghee and treat it with the dried ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply the strong, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, and treat it as described in the appropriate section, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a fistula, mentioned by some earlier authorities, is to carefully cut into the fistula, then place a thin cone, shaped like this, into the opening.
Pour in molten lead weighing approximately one dirham, and hold the cone firmly and steadily in place, ensuring the patient remains completely still so that the molten lead does not flow into their eye.  You should place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or water on the patient's eye.
The lead will burn the fistula and heal it remarkably. If the fistula heals with the described cauterization and treatment, then it is sufficient. Otherwise, it will be necessary to use nasal cauterization to return the fistula to its proper nasal passage, as will be detailed later in the appropriate section.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing a cleft lip.
A cleft in the lip, known as a fissure, often occurs, especially in the lips of young boys.  If the cleft is treated as described in the division but the treatment proves ineffective, then use a small, knife-like cautery iron, as illustrated.
Make its cavity as thin as a knife's edge, then apply it hot and quickly to the cleft itself, until the cauterization reaches the depth of the fissure.  Then treat it with the qirrotti until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing a fistula in the mouth.
If a tumor appears at the base of the gums, or on the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, and then ulcerates and bursts, forming a fistula from the discharge of pus, and treatment proves ineffective, then a cautery iron should be heated to a size suitable for the fistula.
Then insert it hot into the hole of the fistula, and hold it until the heated iron reaches its depths and its end. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterward with the treatment we mentioned until it heals, God willing.
If the matter is cut off and heals, otherwise, it is necessary to examine the place and remove the corrupted bone according to what will be described in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On loose teeth and gums.
If the gums are loosened by moisture and the teeth are moving, and treatment with medicine is ineffective, place the patient's head in your lap, then heat the cautery, whose image will come after this, after placing the tube on the tooth.
And insert the hot cautery quickly, and hold your hand for a little while, until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then lift your hand, and then repeat the cautery several times as desired, then the patient fills his mouth with salt water.
He holds it for an hour and spits it out.  Then the loose tooth will become firm, and the relaxed gums will tighten, and the bad moisture will dry up.
The Twenty-first Chapter.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is caused by cold or worms, and treatment with medicine has not been effective, cauterization can be done in two ways: either with oil or with fire.
As for cauterization with oil, take cow's butter and boil it in an iron ladle or a shell.  Then take a cotton ball, wrap it around the end of a rod, dip it in the hot oil, and quickly place it on the aching tooth. Hold it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth. Or, if you prefer, dip wool or a cotton ball in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, then place the heated iron on top of it until the heat reaches the base of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, use a copper or iron tube, somewhat thick-walled so the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.  Then heat the cautery iron, place it on the same tooth, and hold it until the iron cools.  Repeat this several times.
The pain will disappear either that day or the next.  After the cauterization, the patient should fill their mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out.  This is the shape of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you prefer, as is appropriate.  This is the shape of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing swine.
If swine are affected by phlegm and cold humors, and are not yielding to maturation with medications, and you desire rapid maturation, then heat the hollow cautery, the image of which is shown, having both ends pierced.
To expel the smoke during the cauterization from the other end, place it, protected, on the same tumor, once and again if needed, until it reaches the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, then make the cautery the size of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply a cotton swab dipped in clarified butter to remove what has been burned by the fire. Then treat it with ointment and bandages until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
On cauterization for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
If humidity predominates in the trachea, especially if accompanied by a cold temperament, the patient should first be purged with laxative medicines, then cauterized with a cautery on the notch of the neck at the base of the throat, in the low-lying area.
Be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery and burn only half of the skin.  Then cauterize again at the joint of the neck, at the end of the pearl of it, with a strong cautery.  Let the cautery be an iron one, in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what was previously mentioned until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On cauterizing lung disease and coughing.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold moisture, and the patient does not have a fever or consumption, and the illness is chronic, cauterize twice above the collarbones in the soft, empty, low-lying areas, and once more in the center of the chest between the breasts.
The cautery should be in the form of a nail, as previously described.  If you prefer, the cauterization may be by dripping with the cautery known as the *naqṭa*. Its form has been previously described in the treatment of ear pain.  The number of drops should be from thirty to a similar number.
Then treat him with what was mentioned previously until he is cured.  A cautery with three branches, shaped as described, may also be used. This type of cautery should be employed quickly, as it allows for three cauterizations in one application. This is the form of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus dislocates due to slippery moisture, or if, when replacing it, it does not remain fixed, so that it repeatedly dislocates upon the slightest movement, as we have observed, then the condyle must be reduced first. Then the patient lies on their back or on their right side, and the skin inside the armpit is lifted upward with the fingers of the left hand if the joint has dislocated inward.
Then the double-bladed cautery, whose image is this, is heated.
Then, with it, the skin is cauterized until it penetrates to the other side. The cauterization mark will be four cauterizations.  A three-bladed cautery may be used, in which case the cauterization mark will be six cauterizations; the blades will be of the sharpness of the cut.
This is the image of the three-bladed cautery.
Sometimes one more cauterization is added, making eight in total.  Then, crushed balls mixed with salt are applied to the cauterization.  The patient must rest and not move the limb for a time, until it strengthens.  If the dislocation is upward, this is rare.
Cauterize above the shoulder blade with one good, nail-like cauterization, or many small, dotted cauterizations. The joint will strengthen, the moisture will be expelled, and the patient will recover.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On cauterizing the stomach.
If a cold and excessive moisture develops in the stomach, upsetting its temperament, and frequent discharges occur despite various treatments proving ineffective, the patient should lie on their back, extending their legs and arms, and then be cauterized three times. Cauterize below the breastbone, to the extent of a finger, with a nail-like cautery iron.  Apply two more cauterizations below and slightly off the sides of the first cauterization to create a triangular shape.  Space them sufficiently apart to prevent coalescence should they become suppurated. The depth of the cauterization should be about two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and the cauterizations should form a triangular shape.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cautery in the center of the stomach, in this manner.
The cautery iron used for this single cauterization should be circular, in this manner.
The stomach may also be cauterized by puncturing.  For those who fear the large cauterization, mark the stomach with points of the desired size using ink, then cauterize them with a pointed cautery iron, and subsequently treat with the previously described remedy until healed.
Chapter Twenty-seven.
Concerning the cauterization of the cold liver.
If the liver presents pain due to coldness and dampness, or due to a violent wind, such that it has departed from its natural disposition to an excessive degree, and the patient has been treated as described in the classification but to no avail. The patient should lie on their back, and three cauteries of this shape and size should be applied to the liver, below the collarbones, where a person's arm ends.
After each cauterization, a space the thickness of a finger should be left. The cauterization should be along the body, straight, and do not hold the cauterizing iron tightly.
The burn should be about half the thickness of the skin, no more. The patient should be standing on their feet, or if unable to stand, lying with legs extended and arms raised. This is the shape of the cautery iron.
You may use a knife-shaped cautery iron for these cauterizations if you have skill and care in the craft, being careful not to extend the cauterization too far, as this will burn all the skin, causing burns to the abdomen and reaching the intestines, as the skin there is thin.  Know this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
Cauterization of a liver tumor.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know whether the tumor is in the liver's flesh or its membrane, if it is in the liver's flesh, the patient will experience heaviness and pain without intensity.
And if it is in the liver's membrane, the pain will be accompanied by intense severity.  If the physicians have exhausted their treatment, the patient should lie on their back, and the inflamed area should be marked with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire, the cautery which resembles a hoe, and this is its image. Cauterize it with a single cauterization, until all the skin is burned and the cauterization reaches the membrane, so that all the pus comes out. Then treat it with abscess treatments until it heals.
This type of cauterization should only be performed by those who have extensive experience in medicine, and whose hands have repeatedly dealt with such illnesses through experience.  Only then should they undertake such a procedure; to leave it to me is preferable.
The twenty-ninth chapter.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients recommended cauterizing a cold fistula with the roots of the zarawand plant.  Take a single, long, dried root of zarawand, as thick as a finger, and dip it in oil.  Then hold it in the fire. Then cauterize once between the collarbone and the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the blood vessels, angled towards the area under the chin.  And two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, angled slightly backward.  Another cauterization in the middle of the chest, and one above the stomach.  Three cauterizations from behind, one between the shoulders, and two on either side of the flank, below the cauterization between the collarbones.
One should not penetrate deeply with the cautery, but rather apply it superficially to the skin, as a smoldering action. Some of the ancients noted that some people used a flat iron-like cautery, heating it, and then inserting it between the ribs until reaching the tumor itself.
The pus will drain as described in the case of a liver tumor, and in this type of abscess, cauterization carries a risk; either the patient dies immediately, or a fistula develops in the area, with no cure.
Chapter Thirty.
In cauterizing the spleen.
If treatment of spleen disease, as described in the classifications, proves ineffective, cauterization may be employed in three ways, all correct: one is to cauterize three or four times in a line along the spleen.
In the form of liver cauterization described previously, with a space between each cauterization equal to or slightly exceeding the thickness of a finger.  Use the same cautery as described for liver cauterization, and do not insert the cautery deeply.  The patient should be lying on their back.
The other method is at the elbow. Lift the skin opposite the spleen, where the patient's left elbow ends. Raise the skin across the width of the body so the cauterizations are lengthwise.
Then, insert the cautery tips, very carefully, until they pierce the skin on the other side, then remove the cautery.  The cauterizations will be four in number, and if desired, use the three-pronged cautery.
Then, treat the cauterized area after allowing it to discharge pus for several days. This is more effective than any previous treatment.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is beneficial in dropsy of the abdomen, specifically. If you have treated the patient with the various treatments we described in the classification and your treatment is ineffective, you should cauterize four times around the navel and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, one on the spleen, and two behind his back, one opposite his chest and the other opposite his stomach.  The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then, leave the cauterization open to discharge pus for a long time.
Do not neglect the patient with further treatment after the cauterization, as is necessary to gather the necessary elements for a speedy recovery. The shape of the cauterizing iron used for the back is pointed, as previously described, and the one used for the chest is olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they (the feet) swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, it is necessary to cauterize the back of the foot at the point between the little toe and the ring finger.
Hold your hand with the cauterizing iron straight, do not bend it, and then lift your hand and do not return it; the yellow water will drain out, and the cauterizing iron should be of this shape.
Then cauterize the legs twice on each leg.  The cauterization should be done with the sharp end of the cauterizing iron, along the length of the leg, one below the knee and the other below that, towards the middle of the leg.  And cauterize each thigh twice, twice on each.
Leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, allowing the water to drain out, then treat it with the other methods we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On cauterizing dysentery.
If the dysentery is due to cold and dampness, weakening the binding and digestive powers of the stomach and intestines, and if other treatments have failed to cure it, and you see the patient strong enough to endure cauterization, then cauterize a large area on their stomach. As previously mentioned, cauterize the stomach with a circular cautery iron. Apply four cauteries around the navel using a fine nail cautery iron.  Apply one or two large cauteries to the cotton on the coccyx.
If you see a considerable amount of dampness and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize a spot on the pubic region and a spot on each hip; and possibly add two small cauteries near the large one on the stomach. This is an effective treatment, whose benefit will not fail, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cauterizing hemorrhoids of the anus.
If, in the anus, there are numerous chronic hemorrhoids, or a single one, composed of thick, cold humors or corrupted moisture, and the treatment mentioned in the previous section was ineffective, cauterize the patient with three triangular cauteries on the lower part of the spine, slightly below the table.
And cauterize a spot under the navel the size of two fingers.  If you judge that the patient's stomach is cold, and their food is not digesting, and you see their face swollen, cauterize a large spot on the stomach as previously described, and cauterize a spot on the liver. And another on the spleen with a nail-shaped cautery. Leave the cautery open for a time, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery that resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the same cut wart and exert pressure until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which the blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as described, then treat them with suitable ointments until they heal.  If a large cauterization is applied to the cotton, it will be more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-six.
On cauterizing fistula in the anus and surrounding areas.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and procedure described in its place, and avoids it, cauterization might provide a cure. If someone has a fistula that has existed for a long time, and pus and foul humours are flowing from it, the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then determine its depth with the probe, then heat the cautery, which resembles a spatula, and insert it hot into the fistula along the same path and to the depth that the probe reached. Repeat the cauterization until all the corrupted tissues are burned, once, twice, or three times, as needed.
Be cautious about burning a nerve, if there is one, or a large vein, if the fistula opens into the bladder or into the gut.
Take precautions from all these locations, and only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area, and you judge that it is not penetrating. Then treat the area until it heals. If the area seals up, and the discharges cease, and it remains like that for a time, then know that it has healed completely.
If the discharges do not stop, know that it is penetrating or has a corrupted bone in its depth, or something similar to what will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-seven.

If pain occurs in the kidney from cold or thick wind, and the patient's sexual potency is diminished as a result, it is necessary to cauterize the loins, specifically each kidney, with the nail cauterizer previously mentioned.  Perhaps we cauterize three times on the same area, creating three aligned cauterizations for a greater benefit.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
On the cauterization of the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder due to cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot hold his urine, cauterize once below the navel on the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and cauterize again to the right of the navel, and another to the left.
After the cauterization on each side, make a cauterization the size of a thumb's breadth below the back, or two cauterizations if needed.  The cauterizing tool should be a nail cauterizer, as previously described.
Chapter Thirty-nine.

Chapter Forty.
On the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
Erroneous humors may accumulate in the hip joint, causing dislocation.  A sign is the lengthening of one leg compared to the other when the legs are aligned, and a gap will be found at the site of the dislocation.  A roughly circular cauterization should be applied to the affected area of the hip joint itself.
After applying the cauterization around the joint, as if a circle were drawn, so that the joint itself is in the center of the circle.  Use the cautery shown as an example for cauterizing the stomach.  If this cautery is not available, cauterize with three cauterizations using a large olive-shaped cautery.
Make the cauterization deep enough to reach all layers of skin, then treat until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Forty-one.
In cauterizing the sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint due to cold and dampness, and the treatment mentioned in the classification was ineffective and prolonged, then the patient should be relieved of the thick humours with rotten seeds or prickly pear seeds, or similar ones.
Then cauterize.  Cauterization has two types: either with burning medicines or with fire. Cauterization with fire can be done in many ways. One of them is to cauterize the hip joint itself three times in a triangular pattern like this.
This will create a beneficial depth, with a space equal to the thickness of a finger between each cauterization. The cautery should be olive-colored.  A cauterization could be made in the center, on the head of the hip itself, resulting in four cauterizations.  Or, if you wish, cauterize with the circular motion previously mentioned in the treatment of hip dislocation, using one cauterization.
So that the circle surrounds the entire hip and burns the whole thickness of the skin.  This is the method of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If the pain extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize the thigh twice in the area where the patient indicates pain, and cauterize once above the heel, about four fingers to the outer side. The cautery should be a sharp one, and the depth of the cauterization should only be the thickness of the skin.
If the patient indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize where he indicates with the pinpoint cautery three, four, or more times, if necessary. If he indicates pain below the knee toward the leg, cauterize there with one sharp cauterization.
Be careful in all your cauterizations not to cauterize to the point of burning a major nerve or artery, lest you inflict a serious injury or a prolonged affliction upon the patient.
I have seen one and another who cauterized above the heel and overused the cautery, causing the leg to swell until the swelling reached the foot and the whole foot became perforated and the entire leg decayed.  Then diarrhea and death followed.  If the pain is in both areas, cauterize them in exactly the same way.
Some wise scholars have mentioned a hip cauterization with these characteristics:  A cup-like device is made of iron, with a diameter of half a cubit. Its opening has the thickness of a date pit or slightly less. Inside this cup is another cup, and a third.
The distance between each cup should be the width of a thumb. The cups are open on both sides and their height is about one or two thumbs. A handle of iron is firmly attached to the cups. This is its form.
Then it is heated in the fire until it is red-hot and throws off sparks.  Then it is placed on the affected hip, and the patient is lying on their sound side.  Three circular cauterizations are made at once. Then, the patient is left for three days, bandaged with ghee, and the wound is left open for several days.
Then it is treated with ointment until it heals.  The author of this book said that this type of cautery is rarely used because of its unpleasantness and horrifying appearance, and few can endure it.  However, it is a good cautery for those who can endure it, and who are afflicted in that location.
As for cauterization with burning medicines, one makes two cups resembling the rings in which the hinges of a door are inserted, of bronze or iron.
They will have a height of about two thumbs, and the distance between them will be the thickness of a finger. The bottom and top will be open, and they will be joined together in this manner.
Then place them on the affected hip, with the patient lying on their healthy side.  Hold your hand gently, then pour a little hot, sharp water between the circles.  Hold it for the length of an hour glass. The patient must endure the burning and stinging, as it will feel like fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then remove the instrument and wipe the affected hip with fresh water.  Leave it for three days and apply oil to it until the blackness of the burn disappears. Leave it for a number of days, allowing the pus to drain.  This is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals.  If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for it to hold the water, similar to what was done with the hip. This is the description of the sharp water.  I have demonstrated it in my treatise on the preparation of medicines.
Take one part each of potash and quicklime, grind them, and place them in a new pot with a single small hole at the bottom, just large enough for a rod to fit in. Place another glazed pot underneath the bottom of the first pot.
Pour fresh water over the potash and lime until it covers them by a finger's breadth, after you have thoroughly squeezed them with your hand. Leave the pot until the sharp water drips into the glazed pot below. Then collect all this water and pour it over fresh lime and potash.
It will then be extremely potent, effective in many medical procedures and cauterizing other parts of the body, as it acts like fire itself. Some medications can also cauterize the hip, such as safas, baladi honey, and lime mixed with soap, ground.
Galen recounted a story about an ancient physician treating hip and sciatica pain, greatly praising his method, claiming it required no other treatment and cured the condition in a single application.
Even, perhaps, a patient would be carried into the bath and emerge cured.  To obtain this remedy, take fresh green shiitrakh, or if fresh green isn't available, then take fresh dry shiitrakh. Grind it with some fat and apply it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg or thigh.
Apply the compress and leave it on for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning sensation subside. Then have them enter the bath.  When their body is damp, place them in a basin. The pain will disappear and the patient will be cured, God willing. If not cured, reapply the dressing after ten days.
He will be cured, God willing.  Know that no one should use these cauterizing remedies unless the body has emptied itself. Dioscorides mentioned that goat dung, when used for cauterizing sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization should be done in this way: take wool and soak it in old oil. Place it on the deep area between the thumb and the elbow, closer to the elbow. Then, take dry goat dung.
Then ignite it with fire until it becomes embers, then place it on the wool and leave it until it dies out.  Repeat this process until the sensation extends from the middle of the upper arm to the hip, and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cauterization is called cauterization with goat dung.
Chapter Forty-two.
On cauterizing back pain.
Pain in the back may arise from numerous causes, such as a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and similar occurrences. It may be due to the accumulation of cold, moist material.  Cauterization is applicable only in this type of case.
After the patient has vomited, and has been treated with substances like rotten herbs, the back should be cauterized where the pain is located, using three rows, the width of the table.  Each row should have five cauterizations, or more, as much as the patient's tolerance and strength allows.  First, mark the area with ink.
The cauterization should be done with a point cautery.  Alternatively, three or four cauterizations can be made using a medium-sized nail cautery, in this manner.
Chapter Forty-three.
On cauterizing the initial swelling.
This ailment often affects young children.  A sign of its initial stage in a child is shortness of breath when standing or moving, and at the end of the spine's vertebrae, a prominent bead will appear, protruding above the other beads.
If you see this and wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery, like this. Cauterize each side of the vertebra evenly.  If you wish, cauterize it around the vertebra with a pinpoint cautery in two or three rows, keeping the points close together.  Then treat the affected area until it is healed as described.
Beware that cauterization should be used for swellings arising from nerve spasms.
Chapter Forty-four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are caused by cold dampness flowing to any part of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, physicians commonly call it gout, especially.
If the cold gouty cause is treated with the methods we have described in the classification, but the pain does not go away, then cauterization will relieve it.  Cauterize the area around the joints of the legs with many cauterizations after emesis. The cautery should be olive-oil based, of moderate strength, in this manner.
And if you need to cauterize spots on the leg, use a pointed cautery. If the pain ascends to the knees or other joints, which often happens, cauterize each knee with three or four cauterizations on each side using the same olive-oil based cautery.
If more cauterization is needed, then cauterize, but do not make the cauterization too deep. The cauterization should only be about the thickness of the skin.  If the pain ascends to the hips or back, use the cauterization methods described in its section.
If the pain is only in the hands, cauterize around the wrists in a row on both sides. If the pain persists in the fingers, cauterize a spot on each knuckle and on the palm of the hand. If, after a few days, the pain ascends to the elbows or shoulders,
Cauterize both of them [the elbows or shoulders] on every side.  The patient should not neglect good treatment and taking medicines.  If the diet is improved and the phlegm is expelled, he will recover with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the cauterization of hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin, and some of the intestines and fat have descended to the testicle, and this is a recent onset, then the patient should be instructed to refrain from eating that day, and to use laxatives to evacuate the bowels.
Then he should lie on his back before you, and you should instruct him to hold himself until the fat or intestines emerge. Then you should push them back with your finger.  Then mark with ink under the hernia, on the pubic bone, a mark resembling a semicircle, its ends towards the upper body.
Then heat a cautery, its form is this.
Until it becomes white and throws sparks.  Then return the intestines or fat to their place within. Then have a servant place his hand on the area so that the intestines do not protrude again.  The patient's legs have been spread apart, and a cushion placed beneath him, and another servant ties his legs together.
And another on his chest, holding his hands. Then lower the cautery onto the same mark, your hand holding the cautery upright until it reaches the bone, or repeat it again if it did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And guard against the intestines protruding while cauterizing, so as not to burn them, which would cause the patient either death or a great affliction.  And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed.  And the cautery should be gentle, appropriate for children, and appropriate for adults.
Then treat the cauterized area with butter after three days, until the burn's dryness subsides, then treat it with other ointments until it heals. Let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound is sealed.
His food during his treatment should be what softens his bowels so that the intestines do not protrude when he moves or defecates.  Then, if he wishes to stand up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days.  Reduce exertion, fullness from food and drink, and loud crying.
If he follows this treatment, he will heal completely, God willing. I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its proper chapter, God willing.  As for a hernia that occurs in other parts of the abdomen, if you want it to not worsen, cauterize the hernia with a circular cautery, appropriate for its size. Let the amount of skin burned be about two-thirds of it, then treat it as we have described. It will not worsen, God willing.  Some of the earlier scholars mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After marking the hernia with ink, making a line across the top of the hernia and another line below it, place your hand in the middle and cauterize with a single, nail-like cautery. The first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If, in some part of the body, a fall or blow causes pain that persists for a long time and is not relieved by medication, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pinpoint cautery.  The cauterization of each part should be according to its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
And if the pain and fistula are alleviated, then he is cured from the first cauterization; otherwise, you should repeat the cauterization, for it is the nature of these pains to spread from the affected area to nearby ones, so it is necessary to follow up with cauterization until the patient recovers.
Chapter forty-seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for those afflicted with leprosy, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that arises from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize him, observe; if the leprosy is incipient and you have treated it as described in the classification, and it has not deteriorated or ceased, and you fear that the corruption will affect all his humours, cauterize his head five times. The first in the usual center of the head, the second below it, towards the forehead near the end of the hair, two on the temples, and one from behind on the nape of the neck.  Apply the cautery somewhat vigorously until it slightly affects the bone.
And scales will peel away from it, making it easier for the thick vapors to escape.  Cauterize the spleen as well, as previously mentioned.  However, if the leprosy has persisted and clearly appeared on the patient, then you should cauterize the head with the aforementioned cauterizations. One cauterization on the tip of the nose, two on each cheek, two on the cervical vertebrae, six on the thoracic vertebrae, one large one on the coccyx at the base of the tail, another above it on the same plate, and two on each thigh, one on each. Two on each knee, one on each, two on each shoulder, two on each elbow, two on the ribs of the chest, and cauterize each joint of his fingers and toes, and each heel of his feet and the wrists of his hands.
And be careful to avoid burning the nerves located on the back of the heels.  A cauterization may also be applied to the pubic bone, the mouth of the stomach, and the liver.  Know that the more cauterizations you apply, the more beneficial and effective they will be.
Know that the patient experiences less pain from cauterization than a healthy person, because his body is numb.  The cauteries should vary in size, depending on the organs and joints, as described in the characteristics of the instruments previously.
Then treat the cauterization with fine crushed carob, honey, and other remedies, until healing occurs, God willing.
Chapter Forty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness:  If a part of the body becomes numb and is treated with medications, ointments, and bandages, but does not heal, cauterize the same numb part with cauteries appropriate to the size of the affected area of the body;  your cauterizations should be slightly below the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until healed.  Cauterization may also be used for some numbness affecting the hand and foot, which occurs in the vertebrae of the back, at the point where the nerve that moves that limb exits, and this will remove the numbness.
Only those knowledgeable in the anatomy of the body and the exit points of the nerves that move the body should undertake this.
Chapter Forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of leprosy.
If leprosy persists and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it with a moderate depth of cautery, according to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and the color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose ointment, and the herb *Polygonum aviculare* (or *Polygonum hydropiper*), and dove's blood or the blood of birds of prey, one part of each. Mix them all together, apply it to a cloth, and bind it to the affected area until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it revolves.  Some sages have suggested a severe cauterization in the middle, but I do not see this as advisable, as I anticipate it will ulcerate. I have witnessed this several times.
Therefore, the correct approach is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we have said, or with multiple small cauterizations.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On cauterizing the abscess.
If an abscess occurs and is slow to mature, either because of the agent causing it, or the patient's age (if the patient is elderly and anemic), or due to time, and you wish to hasten the abscess's maturation, cauterize around it with many small cauterizations, dotted around.
Then leave it, for this will hasten maturation. If you wish to cauterize it further, heat the cautery iron, which is of this form.
and place it in the middle of the abscess until it penetrates the skin, and let the cauterization be from below, so that the discharge of pus is easy, then treat it with what is necessary until it heals.
Chapter fifty-two.
On cauterizing cankers.
A canker is a corruption that spreads through the tissue, consuming it, like fire consumes dry wood. If you see a canker in a place suitable for cauterization with fire, heat a cautery iron with many small and large nails, according to what suits the location of the canker.
Then cauterize it from all sides until all corruption is removed and nothing remains, then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur with oil to the cauterized areas until all scabs and corruption are removed.
Then treat it with healing ointments. If after three days you see that the flesh grows correctly without corruption, otherwise repeat the cauterization on the remaining corrupted areas.  The canker can also be treated with a strong medicine.
It acts as a substitute for fire, with faster effectiveness.  We have mentioned its treatment with strong medicine in the classification.  Take it from there when needed.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This affliction often occurs on the soles of the feet. It is a rough, painful condition.  Cauterization can be performed in two ways: either by fire or by strong water.
As for cauterization by fire, heat a hollowed-out cautery, resembling an eagle's beak, made of iron.  It should be sized to encompass the nail on all sides and have a thin edge.  Then, lower it, heated, onto the nail, and rotate your hand with the cautery around the nail until the cautery reaches the depth of the nail.
Leave it for three days, allowing pus to form. Then, dress it with crushed, salty wild bread, leaving the dressing on for a night. It will then detach from its roots.  Then treat the wound with a flesh-promoting ointment until healed, if the nails are not inverted.
It often occurs on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet.  Therefore, one should take a tube of copper, iron, or eagle's beak, and lower it onto the nail or wart.  Then, pour a small amount of strong water into the tube.
Hold the tube with your hand, gently rotating it, so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the nail, and the strong water penetrates to the root of the nail. The patient should endure the stinging of the strong water for a while.
Then, leave it. The nail will detach from its roots.  Do this one by one until all such things on the body are dealt with. Then treat the affected areas with flesh-promoting ointments after they have detached.
Chapter Fifty-four.
In the treatment of the scabies.
If scabies occurs due to cold in the nerves, or from a fever of the quarter, or something else, it is necessary to cauterize four or five times on the back, between each cauterization and one on the chest. And apply one cauterization on his stomach with the olive-oil cautery.  Then the scabies will subside and the cold affliction will mature rapidly.
Chapter Fifty-five.
In the cauterization of a wound occurring on the body.
Ugly boils may erupt on the body due to thick, corrupt cold matter.  Upon their first appearance, a gentle cauterization should be applied to the head of each boil with a heated stick, whose tip has been ignited with fire, or with the long root of a wild plant, or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
Boils, too, should be cauterized in their initial eruption in this manner.  Their active principle will not increase, but dissipate, and the patient will recover, but this should only be done after the patient has been bled (by venesection).
Chapter Fifty-six.
In the cauterization of bleeding occurring when an artery is cut.
Bleeding frequently occurs from an artery severed by an external wound, or when incising a tumor, or cauterizing a part of the body, and similar procedures, and its arrest is difficult.
If this occurs to someone, quickly place your index finger over the mouth of the artery, pressing gently until the blood is contained beneath your finger and nothing escapes. Then, take several small and large olive-shaped cauteries, and heat them in the fire until extremely hot.
Then, take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery ruptured.  Apply the cauterium to the same vein, after quickly removing your finger, holding the cauterium until the bleeding stops.
If the blood spurts forth when you remove your finger from the mouth of the artery and the cauterium cools, quickly take another cauterium from the prepared fire, and continue to do this one after another until the bleeding stops.
Take care not to burn a nerve, lest another affliction befall the patient.  Understand that if an artery bleeds, it cannot be severed, especially if it is large, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been severed. If it is severed, its extremities will contract and the bleeding will cease; or, it may be tightly bound with threads; or, drugs that will stop the bleeding and firmly constrict it may be applied.
As for those who attempt to sever it with a ligature, or by constricting it with cloths, or by applying burning things, and the like, it will not cease in that way, except rarely. If one of these situations presents itself without a physician or remedy being available, let them immediately place the index finger over the mouth of the wound itself, as we have described.
Press it firmly until the blood is contained and flows from above the wound and the artery, and the finger should not be removed from over it with very cold water constantly until the blood thickens and coagulates and stops flowing.  In the meantime, consider what cauterization or medication is needed.
The Second Chapter.
Concerning incisions, lancing, phlebotomy, and other surgical procedures.
In the first chapter, we mentioned every ailment in which cauterization with fire and caustics are suitable, their causes and reasons, and the instruments.  We have organized this into sections, from the toe to the head.
And I will follow the same path in this chapter, to make it easier for the student to achieve their goal.
Before I begin, you should know, my children, that this chapter contains more deception than the first chapter on cauterization.
Therefore, the warning must be more stringent, because in this chapter, the practitioner often risks expelling the blood upon which life depends, when opening a vein or cutting a tumor, lancing a boil, treating a wound, extracting an arrow, or cutting a stone, and similar procedures.  All of these carry deception and fear, and death often results.
I warn you against venturing into doubtful practices, for in this craft, you may encounter various individuals with diverse illnesses. Some are weary of their ailments and have grown indifferent to death.
Because of the severity of their illness and the prolonged suffering, their sickness shows clear signs of approaching death. Some offer you their wealth, hoping for health, but their illness is deadly.  You must not accept help from anyone with such characteristics.
Let your caution be stronger than your desire and eagerness. Do not undertake anything without certain knowledge of the likely outcome, and in all the treatment of your patients, employ foresight and warnings concerning the prognosis for recovery.
For in this, you will gain praise, glory, remembrance, and thanks. May God, my children, grant you wisdom, and not deny you righteousness and success.  For that is in His hand; there is no god but He.
I have arranged this chapter into sections, following the previous chapter on cauterization, from the head to the foot, so that you may find what you seek, God willing.
Chapter One.
Concerning the treatment of water that collects in the heads of children.
This ailment frequently affects children at birth if the midwife applies pressure to the child's head roughly.  It may also arise from a hidden, unknown cause.
I have not observed this ailment in anyone but children, and all those I have seen suffered a swift death. Therefore, I have decided to abandon its treatment.  I have observed a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger each day. Until the child could no longer sit up due to the enlargement of his head and the increase in moisture, until he perished.
This moisture either collects between the skin and bone, or under the bone on the peritoneum.  If the moisture is between the skin and bone and the swelling is small, a single horizontal incision should be made in the center of the head, approximately two fingerbreadths long, to allow the moisture to drain.  This is the method of the surgeon's scalpel.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling is larger, make two intersecting incisions, like this:
If the moisture is under the bone, and the sign of this is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides, and the water descends when you press it with your hand inwards, this is not hidden from you, then three incisions should be made in the middle of the head, in this way.
After the incision, all the moisture will drain out.  Then the incisions are closed with strips and bandages.  Then, apply poultices and oil from above for five days.  Then, remove the bandages and treat the wound with dressings and ointments.  Maintain a moderate head dressing, and provide the patient with all dry, low-moisture foods.
Until the body part strengthens and heals. Another method of incision is to observe where the bone of the swelling and the accumulation of water appear, because it may be more in the back of the head, or in the front, or in the right or left.
Therefore, aim the incision where the swelling and water accumulation appear.  Make the incision as you can, being careful not to cut an artery and cause bleeding, for from that bleeding the patient will die, along with the expulsion of the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears, known as the "hissers.
When someone suffers from a severe illness in the eyes or chest, and the usual remedies are ineffective, the best treatment is to cut these two arteries.
Therefore, if you wish to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery.  Then, hold the patient's neck tight with their garment, and look for where the pulse beats, in the two lowered positions behind the ears.  They are rarely hidden, except in some people.
Then, mark them with ink, and cut them with a dissecting scalpel, down to the bone.  This should be done with the head in a straight line, or if you prefer, you can insert the scalpel underneath the artery and cut upwards.  The length of the cut should be about two clenched fingers.
If the artery is cut, blood will flow out in a pulsating manner, spurting forward in a continuous stream.  If the artery is not visible, then measure from the ear about three fingers, mark it with ink, and cut down to the bone.  And what is necessary...
