About six ounces of blood will be released immediately, perhaps more or less, depending on the patient's strength and the fullness of their arteries.  Then, examine the wound.  If any part of the peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it off.
So that a hot swelling does not occur, then bind the wound with a linen thread and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic headaches or acute attacks brought on by sharp humidity and heat in the temples' muscles, or chronic severe headaches, and similar ailments, and treatment by various medical methods is ineffective, we have tried, in these conditions, to dissect the arteries of the temples or cauterize them, as described.
The procedure for dissecting them is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the visible artery in the temple should be located.  Its pulse will make it evident, and it is rarely hidden, except perhaps in some individuals.
Or, if the cold is severe and it is hidden, the patient should tighten their neck with their garment. Then, you should rub the area with a cloth or apply warm water until the artery is clearly visible. Then, take the scalpel, which is depicted in this way. Then, gently slice the skin with the scalpel until you reach the artery.  Then, insert a hook and pull it upwards until you remove it from the skin and free it from the membranes underneath from every side.  If the artery is thin, twist it with the hook's end. Then, cut off a section of it to the extent that its ends separate and constrict, and no bleeding occurs.  If it is not severed and cut, the blood will not drain at all. Then, drain the blood from six to three ounces.
If the artery is large, it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong doubled thread.  Let the thread be either of silk or of bowstrings, so that putrefaction does not quickly reach it before the wound heals, causing bleeding.
Then, cut off the excess between the ligatures.  Do this at that time, or after another time. If you wish, cauterize to the bone with a knife-shaped cauterizing iron, until its ends are severed.  This will accomplish the same work, or better, as we said.
However, if the patient has a fever or a heated temperament, because cauterization helps to destroy moisture, it will be more beneficial.  After removing the arteries, the area should be packed with old cotton and secured with tight bandages.  After healing, treat with dry medications that promote flesh growth and with compresses until he is healed, God willing.
If during your work, bleeding occurs from the artery, quickly cut it, either by cauterization, or fill the area with alum and press your hand firmly until the blood stops. If none of these are available, place your finger on it until the blood clots.
Apply very cold water to the area until the pain subsides and the wound is tightened properly.  A less difficult and easier way than cutting the artery is to cauterize the vein with this knife-shaped cautery iron, after marking the two places with ink.
Apply it very hot until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed at two places so that the distance between its ends is increased.  It will not heal with this cauterization at all. This is a picture of the cautery iron.
It should have two sharp blades, resembling small daggers, but much less sharp than a knife, because if they are as sharp as a knife, the cold will reach them quickly, and the flesh will not cut quickly.  If they have some thickness, the heat of the fire will remain within them.
Cutting the flesh quickly is the best, easiest, and simplest method.  Make sure the distance between the blades is about the thickness of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
For the treatment of constant, hot tearing from the eyes.
If the tearing is constant and originates from the veins on the skull's exterior, and no medicinal treatment is helpful, and you see the patient's face has become red. He feels a crawling sensation in his forehead like ants crawling, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their edges eroded and their eyelids stuck together due to the intensity of the tears.  The earliest practitioners of this treatment resorted to this procedure, calling it "treatment with the sword."  It involves instructing the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then, three parallel incisions are made on the forehead, running along the forehead's length. Each incision is about two fingers long, parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second one slightly off from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third one on the other side.
And be mindful not to sever the arteries on either side, and keep your hand away from the contact of the jaws. After each incision, leave a space of three fingers clasped together. Have with you prepared sponges or many dry cloths to absorb the blood.
Then, insert the sharp, two-pronged scalpel, which is of this design. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, use it to peel away all the skin between the incisions, along with the periosteum on the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision, and then remove the scalpel. And insert into the first incision another instrument, called a scalpel, sharp on one side and smooth, blunt on the other.  This is its image.
Place the sharp side of the scalpel towards the flesh attached to the skin, and the smooth side towards the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision, and sever all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do the same from the middle incision to the other incision. After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, compress the areas from the clotted blood.  Then, in each incision, place a wick of cotton wool, and apply a dressing soaked in a mixture of syrup and oil, or vinegar and oil.
So that a hot swelling does not occur. On the third day, dissolve the ligaments and use plenty of lukewarm water for irrigation. Then treat it with basil ointment, rose oil, and other remedies used for treating wounds, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
On treating tears and discharges from the interior of the head to the eyes.
When someone suffers from frequent, severe, persistent discharge, and the eyes appear sunken and small, with impaired vision and ulcerated eyelids with eyelashes falling out. And there is severe, painful pain deep within the head, and frequent sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these materials and discharges come from numerous deep veins.  The best treatment is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is severe, painful pain deep within the head, and frequent sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these materials and discharges come from numerous deep veins.  The best treatment is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it slightly laterally, beginning the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and the incision should be to the bone. Keep your hand away from the moving temporal muscles involved in chewing.  Once the bone is exposed, and all the blood has been dried with a sponge...
Separate the edges of the incision with used cotton or flax thread, then secure it from above with bandages.  Soak the bandages with medicine and oil to prevent a hot swelling.  Once you have resolved it and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should rub the bone to encourage the growth of flesh.
Then treat it with a desiccated preparation that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts wheat flour and four parts of calphonium, and preparing an ointment from them.  This is used for flesh growth in wounds like these.
Chapter Six.
On treating things that fall into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic or semi-metallic stone, such as iron or glass; or a plant seed, such as chickpeas and nuts, and the like; or a liquid, such as water or vinegar, and the like.
Or if it is an animal, if a stone or a similar object that does not grow in the ear falls into the ear, expose the ear to the sun.  If you see the stone, put a little violet or ceruges ointment on it, then try to remove it by moving the head or by inducing sneezing with a spoon. And close the nostrils when sneezing occurs, after placing a bandage of cloth or wool around the ear and pulling the ear upward.  This treatment often removes it. If it does not come out, try to remove it with gentle forceps, which has this form.
If it is removed with the forceps, otherwise, try to remove it with a gentle, slightly bent, blind hook. If it is not removed with that, then make a copper tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal.
Seal the area around the tube with softened pitch mixed with oil, so that the air has no way out other than the tube, then pull it out with force.  This often removes it. If it is not removed by these methods, take a small piece of mastic gum or a type of gum used for catching birds, and place it on the end of the rod.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, then gently insert it into the ear canal after the ear is dry, if it is not removed by all of the methods described, then proceed to incision before the hot swelling or tightening occurs.
The method of incision is to first bleed the patient at the temples to a degree commensurate with their strength. Then, sit the patient before you, tilt their ear upward, and make a small incision at the base of the ear, near the cartilage, in the lowest part.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone. Then, remove it with whatever instruments you can.  Then, quickly suture the incision and treat it until healed.
If the object that fell into the ear is one of the kinds that proliferate and swell, try to remove it by the methods described.  If that does not work, take a thin, delicate forceps, like this.
And try to cut that type of fallen ear grain with it, only if you are certain that the grain has been moistened by ear vapor, so that it becomes many small pieces. Then remove it with a blunt hook, or with a soft cloth, or by suction.
As mentioned, it is then easy to remove it.  As for water entering the ear, the patient should first use a sneezing movement with a censer.  Having previously filled their ear with some old cotton, lying on the affected ear, if it comes out by this method.
Otherwise, take many small, smooth pebbles, warm them slightly by fire. The patient inserts one into the ear hole. The patient should then stand on one leg, on the affected side. Striking another stone against the stone in the ear.
He continues doing this with pebble after pebble until all the water comes out.  Water may also be removed by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and burning the other end until it is mostly consumed, then repeating with another feather.
Repeat this several times until all the water is removed, or draw it out with a tube, as previously described for the stones.  As for removing an animal that has entered, if it is small like a flea or similar, treat it according to what was mentioned in the classification.  As for...
If its body is large and visible to touch, try removing it with dry cloths and tweezers. This is easier than anything else that invades the ear. As for removing worms that have developed in the ear, if you treated it as mentioned in the classification in the chapter on ear problems and your treatment did not work.
Then you should look at the ear in the sun. If any worm is visible, remove it with dry cloths or fine tweezers. If nothing is visible, take a tube, which is depicted as follows...
Narrow at the bottom and wide at the top.  Insert the thin end into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly with it. Repeat this several times until all the worms are removed. If it does not come out, seal around the tube with wax, as I have mentioned for the stones.
If it does not come out with the methods mentioned, use the ear drops that I have described.  The ancients used them to kill worms.  You will find this in the chapter on ear drops.  You should use the instrument to apply ointments and medications to the ear.  This is its image/illustration:.
As you see, it is made of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, and wide at the top.  If you wish, the tube within the instrument can be made of tight copper.  Or, if you wish, you can take a piece of string and tightly wrap cotton around one end.
Then you put the ointment, or juice, or whatever medication you want, into the tube that is in the ear.  Then, insert the string with the cotton from above and gently squeeze with your hand until the ointment is driven into the ear cavity and the patient feels it.
Have the patient inside [the instrument] and make sure that what is being dripped into the ear has been warmed slightly in the fire.  Be careful that the thing being dripped in is not too cold or too hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
In treating ear blockages.
Some children may be born from their mothers' wombs with their ear openings unpierced.
Some people may also experience a blockage in the ear canal due to a wound or a growing piece of flesh, and this blockage might be deep within the ear opening, undetectable by sight.
It may also protrude slightly from the opening, and be visible; the one that occurs deep in the ear and is not visible is, in most cases, harder to cure; and the one that is visible, one should place the patient's ear in the sun. And look at it; if you see the blockage outwardly, then open it with a gentle scalpel, and this would be its image:.
Its tip should have a small, limited protrusion, and the rest of the scalpel should be smooth on both sides, so as not to harm the ear. If the blockage is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a gentle snare and cut it off with extreme gentleness until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, take a smooth, gentle probe, warm it slightly in the fire, and then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the blockage, along with the patient's experience of hearing impairment, then gently remove it.
Be careful not to injure the ear drum. Then, place a wick in the ear, sized to fit the opening, and coat it with Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are sure the wound is healed, or take a wick, soak it in water, and sprinkle powdered alum on it.
Use it. If you see that the ear has become inflamed with a hot swelling, then you should remove the wick and replace it with another wick coated with a preparation made from rose oil, so as to reduce the inflammation. Then treat it until it is healed.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear. Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the section on haemostasis.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be wet or dry.  It is necessary to grasp them with a forceps or a needle, and cut them off at their roots with a scalpel.  Then, apply powdered alum to the affected area.
If blood flows from them, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-shaped cautery iron.  Cauterization after cutting is better.  It often returns if any part of the root remains.  If cauterized with fire, the roots are burned and it will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of cold affecting the eyelids.
A condition resembling a cold in its severity and hardness often appears on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called a cold, which is the accumulation of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids.  If the cold is protruding on the surface of the eyelid and moves in every direction, the treatment is simple: cut it across.
Then gently peel it away from every side until it is removed, then hang it up with a hook and cut it. If you cannot cut it without cutting through the eyelid, that will not harm the patient. If the cut is large, stitch it together and treat it until healed.
If it is small, there is no harm. The ointment will mend and unite it. If the cold is inclined towards the inner surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid inside out, and hang the cold with a hook, without needing to cut, and trim it from every side.
If it cuts through the eyelid, that will not harm anything. Then wash the eye with salt water after cutting the cold, and treat the area with something that will heal it until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
On treating the stye that appears on the eyelid.
A stye is a swelling in the layers of the upper eyelid.  It appears most often in children.  It weighs down their eyes, causes them colds in the early morning hours, and they cannot look at sunlight. So that the tears come quickly to them.
Therefore, you see them leaning on their faces, and sleeping on them.  Their eyelids beneath the eyebrows are wet, possibly swollen.  The stye is evident.  If you press the area with your fingers, you feel the stye between them.  The method is for the patient to place their head on your lap.
Then take a linen cloth, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick, large enough to surround the stye on all sides.  Then place it over the stye and press with your fingers on all sides to gather the stye in the center of the circle.
Then, with a lancet, make a transverse incision through the center of that moist area, not larger than an incision made in phlebotomy.  As for depth, it is necessary to cut through the entire skin until you reach the stye.
In most cases, the stye protrudes from the incision as a white fatty piece.  Pull it outward with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, turning your hand to the right and left, until it heals.  Be careful not to enlarge the incision, lest the lancet injure the eye.
If the stye does not appear at the initial incision, you should gently enlarge the incision slightly until the stye protrudes.  Then, pull it out as described.  Then, dip a cloth in vinegar and water, apply it to the area, and bandage it firmly. Some people crush salt.
Place it in the incision to dissolve any remaining moisture. Then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs at the site, treat it with cooling compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the types of eye dressings.
If superfluous eyelashes grow on the eyelid, deviating from the natural course beneath the natural eyelashes and becoming thick, they harm the eye and cause various ailments such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, white discoloration, and thickening, thus causing blindness.
And the treatment of the eye has four aspects: either by cauterization with fire, or by a strong medicine, as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or by cutting and stitching, or by cauterization with a stick, as I have described.
You should place the patient's head on your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, then insert a needle with thread underneath the eyelid, and pierce the needle and thread through from above, close to the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards through the eyelid.
Then turn it with a spatula, and make a cut in the inside of the eyelid, excluding the excess hair, with a sharp scalpel, from the larger to the smaller incision.  Then remove the thread, and place a small pad of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and stitch the eyelid with thread.
Like the shape of an anise leaf, but the shape should be as much as is needed to lift the eyelid, because this may vary from person to person. Some people may require a certain amount of the eyelid to be cut to match the amount of eyelid relaxation.
Some people need a smaller cut.  All this depends on the relaxation of the eyelid.  Then, using the scalpel, make incisions along the lines you marked, starting from the larger incision towards the smaller one.  Each incision should be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a spatula.
Then, insert a hook into one corner of the skin and peel the entire skin away.  Then, using a needle and thin wool thread, stitch the two edges together.  Wipe away the blood, and attach any excess threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
You may wish to do this, or not bother.  Then, leave the stitches and threads for about three or four days, and then treat it.  If you wish, you may leave the wound unstitched and treat it with something to dry and tighten it.  The eyelid will lift when the wound is closed.  Stitching is better.
This method of eyelid lifting was mentioned by the ancients, but it involves hardship for the patient.  It is a good technique with no risk.  Another method of eyelid lifting is to shape the eyelid like an anise leaf, as described. Then, lift the eyelid using three hooks, which may be separate or grouped in this configuration.
Then, cut away any excess eyelid with a small pair of scissors, in this manner, with precision.
If you cannot hold the hooks in place or if it is not working, take a needle with thread, insert it into the center of the shape, insert another thread near the larger incision, and a third near the smaller incision.  Hold the threads with your fingers, evenly, then lift your hand with a steady motion.
Lift all the marked skin as described, then stitch together the edges of the wound and treat it until healed. If a hot swelling appears after cutting or incision, soothe the swelling with qīrūtī or similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also have its edges turn inward; in this case, the described cuts, stitches, cauterization with fire and strong medicine, and cauterization with fire or strong medicine are to be applied.  Cauterization with fire or strong medicine is more gentle on the patient than cutting and stitching.  The latter is a more difficult procedure.
Cauterization with reeds is done in this way:  Turn the eyelid, make the incision described from the inside, then prepare two thin reeds or sticks, the length of the eyelid and a width smaller than that of a scalpel.
Nibble the ends of each, on both sides, where the threads will be held, then gently bring them together over the remaining part of the eyelid and tightly bind the reeds on both sides. Leave it for several days, as the tightened skin will die, turn black, and putrefy.
Until it falls off on its own. If it takes too long, trim it with nippers, then treat it until healed. When it is healed, the eyelid will rise and the eyelashes will not irritate the eye.  This is how the reeds are made: this shape and exact length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
Removing irritating hairs from the eye with a needle.
If there is a hair or two, the procedure is to take a thin needle, thread it with a smooth, thin silk thread, tie its ends in a very delicate knot, and let the thread's length be about a span.
Then, thread another thin, shorter thread into the needle.  Tie its ends, if desired. Have the patient rest their head on your lap, near the light, so the work is clear.  It is a delicate operation.  Then, using the needle and thread, insert the needle into the root of the superfluous hair.
And push it through the eyelid quickly until you remove it above the natural hair. Then pull the thread upward, through the needle, and the hair—if it's one, two, or three—is removed from the needle.
Then, pull your hand, so that the hair comes out with the needle in the eyelid with the natural hair.  If pulling the thread does not remove the hair, pull the thread downward, using the thread that was previously threaded into the needle, until the needle comes out the top hole.
Then, return the hair to the needle and pull them out. Repeat this until the hair is elevated at the upper hole, visible to the eye. Then, remove the needle and thread, and gently press down on the eye. Leave it pressed for two or three days until the hair is firmly in place, and the surrounding flesh has healed.
If the hair is short, either add a longer hair from the natural hair, so it rises with it, or let it grow longer over time, and then remove it.
Chapter Thirteen.
On treating the stye that occurs in the upper eyelid of the eye.
This stye, which is called an arnbie, can be either natural or accidental.  Accidental styes result from a wound, a cut, a burn, or the like.
The treatment involves incising the abscess, separating its edges, and placing a linen thread between them, tying it off until it heals.  Things that dry and constrict should not be used in its treatment.
If you do otherwise, the stye will return more severely.  Instead, use things that loosen, such as fenugreek, poultices made with water in which a ram's horn has been boiled, flaxseed, and diachylon ointment melted with some grease.  Apply this to the thread and treat with it.
The goal of the treatment is to prevent the adhesion from returning to its initial state by all means possible.  The method described is only part of the stye's repair and does not necessarily restore the area to its original condition.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of styes located in the lower eyelid.
This stye, arising from the lower part, is the true stye, and is either natural or accidental.  The accidental stye results from a wound, tear, burn, or similar injury.
The method involves taking a needle with a double thread, inserting it into the flesh, and piercing from the inner to the outer eyelid until the thread is on both sides of the flesh.  Then, pull the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, then stop. Otherwise, take a spatula, place it over the wound, and turn the eyelid.  Make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, with the ends of the incisions meeting at the angle of the cut, forming an angle. So that when they meet, the shape will be similar to this shape:
Then, remove the flesh to the extent that the sharp edge is below the eyelid.  Then, bring together the separated parts with two stitches using a thread of wool.  Then treat it with the relaxing and twisting medications we have mentioned until it heals.
If the ulcer has occurred from a cut, stitch, or cauterization, it is necessary to make a small incision, also without affecting the eyelashes, as previously described. Then, separate the lips with twisting, as we have mentioned.
In summary, treating ulcers, whether above or below, requires adapting the procedure based on the ulcer's specific appearance, as its presentation can vary significantly.  The skilled practitioner should employ the most appropriate method possible.
Until the shape returns to its natural state, or approximates it. The disease itself will often indicate the appropriate treatment and tools.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion can affect many people, causing the upper eyelid to stick to the white of the eye, hindering its free movement and natural functions.  Therefore, a slender instrument should be inserted beneath the eyelid.
Then lift it upwards, or stretch it with forceps, and cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not as sharp as surgical blades, but rather slightly blunt, so as not to disturb the patient during the procedure and risk the scalpel slipping and injuring the eye with its sharpness.  Cut as if you are peeling off the adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its normal position and the entire adhesion heals. Then, irrigate the eye with saline water, or instill a solution of zinjari (a type of astringent) in water into the eye.  Then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen thread.
Place wet cotton wool soaked in egg white over the eye. After three days, use poultices until healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
In cutting the nail and deep flesh growths.
The nail comes in two types: either nervous, resembling a tough, thin membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a moist, white, solid substance that shatters if touched by iron or attempted to be grasped with a hook.
No hook can hold onto either type. Both types begin from the largest part, gradually spreading until they cover the eye and block light and eye movement.  The procedure for cutting them involves the patient placing their head on your lap.
Then, the patient opens their eyes and you lift the eyelid with your hand. Next, grasp the nail with a slightly curved hook, pulling it upwards. Then, take a needle, thread a horse or cow hair and a strong thread through it, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and pierce the nail's center.
Pierce it with the needle, tie the nail to the thread, pull it upward, and peel the nail's side towards the pupil with the hair, as if spreading it with the hair to its end. Then, cut the remaining part at the root of the growth with a gentle scalpel or small scissors, leaving the natural flesh of the growth.
So that cutting it does not cause constant tearing. The nail may be pulled with the hook alone, or with the thread, as we said, then the smooth-ended scalpel is used to peel the portion that resembles this image.
Care must be taken that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, causing a tear in the pupil. After cutting, a pinch of powdered salt or some zinc oxide is placed in the eye. This is left for another day. Then, it is treated appropriately until it heals.
If the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle or secure a hook, this type of nail is often treated with medications, healing as described in the classification. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a gentle, smooth, blunt scalpel, like this image. Gently scrape the nail from above using the scalpel, if you see it crumbling and breaking apart.  If only the scraping is effective, place some zinc oxide, red oxide, or powdered salt into the eye from your container.  Press the eye closed for another day.
Then, continue this procedure until all of it is gone. However, if a hot tumor appears in the eye, leave it and treat the tumor until it heals, then resume the scraping until it heals.  And as for the protruding flesh of the cornea...
If the protruding flesh is causing severe injury to the eye, hook it with a forceps, and cut away a portion; do not cut deeply, lest tears begin to flow. Then, place red or zinc oxide into the affected area until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
Cutting superfluous flesh growth in the eye.
In some people's eyes, red, layered flesh may grow, covering the pupil or nearly so, or overflowing onto the eyelids; the eyelids might even turn outward, resembling a rose.
The treatment is to have the patient place their head on your lap, then open their eyes, and using the appropriate forceps, remove all the red flesh.
Or hold it with a lancet or a forceps, then cut the flesh piece by piece until all of it is removed. Protect the eye so as not to injure it during the procedure. You may cut it with the scalpel described in the chapter on trimming the fingernail or with small scissors. As with the forceps described in the next chapter.
Once the removal is complete and all the rose-like growth is gone, fill the eye with powdered salt or instill a corrosive red drug and apply a white-of-egg compress externally to prevent a hot swelling.
If any rose-like growth remains, and you are overwhelmed by bleeding and fear a hot swelling, leave the eye alone and treat it with something to quell the swelling, then return to the procedure until it heals.  Do the same for any excess flesh of this kind that appears in the eye.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye by nature or by appearance with the iron instrument. Do not insert your hand into any of these procedures until you ascertain that the organ can tolerate the procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
In removing obstructions from the eye.
Obstructions are red vessels woven over the eye, hindering its function and weakening it over time.  First, you must observe; if the eye containing the obstructions is strong and has no other ailment besides the obstructions...
Then, the extraction, which is to instruct the patient to place their head on your lap, and then remove those vessels with a single or double hook, according to your skill. The hooks should be delicately curved in this manner:
Or two hooks joined together as one, in this manner:
Then, delicately remove those vessels with a fine pair of scissors, wiping away the blood periodically until you see the eye is free of those vessels and the blood has drained.  Protect the eye and do not injure it with the tips of the scissors.  Work in the mid-day sun.
Be very diligent in your work, so as not to cut anything other than those vessels.  Once finished, instill green or red eye drops to consume any remaining obstructions.  If you cannot remove all of them at once, bandage the eye to relieve any pain.
Leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the inflammation cools down.  Then repeat the procedure using the same method, until the eye is healed.  This is the shape of the needed scissors.
Leave it [and let the head of the patient rest in the student's lap, and another student wipe away tears and examine the front and back of the eye.  You] The essential procedure is to open the eye using fixed or triangular-shaped instruments held by a student.
Keep a close watch, with the patient's head resting in the student's lap, and another student wiping away tears and examining the front and back of the eye.  You have the choice to begin removing the vessels from the top or bottom of the eye.  Preferably, dry two forceps, one at the end of the eyebrow, the other at the beginning of it.
Hold the forceps between the middle and index finger, then hang the forceps from below, opposite them.  Then hold them between the ring and pinky finger, and gently pull your hand back so that you see all the vessels have risen.  If desired, cut them with small scissors, or a gentle scalpel.
Insert the needle into the vessel and trace the entire vessel until it rises above the conjunctiva.  Do not cut the vessel; if tears and blood flow (liquefy) upon you.
Then, cut with the scissors any excess from above and below the eyebrow so that only the corneal portion remains unstripped. If you can, lift it off the cornea using the needle; this is better, and it may be possible in some people.
If you cut it, a scab will form in the area, which will settle the pain and prevent swelling. Then repeat the procedure.
Chapter Nineteen.
In returning the quill to the nose.
Doctors call the quill a nassour. If, after treating it with cauterization or a strong, burning medicine as previously described, it does not heal, the only recourse is to lance the swelling when it is ripe and remove all the moisture or pus from it.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, scrape it with an instrument of this form:
The instrument is called the "rough head" and is made of Indian iron. Its head is round like a button, engraved with fine carvings like a whetstone or a scraper.  Place it on the affected area of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers while gently squeezing your hand until you ascertain that the corruption has been removed.
Do this several times, then apply binding, dried medicines to the area.  If the area is sealed, flesh grows, the discharge stops, and forty days pass without recurrence, or discharge, or any further incident.
Then know that it has healed.  Otherwise, there is no other recourse than to return the nassour to the nasal opening in this way: expose the bone again with iron or a sharp medicine.  When the bone is exposed, take a probe of this form:
The tip of the iron is triangular, and its shaft is a tapered cone, narrowing towards the tip, as you see. Place it on the bone itself, near the nasal opening, with your hand slightly away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
By finding air exiting from it when the patient holds their hand on their nose.  Then, bind the area with the astringent, dried medicines, as I have taught you.  If the flesh grows, and the area hardens, the material that was protruding outward will return to the nose; this will be less difficult for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On returning the protruding eye.
If the entire eye protrudes, and no damage or loss occurs to the sight, the patient should be made comfortable, then held in a recumbent position on their back.  A cupping glass should be placed on the back of the neck, without any particular condition, and a gentle suction performed.
Then, apply a compress made of acacia, aloes, frankincense, and henna to the eye.  Then, the eye should be secured above the dressing with many bandages.  The bandages should be made by taking a series of soft strips of cloth, folding each into four layers, and creating many such strips.
Then, layer them one over the other as needed for the eye.  Secure them firmly with a strong bandage.  Return the cupping glass and apply suction again, without restrictions. Then, remove the cupping glass and keep the eye bandaged for one day and one night.
If the ligament relaxes during this time, it should be tightened again.  Then, remove the bandage after one day and one night, as we said.  If you see the eye has returned to its proper position, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the dressing, bandages, tightening, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-one.
On severing the tendons.
If a rupture occurs in the orbital layer, and the tendon protrudes outside the eyelids, appearing like a tendon, disfiguring the person's appearance, and you wish to sever it, proceed as I describe.
Insert a needle into the tendon's origin, from below upwards.  Then, insert another needle, with a doubled thread from the medial side, and pierce it. Leave the first needle as it is.
Then, cut the place where the doubled thread is bent, and tie some of the tendon around the needle and tighten it firmly.  Then, remove the needle and apply wool soaked in egg white to the eye. Leave it until the threads and tendon fall out. Then treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  The tendon may be tightened from all sides with a circular bandage until the eye becomes more prominent. Then, use a thin scalpel to reach the depths of the eye, causing the whitish moisture to flow out, and the eye to droop. Then tighten it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of the abscess.
This affliction, called an abscess, is a collection of matter in the eye, resembling descending water, but without it.  The method of treatment is that the patient sits upright on a chair, and then you take their head with your hands from both sides.
And you move it so that you see the matter move downwards with your eyes, then you hold it still so that it does not move.  The light will return, and the patient will see things as they used to see them.  If it does not descend downwards, we know it is water. If the matter does not descend as described.
Otherwise, have the patient sit before you, then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision above the corneal membrane, where the adhered material meets the corneal membrane in the limbus, until the matter is expelled.
Then, drop warm water into the eye, in which honey has been mixed, or water in which licorice and honey have been boiled.  Then treat with further remedies until it heals.
The twenty-third chapter.
We have previously described the types of water in a detailed and explained classification; consult that for the proper water to use for instillation.  At that time, the patient should sit before you, seated facing the light near the sun.
Tie and firmly hold the healthy eye, then raise the eyelid with your left hand if the affected eye is the left eye, or with your right hand if the affected eye is the right eye.
Then, place the tip of the cup close to the limbus, slightly pressing it against the white of the eye, nearer the smaller conjunctiva.  Then push the cup forcefully while rotating it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel the cup has reached an empty space.
The depth of the cup's insertion should be the same distance as from the pupil to the edge of the colored iris (limbus).  The copper cup, due to the clarity of the cornea, will be visible to the observer as though it were part of the eye. Then, raise the cup to the area where the fluid is located.
Then press it downward repeatedly. If the fluid descends immediately, the patient will see what has been opened to their sight by the cup, which is in their eye. Then rest for a while. If the fluid rises, lower it again without removing the cup.
If it settles and does not rise, remove the cup gently, twisting your hand slightly. Then dissolve a little of clear, fine salt in the fluid and wash the eye from the inside.  Then apply a moistened compress or bandage to the entire outside of the eye.
Apply rose oil and egg white and bandage the healthy eye.  As for us and the people of our time, we apply crushed cumin with egg white. If the cup does not enter the eye due to its hardness, for some people have extremely hard eyes. One should take the lancet called the *al-bārid*, which has this form:
Pierce only the adhered part with it, do not delve into the piercing.  Instead, gently insinuate the cup, then insert the cup as previously described. Once your work is complete, prepare a secure and dark bedroom for the patient to lie on their back.
He must refrain from all movement and coughing.  His food should be soft and easily digested, and he must not move his head at all, right or left.  The bandage remains in place for three days.  Then, in that same darkened room, his vision is tested.
Show him objects, then remove the bandage on the seventh day.  One should not do this during the treatment or immediately after the cupping, but rather before the water rises rapidly due to intense gazing.
If a hot swelling appears, the eye should be unbound before the seventh day, and treated to soothe the swelling.  Once the swelling subsides, then release the vision and place a veil over the face, allowing his sight to adjust gradually from beneath the veil for several days while he remains in the dark room.  Then...
He is gradually released from the room and allowed to manage his affairs.  Know that the student of cupping cannot dispense with repeated observation before undertaking the procedure.  I have heard that some Iraqis claim to make a hollow cup in Iraq that draws out water.
I have seen no one in our country perform this, nor have I read about it in any of the earlier books.  It may be a recent innovation.  Here are illustrations of various types of cupping instruments, for their clarity.
They are made of copper, particularly, and their tips are precisely this shape: triangular, sharply pointed.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
For the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
Various growths may develop in the nose, some resembling scorpions with many legs, others cancerous, hard, and dark-colored, and still others soft and not dark-colored.
If these growths are soft, not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun, their nostril opened, and a hook inserted into the growths, pulling them out.  Then, using a sharp, gentle scalpel, cut what is caught from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh is gone. If any remains that you cannot cut, then remove it gently with one of the softer instruments, so that nothing of it remains.  If blood or a hot swelling prevails.
If it is a malignant tumor, then promptly cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone. Then, after the cutting, apply vinegar and water or a drink to the nose, so that it opens and moisture flows from it to the throat.
Know that it has healed. If the moisture does not drain properly, know that there is a growing flesh within the porous bones that the cutting instrument did not reach.  In that case, you should take a thick linen thread.
Tie many knots in it, placing each knot the size of a finger or less.  The patient maneuvers one end of the thread into their nose with a probe or anything suitable, shaped like a button, and pulls the thread so that it reaches the nostril.
It emerges at the throat.  Children often do this in school, and it's easy for whoever desires to do it.  Then, gather the two ends of the thread—one that came out of the mouth and the other that remains in the nose.
Then, use the knots in the thread to dissect the flesh, doing this until you know that the flesh has been separated by the knots of the thread. Then remove the thread, and once the blood has been cleaned from the nose, place a thread soaked in Egyptian ointment into the nose.  Do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes any remaining flesh.  Finally, a lead tube is placed in the nose for a number of days until it heals. If further drying treatment is needed, use that.
This is an image of the *mas'at* used to instill ointments and medicines into the nose.
It is made of silver or bronze, resembling a small, open lamp, with a channel as well.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the applicator's ointment container flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when heating ointment or any other liquid substances within it.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow on the tip of the nose, increasing in size and number over time, until their appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance and remove it entirely. Then, either cauterize the area or apply a burning medication in place of cauterization. If you delay cutting it until it grows large, observe it; if it is hardened, solid, dark-colored, and insensitive, ...
Do not use iron on it, for it is a cancerous tumor.  I have often seen that cutting this tumor brought about a great calamity for the sufferer.  If the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark-colored, and you see that it can be cut entirely, ... Then drain the pus, and cut it without hesitation or delay. Treat the area with something that dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear. If its connection is separated by a wound or something similar, know that if a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, rarely will treatment be successful, except in some people.
Therefore, when someone presents with such a condition, examine if the wound is fresh and bloody. If so, bring the edges of the wound together by stitching and then treat it until it heals.  If the separation of the connection has separated its edges and each gap is sound, then it is necessary to dissect each gap with the thickness of the visible skin until it bleeds.
Then, bring the edges together with stitches, tighten them, and sprinkle powdered sumac and frankincense on them. Place a plaster of Palestinian ointment, or another of the uniting ointments, on top of the powder. Leave it tightened for two or three days, then remove it.
Then change the medication and leave it until the threads detach on their own.  Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to bring together the separation of the connection, either with needles as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread as I explained there.
Chapter Twenty-seven.
On removing the knots that appear on the lips.
Many people may have small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling carbuncles, others smaller or larger.  Therefore, you should turn the lip inside out, cut every knot, and remove them with forceps.
Cut it from every side, then fill the wound with powdered alum after cutting to stop the bleeding.  Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas with something astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
On cutting excess flesh in the gums.
Excess flesh often grows on the gums, which the ancients called "apuls".  It should be grasped with forceps or a curette, cut at its base, and allowed to bleed.  Then, powdered alum should be placed on the area.
Or one of the astringent powders, dried. If that flesh returns after treatment, it often does. Cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-nine.
On cleaning teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly scales may accumulate on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums.  These may turn black, yellow, or green, leading to gum disease and an unsightly appearance of the teeth.
The patient should be seated before you, head resting on your lap.  Clean the tooth or teeth exhibiting scales and sand-like material until none remains.  Do the same with black, green, or yellow discoloration, and any other blemishes, until clean.
If the initial cleaning removes the impurities, well and good.  Otherwise, repeat the cleaning process on another day, and again on a third day, until the desired result is achieved.  Know that teeth require various instruments of differing shapes and forms, according to the task at hand.
For the instrument used to clean the inside of a tooth is different from the one used for the outside, and the one used between the teeth has a different shape.  You should have a variety of these instruments prepared.
Chapter Thirty.
On the extraction of teeth.
One should treat the tooth's pain by every possible means, and delay extraction.  For once extracted, there is no return, as it is a valuable substance, even if extraction is unavoidable.
Therefore, if the patient is determined on extraction, you must first make sure the tooth is definitely painful. Often the patient deceives himself about the pain, thinking it is in a healthy tooth and extracting it, yet the pain does not subside until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have witnessed this frequently in the actions of the barbers.
If you are certain the pain is in the tooth itself, then you should use a strong scalpel to cut around the tooth, separating the gum on all sides.  Then, move it gently, little by little, with your fingers or fine forceps, to loosen it.
Then, with the large forceps, firmly grasp the tooth, holding the patient's head between your knees to prevent movement.  Pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it.  If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and gently insert it under the tooth from all sides, moving it as you did previously.
If the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill the hole with a cloth and seal it firmly with a thin, smooth instrument so that it does not break when you apply the forceps.  You should carefully cut around the gum on all sides, thoroughly.
Take care to avoid breaking it, for a piece left behind will become a greater affliction for the patient than the initial pain.  Beware of what the ignorant barbers do, rushing to extract it without using the methods we have described.
Often they inflict great suffering on people, the least of which is breaking the tooth and leaving the roots, either all or some of them. Or, they might even pull out some of the jawbone, as we have observed repeatedly.  After extraction, the patient should rinse the area with liquid, vinegar and salt.  If bleeding occurs, which is frequent, then powder some alum and apply it to the area.
Otherwise, if alum does not help, use the forceps.  The design of the delicate forceps used to move the tooth first:  long handles, short grip, and thick to prevent bending when gripping the tooth.  This is an image of the large forceps.
The handles should be thick, so that when grasped, they do not yield or bend. The tips should be short.  The forceps should be made of Indian iron or tempered steel, with secure, fitted tips, and teeth that interlock for a firm and tight grip.
The tips can also be shaped like a file, and thus will be strong and precise.

Short, slightly thick, and not pointed, lest it breaks. If the root is removed by this, otherwise, use these other instruments, which are pictured. The first one is triangular and somewhat thick.
Another gentle triangular image.
We may also use this two-pronged instrument, of which this is also a picture.
And with other instruments and tools, as mentioned in the description of teeth extraction. We may also use this instrument, resembling a large fish hook, which this is a picture of.
Triangular, bent, slightly thick at the tip so it does not break; and it should not be pointed. Know that tooth extraction tools are numerous, and so are other tools; almost innumerable. A skilled artisan, with his craft, may invent tools, tailored to the work and the diseases themselves.
Because some diseases were not described by the ancients with instruments due to their diverse types, if a bone in the jaw or in one of the mouth's bones breaks or decays, investigate the area using the appropriate tools and tweezers as described for removing roots.
And we use a forceps, this is its image.
