The Thirty-second Chapter.
In the eruption of teeth that grow over others.
Teeth, if they erupt outside their natural course, disfigure the appearance, especially in women and slaves.  If a tooth has erupted behind another, it is necessary to consider this.
If it cannot be moved forward or backward, extract it.  If it is attached to another tooth, cut it off using this instrument, whose image is this:
It resembles a small beak, made of sharp Indian iron.  Cut it out over several days, due to the tooth's hardness, so as not to dislodge other teeth. If it is protruding and firmly in place, cool it down with an Indian file, whose image is this.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, very finely engraved, like the file used to make needles.  Cool the tooth gradually over many days, gently, so as not to dislodge it and cause it to fall out.  Then, smooth it finally and scrape it with some scrapers.
If a tooth has broken and is irritating the tongue while speaking, it should also be cooled down until the roughness of the fracture is gone, it becomes smooth, and does not irritate the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In fastening loose teeth with silver or gold thread.
If older teeth become loose and shift due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot eat because they fear the teeth will fall out, and the astringent remedies were ineffective.
The remedy is to fasten them with gold or silver thread, gold being preferable, as silver tarnishes and rots after a few days, whereas gold remains the same indefinitely. The thread should be of medium thickness and fineness, sufficient to fit between the teeth.
The method of fastening is to take the thread, insert its fold between the sound teeth, and then weave the ends of the thread through the loose teeth, one or more, until it reaches the sound tooth on the opposite side.
Then, weave back to the starting point, tightening gently and skillfully so that the teeth do not move.  Tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth to prevent slippage.  Cut the excess thread ends with scissors, gather them, twist them together with a file, and hide them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
So that the tongue is not harmed.  Then leave it tightened like this as long as it remains. If it loosens or breaks, tighten it again with another thread, and it will be usable forever.  This is the image of the teeth and method of fastening in two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
A single or two teeth may be returned to their place after falling out, fastened as described, and remain.  Only a skilled craftsman can do this.  He may also carve bone from an ox, shape it like a tooth, and place it in the space where the tooth has been lost. And it is tightened, as we said, and it remains usable.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On severing the ligament that lies under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament lying under the tongue may be either natural, present from birth, or accidental, resulting from a healed wound.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth, place their head in your lap, and lift their tongue.  Then, cut the ligamentous nerve transversely so the tongue is released. If there is some stiffness or entanglement, and it is from a healed wound, use a forceps to slice it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot is loosened.
Be careful not to cut too deep into the flesh, lest you sever an artery and cause bleeding. Then, after the cut, rinse the area with rosewater or vinegar and cool water. Afterwards, place a linen wick under the tongue for the patient to hold each night.
To prevent re-adhesion.  If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the area.  If the bleeding is excessive, cauterize the area with a suitable lens-shaped cautery iron. Then treat him with other remedies until he is healed.
Chapter Thirty-five.
Removing a toad that has developed under the tongue.
A tumor resembling a small toad may develop under the tongue, hindering its normal function, and possibly growing to fill the mouth.  The procedure is to open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the tumor.
If you see it dark-colored or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation, do not attempt to treat it, for it is cancer.  If it leans towards white and has moisture, use the forceps and carefully dissect it with a scalpel, freeing it from all sides.
If bleeding occurs during the procedure, apply powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then resume the procedure until the tumor is completely removed.  The patient should then gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with further appropriate therapy until healed.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the treatment of tonsil tumors.
Any tumors growing in the throat may present glands resembling those appearing externally, called tonsils. If you treat them with the methods mentioned in the classification, and they do not heal,
If the tumor is hard, dark-colored, and insensitive, do not use the iron.  If it is red and thick-based, also do not use the iron, for fear of bleeding. Instead, allow it to mature, either by softening or spontaneous rupture.
If it is white, round, and thin-based, this is what should be cut. Before proceeding, observe if the inflammatory swelling has completely subsided or if some of the swelling has reduced.
Then, have the patient sit facing the sun, head resting on your lap, mouth open.  Have an assistant hold the tongue down with an instrument; this is its image:
It is made of silver or bronze, thin like a knife.  Once the tongue is held down, and you can clearly see the tumor, use a hook and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it outwards as much as possible, without pulling any of the membranes along with it.
Then, cut it with an instrument resembling scissors, but with both ends curved, each point very sharp.  It is made of Indian iron or Damascene steel.
If this instrument is unavailable, then cut it with a scalpel, this is its image:
Sharp on one side, blunt on the other.  After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner.  Then, after cutting, have the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, have him gargle with water boiled with pomegranate rind, or rue leaves, or similar astringents, until the bleeding stops.  Then treat it until healed.  Other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils; cut these in the same way as the tonsils.
I treated a woman with a tumor that had grown inside her throat, approaching the size of a small lump, nearly obstructing the throat.  The woman was breathing through a narrow passageway.  It had prevented her from eating and drinking.
She was close to death if she remained for a day or two.  The tumor had grown two branches that extended to her nostrils.  Acting quickly, I inserted a hook into one of them, and pulled out a suitable piece.
Then I cut it where it emerged from the nostril, and did the same with the other nostril's protrusion.  Then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, and inserted the hook into the tumor itself.  I cut off some of it, and only a small amount of blood was released.
The woman's throat opened up, and she immediately drank water and then ate food.  We continued to cut away at the tumor repeatedly for a long time, and the tumor would regenerate parts that were cut away.  This continued for a long time for both of us.
I cauterized the tumor within her throat, hoping to stop its growth.  Then I left the area, and I do not know what God did to her afterwards.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
In cutting a tumor of the larynx.
If a tumor, called *'unba'*, descends to the larynx and swells, and is white and oblong, it is called a *'amood'*.  If it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called an *'unba'*.  If treatment as described in the classification does not work.
If the hot tumor has subsided and is thin, it should be cut.  If it is a solid, round mass without length, and is blood-red, dark-colored, black, or without sensation, it should be avoided.
In this case, there is risk to the patient. Therefore, if you see the tumor with the characteristics mentioned - whiteness and length - the patient should be seated facing the sun, and the tongue should be pressed with the instrument described earlier. Then, the snare should be inserted into the *'unba'*.
Pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments mentioned in the section on cutting the tonsils.  Only the excess beyond the natural amount should be cut, as cutting more will harm the voice and speech.
After the cutting, use the methods described for cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is hesitant about having it cut, use the cautery method without fear or caution. The cautery should be with a sharp medicine, and the patient's head should be placed on your lap.
Then, press the patient's tongue with the instrument mentioned before.  Take the sharp water mentioned in the section on cauterization, mix it with un-extinguished gypsum, making a consistency that is neither thick nor thin. Fill the indentation of the instrument with this mixture.  This is its form/image:
The tip where you place the medicine has a concavity like the concavity of a mrod spoon.  Place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself.  The patient should be lying on their side, so saliva can drain from the mouth, from inside the throat, to prevent anything from going back into the throat and hurting them.
Then, hold the instrument with the medicine and apply pressure to the uvula for about half an hour until you see it has blackened and the burning from the medicine has subsided. If you prefer, take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the tip of a mrod, and moisten the cotton with the medicine.
Insert the mrod with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. Repeat this several times until you achieve the desired level of cauterization of the uvula.  Then leave it; it will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to repeat the application of the medicine, repeat it.
After the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in clarified butter, removing any remaining medicine. Then, the patient should rinse with cold water, and be treated externally with lotions and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and less frightening.
The uvula can also be treated with something gentler than cauterization or cutting, using incense in this manner:  Take frankincense, myrrh, sage, wormwood, dittany, chamomile, and quassia, or similar herbs.  Gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
Bring it to a boil, and the pot should be sealed with clay.  A small hole is made in the lid, into which the hollow instrument is fitted.  This instrument is made of silver or brass.
Insert the end with the pomegranate into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the uvula through the tube, causing the uvula to shrink.  Repeat this process several times until it withers.  Avoid using this treatment at the very beginning of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling.  Instead, it is necessary to do this when the hot swelling has subsided.  If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit an egg shell to the end of it, so as not to burn the patient's mouth.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth.  This is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
For removing thorns and similar things that lodge in the throat.
Often a bone or fish thorn, or something similar, lodges in the throat.  It is necessary to remove what is visible to the eye, after pressing the tongue with the instrument in the light.
To make clear what is in the throat.  If something is not visible and hidden in the throat, it is necessary to induce vomiting before the patient's food digests in the stomach.  Perhaps the lodged object will come out with the vomit, or the patient will swallow a piece of linen or a stalk of lettuce.
Or the patient may swallow a piece of dry bread, or take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a thread, and swallow it. When it reaches the thorn, pull the thread quickly. Repeat this several times.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and come out. If it does not come out with these methods, use a lead instrument like this:
It is slightly thicker than a stylus.  At its tip is a bend.  The patient, raising their head, gently inserts it into their throat, taking care not to touch their larynx to avoid coughing. This will push out the bone or thorn.  Or the physician may insert it with their hand.
The patient's insertion is better, as they know the location of the lodged object.  They push it downwards or pull their hand with the instrument upwards, all according to what they are able to do until it is removed.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
Removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If the leech, treated with the remedies described in the section on medications, proves ineffective, then examine the patient's throat in the light of the sun, after pressing their tongue with the instrument described to you.
If you spot the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a gentle, firm forceps.  If that's not possible, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech.  Then insert a heated iron into the tube's interior.
Repeat this several times. The patient should refrain from drinking water all day.  Then, he should take a bowl filled with cool water, open his mouth over it, rinse his mouth, and not swallow any of it. He should move the water with his hand from time to time.
If the leech feels the water, it will fall off. If it doesn't come out with the methods described, fumigate the throat with urine and with *ḥalītiyy* using the instrument described for inhaling vapors to the throat.  Repeat this several times, and it will fall off.
The procedure for fumigation is to take a container holding heated embers, the container covered with a lid having a hole in the middle. Place the end of the instrument in the hole, then place the incense in the container. The patient puts their mouth at the end of the tube and closes their mouth so the incense reaches the throat.
The leech will fall off at that point. If it does not, the fumigation is repeated several times.  The patient should endure thirst and eat salty foods and garlic, but drink no water.  This procedure will be successful in removing the leech.  This is the form of the instrument used to pull the leech from the throat once it is seen.
It resembles tweezers, as you see, but has this curvature that enters the throat.  Its tips resemble a bird's beak.  They are rough like a whetstone, and if they grasp something, they will not release it.
Another instrument is used to hold the tongue. It is a wide iron ring, sized to fit over the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth, standing upright on its side, keeping the mouth open against the patient's will. The patient cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument used to open the mouth in this exact manner.
Chapter Forty.
It will deal with the lancing and incision of tumors.
Tumors are of many diverse types, as will be described individually in this book.  They differ in their lancing and incision in two ways: one is from the nature of the tumor itself and the humors it contains.
The second type is according to the locations in the body where they occur, because a tumor arising in the head is different from one arising in the buttocks, and a tumor arising in fleshy tissue differs from one arising in a joint.  Each has its own treatment procedure.
Some tumors should not be lanced until the pus within them has matured and is complete, while others should be lanced while they are still raw and not fully matured, such as tumors that are close to joints.
Because if a tumor arises near a joint and grows to the point of causing the surrounding tissue to decay, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing dysfunction of that limb, or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
Because if you delay lancing it until it matures, you will harm the vital organ, or if it is near the buttocks, lance it while raw.  It is necessary to lance an immature, raw tumor near the buttocks to prevent the rot from spreading deep into the buttocks, becoming a fistula.
And it will become incurable in some cases.  You should know the time to lance tumors that have fully matured, which is when the tumor pain subsides, the fever is gone, the redness and throbbing decrease, the head of the tumor softens, and other similar symptoms.
The incision should be made at the lower part of the tumor, if possible, so that the discharge flows downwards more easily, or at the thinnest, most prominent part of the tumor.  If the tumors are on the hands and feet, the incision should run along the length of the limb.
And at the locations of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and generally in all flat, non-bending areas.  For areas that bend, the incision should follow the contours of that area.  If the tumor is in fleshy areas, it is best to avoid lancing it.
Until its maturation is complete, as we said. If you lance it before that, the discharge of pus will be prolonged, causing much harm and filth, and its lips and recesses might harden. Some tumors may be lanced across the limb, if necessary, or as the organ requires.
Small tumors should be lanced once; large tumors should be lanced widely or with many incisions, according to the size of the tumor.  Some tumors may require the skin to be scraped or cut, if it has become like a torn cloth.
Some have become somewhat dead, like what is seen in many bumps and cavities. Some are split into a three-sided shape, and some have a shape like a rue leaf, like a dropsy tumor.
Some require a circular or crescent-shaped incision, or similar cuts.  If the tumor has no head, like flat, smooth tumors, a simple incision should suffice. If the tumor is large, has accumulated for a long time, and you have lanced it, do not rush to drain all the pus at once.
Rather, drain some of it, then compress the tumor for another day, and drain some more pus.  Do this repeatedly, gradually, until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, a pregnant woman, a young child, or an elderly person, the animal spirit often dissolves with the sudden discharge of pus.  The patient may die unexpectedly without your knowledge. Therefore, take great care in this matter.
After lancing these tumors, you should cleanse the wound and examine it. If the tumor wound is small, or the incision is simple, use a bandage made of linen or old cotton. If the tumor is large and there are many incisions,
Then you should insert a thread into each incision until they meet. If the tumor has caused some skin to be detached or eroded, you should fill it with old cotton or linen lint, without moisture, and compress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after soaking a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding area repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the remedies and treatments, and the cauterization methods, that we have described in many places in this book and in the section on classifications. If it is winter, and the tumor area has many nerves, you should soak the bandages with warm water and oil.
And apply them to the area. If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply bandages soaked with water and oil, or with a cold drink and oil, until the third day, as we said.  Then you should loosen the bandage and cleanse the tumor.
And use treatments similar to these until it heals. This is what you need to know about treating tumors in general.  As for detailed treatment, each tumor's method of treatment has been mentioned in a summarized and categorized way.
Chapter Forty-one.
On the treatment of tumors appearing on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, which are a type of swelling enclosed in membranes.  They have characteristics like a hen's egg, and there are many types. Some are fatty, and some contain a moisture resembling mud.
Some contain a moisture like broth or soup, and so on.  Others are hard and stony.  All of them are harmless to lance and remove, unless a blood vessel is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for lancing them is to first probe them with the instrument whose image will follow, called the 'meds', so as to know what they contain. If they contain moisture, lance them lengthwise with a simple incision, like this image:.
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~c~. If the moisture has drained, dissect the sac containing the moisture and cut it all away completely, leaving nothing behind, as it often recurs if any is left.
Then, dip a cotton swab in the astringent ointment, if available, or else in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the next day, as it will consume any remaining sac material. Then, repeat the application of the cotton swab with the astringent, a second and third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that no part of the sac remains.  Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals.  If the swelling contains fatty material, lance it in this manner:
Lance it firmly, and insert forceps into the edges of the wound.  Dissect it from all sides and do your utmost to remove the peritoneum it contains. If an artery is encountered, do what we have described for it previously; and do the same for the swelling, if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as previously described. The incision on hardened swelling is easier because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these swellings in the head, in some people, have no moisture at all. This is because I incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman, and found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone.  It could not be broken, even if someone threw it, it would only chip.  The other swellings that occur on the heads of children are not like this.
At the roots of the ears, make a simple incision in all of them.  Make the opening from below so that the matter drains downward. Then treat it with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-Two.
Concerning the incision on swine-like swellings that frequently appear on the neck. These swellings often appear on the neck, under the armpits, and in the groins.  They may also appear elsewhere on the body.  A single swelling may appear on the neck, or many, and some may develop from others.
Each such swelling has its own enclosed membrane, as do tumors and swellings of the head, as we have described.  There are many types of these swellings, some stony, some containing moisture, and some malignant, not responding to treatment.
Among those I examined, some presented favorably to the touch.  Their appearance was close to the skin color; they moved in all directions and were not attached to the neck's nerves, veins, or arteries, nor were they deep-seated. Therefore, it should be incised simply, from top to bottom, along this line: from line ~J~ to line ~B~.
Peel it away from all sides, and stretch the skin flaps with a hook or two, or many hooks if necessary, as we described for tumors of the head. Remove it gradually, taking care not to cut a vein or nerve. The scalpel should not be overly sharp.
So that your hand does not inflict more cuts than necessary or distress the patient by cutting what does not need cutting.  If you do cut a vein or artery and that prevents you from continuing, apply powdered alum or some of the substances that cause clotting of the blood to the wound.
Close the wound and leave it until the inflammation subsides, the wound relaxes, and it is about to become infected.  The bleeding will cease then; return to your work until you are finished.  Then, check with your index finger if any small pieces remain, and remove them, removing them one by one.
If a large vein is located at the root of the piece of flesh, you should not cut that piece of flesh at its root.  Instead, tie it tightly with a doubled thread and leave it until it falls out on its own without causing harm. Then fill the wound with used cotton, soaked in Egyptian ointment, and treat it.
If you have cut the entire piece of flesh, you should bring together the edges of the wound and suture it immediately, after making sure that no remnants remain.  If you see that a portion of the piece of flesh is left over, note that.
You should aim for the root and base of the piece of flesh when cutting it, using the suturing and other methods that we mentioned.  For pieces of flesh containing moisture, apply a simple dressing where you see signs of ripeness, positioning it on the lower part, as we have said.
Then, after the dressing, apply ointment, like Egyptian ointment, to consume any remaining corruption.  Once you see the wound is clean, treat it with ointments that promote tissue growth until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Three.
In the incision of the throat for a tumor occurring inside the throat.
The ancients described this incision of the throat.  I have not seen anyone in our country perform it, and this follows their account.  However, sufferers of quinsy should avoid incision of the throat, as it will not benefit them.
In order that all tumors and the lung may be diseased, and for those with a burning tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if the cause is not in the trachea, incision of the throat is necessary to escape the harm caused by suffocation.
Therefore, if we wish to do this, we should incise the throat below three circles of the trachea, or four small incisions across between two circles, to the extent of the incision in the diaphragm, not in the cartilage.  This place is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it, if the surgeon is timid, he should extend the skin of the throat with a hook, then incise the skin until, when he reaches the trachea, he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then, he should incise the diaphragm that we described, and the incision of the trachea is evident from the phlegm which emerges from it with what is pierced and from the cessation of the voice.  He should leave the wound open for a while.  When the time in which suffocation was feared has passed, he should bring the edges of the wound together from the skin.
And he incises it alone, apart from the cartilage.  Then, the medicines that cause flesh to grow are used until it heals.  The author of this book says, the explanation of this passage that we have recounted is that if they see the patient's throat blocked by one of these tumors that have been mentioned.
When the patient is close to death, and is concerned that he will stop breathing, they proceed to incise the throat so that the patient may breathe through the wound, and be saved from death.  However, they are instructed to leave the wound open until the course of the disease is completed, and the course is typically three days or similar.
At that point, they instructed to sew up the wound and treat it until it heals.  What I personally witnessed was that a servant took a knife and inflicted a cut on her throat, severing some of the bronchus.  I was called to treat her, and I found her weakening as a slaughtered animal weakens.  I uncovered the wound.
I found that the blood coming from the wound was slight, so I was certain that she had not severed an artery or a vein.  Air was escaping from the wound.  I immediately sutured and treated the wound until it healed.  The servant experienced nothing more than a hoarseness in her voice.
And after a few days, she returned to her best state.  From this, we say that incision of the throat poses no danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
Concerning the incision on a tumor that appears on the larynx from the outside, and is called a laryngeal tumor.
This tumor, called a laryngeal tumor, is a large tumor the color of the body; it is common in women and is of two types.
Either it is natural or accidental; the natural one has no remedy.  The accidental one is of two kinds, one like fatty tumors, and the other like a tumor caused by arterial complication; incision poses a danger in the latter. Therefore, one should not use the knife at all, except for small ones.  If, probing and examining them with a probe, you find them similar to fatty tumors and not connected to any blood vessels, incise them as you would fatty tumors. Remove whatever is contained within the cyst if it is in one, otherwise, remove all of it, and then treat the affected area with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the incision of various tumors.
Tumors are of many types, and all their types have been mentioned in the classification.  It is necessary to explain here the difference between a tumor and an abscess, since it is a source of difficulty. I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and severe pain until the inflammation subsides. Pus develops, and then the fever and inflammation subside.  A tumor is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain. It is enclosed in a peritoneal sac, which is a specific covering for it. Its color is the same as the surrounding skin. It begins like a boil and becomes like a cyst, larger or smaller.
Tumors are of two types: either fatty, or containing moisture; and the varieties of moisture are numerous, as mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when treating a tumor, one must first probe and examine it with the instrument called the "midas," as its image will be shown in the following chapter.
Assess the size of the tumor, then remove the midas and observe what comes out afterward. If a liquid substance of any color emerges, make a small incision, as described for other tumors. If no moisture follows the midas, then know it is fatty.
Make a firm incision on it in this manner, as I have instructed you.  Suspend it with hooks, and carefully peel the skin from all sides.  If possible, preserve the sac intact, extracting the tumor along with it.
If the sac breaks during the procedure, and you are unable to remove the tumor intact, which frequently happens, remove it piece by piece, so that nothing remains. If any part remains, regardless of size, the tumor will likely return.
If you are unsuccessful and only a small part remains, cauterize the wound with some sharp, potent cauterizing agent, firmly close the wound, and apply something to alleviate the inflamed tumor, and treat it with other remedies until it heals. If the tumor is large, suture its edges and treat with remedies that promote healing.
If you encounter a bleeding vein, whether prominent or not, promptly apply powdered alum to the affected area, keep it pressed for two or three days until the wound becomes suppurated and the bleeding subsides, then return to removing any remaining tumor.

Chapter Forty-seven.
In the treatment of the male breast.
Some people's breasts may swell during puberty, becoming like women's breasts and remaining swollen and unsightly.  If one dislikes this, one should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast, as shown in this way.
From line ~B~ to line ~C~. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with the medicated plaster, then stitch the edges of the wound together and treat it until it heals. If the breast droops and relaxes due to its bone structure, as happens with women, treat it.
One should make two crescent-shaped incisions in its upper sides, each connecting with the other at their ends, so that the larger line encircles the smaller one, as shown in this manner. From line ~B~ to line ~C~, then the skin between the incisions is peeled away, the fat is removed, and the mentioned stitching, sutures, and necessary treatment are used until it heals.
If the incision does not encompass the necessary portion to be cut due to the patient's distress or bleeding, the wound should be packed with cotton soaked in caustic ointment, left to consume any remaining fat, and then treated until it heals.
The Forty-eighth Chapter.
On the treatment of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the swine-like type, solid and enclosed within a sac, some containing moisture.  Those containing moisture should be incised with a crescent-shaped cut, like this.

The forty-ninth chapter.
Incising the tumor that appears near the artery or vein, called an aneurysm.
If the artery is wounded and the skin above it adheres, a tumor often results.  Similarly, a vein may also develop a swelling and tumor.
The signs by which one can determine if the swelling is from an artery or a vein: if it is from an artery, the swelling is oblong, located deep in the body, and when you press it with your finger, you feel a crackling sensation.
And that which is from a vein, the swelling is round, on the surface of the body.  Incisions on these tumors are dangerous, especially in the armpit, groin, neck and many other parts of the body, and if they are very large, one should avoid treating them with iron.
Also, those in the extremities or head should be avoided.  If it is a swelling of the artery's mouth, make a longitudinal incision in the skin, then open the incision with hooks, then dissect and free the artery from the sheaths.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle beneath it and pierce it to the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread at two places, as I explained to you in the ligation of the arteries in the temples.  Then puncture with a lancet the area between the ligaments, until all the blood is removed.
Then the swelling will subside, and then use a treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall off.  Then treat with suitable ointments until healed.
If the swelling is from a vein rupture, then you should grasp as much of the swelling with the skin as possible. Then insert a needle below the area you grasped, piercing it and a double thread within it, until you remove it from the other side. Then tie the swelling firmly, according to what I mentioned to you regarding tying the pouch from both sides with the ends of the thread.  If you fear the threads will slip, insert another needle with another thread beneath the entire swelling where the first needle crosses.
Tie your threads in four places, then cut the swelling in the middle until what is inside comes out.  Cut off any excess skin, leaving the tied portion. Then apply a poultice soaked in liquid and oil. Then use treatment with lotions and ointments until healed.
Chapter Fifty.
In the swelling that arises from nerve twisting.
Just as a swelling appears in the artery and vein, so too does a swelling appear in the nerve if struck or caused by excessive exertion, or similar.  This is most common in the wrists and ankles.
And in every location where the joints move, it is a hard swelling, similar in color to the rest of the body.  It is often without pain.  If pressed hard, the sufferer feels a sensation similar to numbness.  The swelling is not deeply embedded in the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, but does not go forward or backward.  Any swelling in the joints should not be treated with iron, for it might cause a wound.  Any swelling in the head or forehead...
Cut the skin with a scalpel. If the swelling is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from the root. If it is large, suspend it with a hook, dissect it, then remove it. Close the wound with stitches and treat it until healed.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On the removal of warts that appear on the abdomen.
Warts, called fungal warts for their resemblance to fungi, often appear on the abdomen and other parts of the body. Their base is thin and their head thick; sometimes their lips are swollen.  They vary in size, from small to very large.
I observed a man with two warts on his abdomen, resembling fungi.  Their bases were thin and white, their lips swollen and cracked, and moisture constantly dripping from them. I removed them, the weight of one being approximately eighteen ounces and the other about six ounces.
If the affliction is moist, and the wart's color is white, moist, and thin-based, cut it with a broad scalpel, having a cautery iron at hand.  For frequently much blood flows when cutting them.
If blood overwhelms you, cauterize it.  If the afflicted person is afraid of the cut, take a strong lead thread, tie it tightly around the wart, and leave it for two days.  Then increase the tension of the lead thread, tightening it further.
Continue tightening until the wart breaks off and falls away of its own accord, without further effort.  If the wart has a thick base, cutting it presents some danger, particularly if located on the abdomen.  However, it might be possible to cut off half or a part of it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat the wound until healed. Beware of cutting a wart that is dark, insensitive, and dull-looking, for it is a cancerous tumor.  We will discuss cancer after this.
The Fifty-Second Chapter.
In the treatment of navel growths.
Navel growths arise from various causes: either from a rupture in the abdominal peritoneum, allowing the intestines to protrude, as seen in other hernias; or from bleeding from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from trapped gas.
If it is due to a rupture in the peritoneum and the protrusion of the intestines, the tumor will be similar in color to the body, soft without pain, and will have a varied appearance. If it is due to the intestines protruding, its appearance will be even more varied than what we have described.  When you press it with your fingers, it disappears, then returns; and there may be rumbling sounds with it, and it may grow significantly larger during baths and intense exertion. If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
If it does not disappear when pressed with your hand, and does not increase or decrease, if it is due to blood, the tumor will take on a dark appearance along with these signs. If it is due to growing flesh, the tumor will be hard and firm, and will remain the same size.
If it is due to gas, its touch will be soft.  In such a case, one should refrain from treatment, as there is fear and deception involved, as I instructed you in the chapter concerning tumors that arise from arteries and veins.
If the protrusion of the navel is due to the intestines or intestines, you should instruct the patient to hold still and stand upright, then trace the outline of the entire navel with ink. Then you should instruct him to lie on his back before you.  Then, using a wide scalpel, cut around the navel at the spot marked by ink.  Then, use a large probe to lift the center of the tumor upwards.  Finally, tie the incision site with a strong thread or silk tendon in a firm manner.
The knot of the bandage should be a figure-eight knot.  Then, open the center of the constricted tumor above the bandage, insert your index finger, and feel for the intestine. If you find the bandage has caught it, loosen the figure-eight knot and push the intestine back into the abdomen. If you find an opening (fistula), dilate it with a probe.
And cut off any excess. If an artery or vein presents an obstacle, ligate it tightly and return to your work. Then take two needles, thread them with strong threads, and insert the needles into the incision around the tumor, having made them pierce the tissue, and then pull the tumor tightly in four places at the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the pulled flesh decays and falls off on its own, or cut it once it thins and rots. Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If the protrusion of the navel is from growing flesh in it.
Or if it's from moisture, then you should cauterize the tumor as we said, and remove the blood or moisture you find in it.  Then treat it with what will heal the area.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the treatment of cancer.

The ancients stated that if the cancer is in a place where it can be completely removed, such as cancer in the breast or thigh, and similar removable organs, especially if it is small and early, then it should be completely extracted. However, if it is advanced and large, one should not approach it.
I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve this.  If it is advanced, as we said, one should proceed carefully, and allow the affected person to have several episodes of black discharge, then to dissect it if there is a clear fullness in the veins.
Then, the patient should be positioned in a way that allows for work to be done. Allow the blood to flow, do not stop it quickly.

If during the procedure, a large amount of blood is encountered due to cutting an artery or vein, cauterize the affected area until the bleeding stops and then treat it with other methods until it heals.

Chapter Fifty-Eight.
On the treatment of urinary retention in the bladder.
Urinary retention in the bladder occurs due to blockage by a stone, coagulated blood, pus, or a growing piece of flesh, and the like.  If you treat this with the various treatments described in the classification.
If the urine does not flow and you suspect the obstruction is due to a stone that has harmed the neck of the bladder, the patient should be placed on their knees, then have someone sit on their back, extending their abdomen towards the back, and the patient should hold onto themselves as much as possible.
The neck of the bladder will then be forced to push the stone backward, and the urine will flow. If the urine does not flow using the method described and the patient's condition worsens, it is necessary to use a device called a catheter, of which this is a picture:.
It is made of silver, thin and smooth, hollow like a bird's feather tube, about one and a half spans long, with a gentle funnel at its head.  The method of drawing out urine with it is to take a folded thread and tie a piece of wool or cotton firmly to one end.
The thread is inserted into the lower end of the catheter, and any excess wool is trimmed with nippers so that it fits snugly within the tube, plugging it like a stopper. Then, the catheter is lubricated with oil, butter, or egg white, and the patient sits in a chair.
His bladder and urethra are lubricated with moist ointments or warm oil and water.  Then, the catheter is gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the base of the urethra. Then the urethra is bent upwards towards the navel, and the catheter is pushed further inside.
Once the urethra is bent, the catheter is pushed further inside until it nears the anus.  Then the penis is tilted downwards, the catheter inside it, and it is pushed until the catheter reaches the bladder, and the patient feels that it has reached an empty space.
This is done in this way because the passage through which the urine flows is curved.  Then, the thread with the wool is pulled gently, so the urine follows the wool, and then it is withdrawn and the urine is drained, and the catheter is reinserted.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.

Chapter Sixty.
On removing stones.
We have previously described the types of stones, their treatment with medications, and the differences between kidney stones and bladder stones, along with the signs of each. We have also described stones requiring manual removal and incision.
This is particularly the case with bladder stones, and those that form in the urethra. I will describe this clearly and concisely.
I say that bladder stones most often affect boys. A sign is that the urine exiting the bladder is as thin as water.
Sand appears in it, he rubs and plays with his penis, and it often hangs down.  The anus often protrudes in many of them.  Recovery is easier in boys until they reach fourteen years of age, more difficult in the elderly, and middling in the case of young adults.
Those with larger stones have easier treatment, and the reverse is true for smaller stones.  Therefore, in treatment, the patient should first be administered an enema to remove all the impurities in his intestines, as this may prevent the detection of the stone.
Then the patient is taken by the legs, shaken, and moved downwards to bring the stone to the neck of the bladder, or he is made to jump from a high place several times.  Then, he is seated upright before you, hands under his thighs, so that the entire bladder tilts downwards, and then you examine him.
Palpate him from the outside.  If you feel the stone in the space, immediately make an incision on it. If it is not at all within your reach, then you should lubricate the index finger (if the patient is a boy) or the middle finger with grease from the left hand.
If it is a fully developed boy, insert it into his anus and search for the stone.  Once found, move it slowly towards the neck of the bladder, then press on it with your finger and push it outward towards the area you intend to incise.
Tell a servant to squeeze the bladder with his hand, and tell another servant to stretch the labia upwards with his right hand, and with his other hand, to stretch the skin below the labia towards the location of the incision.  Then, take the dissecting forceps, which has this form:
Incise between the anus and the labia, not in the middle, but on the side of the left groin. The incision should be directly over the stone. Your finger should be in the anus, pressing the stone outward in a sloping manner, so that the incision is wide from the outside and narrow from the inside, as much as possible, to allow the stone to exit.
Do not increase the pressure.  Perhaps the finger in the anus will dislodge the stone during the incision, allowing easy removal.  Understand that some stones have angles and edges, making removal difficult.
Some are smooth, like acorns, and round, making removal easy. If it has angles and edges, increase the incision slightly. If it does not come out this way, you must devise a method, either to grasp it with a strong forceps whose tip is like a rasp.
To grip the stone so it does not slip, or to insert a delicate, curved instrument underneath it.  If you cannot accomplish this, widen the incision slightly.  If any significant bleeding occurs, stop it with a cautery iron.
If there are multiple stones, first push the largest one into the bladder opening, then incise around it.  Afterward, push out the smaller stones.  Do the same if there are more than two.  If the stones are extremely large, it is foolish to make a large incision, for it exposes the patient to one of two things.
Either he will die, or he will experience constant dribbling of urine because the area will never heal properly.  However, attempt to push them out, or devise a method to break them with forceps until they are expelled.
Once your work is complete, dress the wound with kender, sabr, sheyan, and strong bandages.  Place over it a compress moistened with oil and a drink, or rose ointment and cool water to soothe the hot swelling.  Then, the patient should lie on their back, and do not remove the bandages until the third day.
Once the swelling subsides, bathe the area with plenty of water and oil.  Then treat it with palm ointment and basil ointment until healed. If there is excess swelling, inflammation, or similar issues in the wound—for example, if blood clots in the bladder and urination is blocked, and this is recognized by the presence of blood in the urine—
Insert your finger into the wound and remove the blood.  If it remains, it will lead to the corruption and infection of the bladder. Then, wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type with its corresponding remedy until healed.
Throughout the treatment period, the thighs should be bound together to secure the medications applied to the area. If the stone is small and has lodged in the urethra, causing inflammation and blockage of urination, treat it with the methods I have described before it reaches the rupture.
Often, this treatment obviates the need for incision. I have tried it; it involves taking a small, steel probe, shaped as follows:
The tip of the probe is sharp and pointed, attached to a stick. Then, a thread is used to tie the penis below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder. The probe is then gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the stone itself.
Rotate the probe slightly against the stone, gradually, until it pierces it from the other side. Urine will flow immediately. Then, press with your hand on any remaining stone outside the penis; it will break up and pass out with the urine. The patient will recover.
If this treatment proves impossible due to an obstacle, tie a thread below the stone and another above it. Then, make an incision on the stone within the penis, between the threads, and remove it.
Then, loosen the threads and cleanse the coagulated blood from the wound.  The thread was tied below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other above to ensure that when the thread is loosened after the stone is removed, the skin will return to its place and cover the wound.
Therefore, when tying the upper thread, you should pull the skin upwards so that it returns to its place and covers the wound, as we have said.
Chapter Sixty-One.
On removing stones in women.
Stones rarely develop in women.  If one of them presents with a stone, treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons.  One is that the woman may be a virgin, and the other that you will not find a woman who will allow herself to be examined by the physician.
If she is a chaste woman or one with close relatives, the third reason is that you won't find a woman skilled in this craft, especially the manual work involved.  The fourth reason is that the opening to the stone in women is far from the position of the stone, requiring a deep incision.
In this case there is danger.  If necessity demands it, one should seek a skilled female physician, though such are rare.  If one is not available, seek a virtuous and reliable male physician, or bring in a skilled female midwife or one who has some knowledge of this craft.
Bring her in and instruct her to examine the stone first.  If the woman is a virgin, insert a finger into her vagina to search for the stone. If found, press it against the finger.
If she is not a virgin, but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the woman's vagina and search for the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it firmly.
If she finds it, she should gently guide it from the opening of the bladder downwards, as far as possible, until it reaches the root of the thigh. Then, she should make the incision at the midpoint of the vagina, near the root of the thigh, whichever side feels the stone to be present.
Her finger should remain pressing on the stone, and the incision should be made small at first. Then, introduce the forceps into the small incision. If she feels the stone, increase the incision as needed to remove the stone.  Know that there are many types of stones.
There are small and large stones, smooth and rough, long and round, and those with branches. Know their types so you can determine what you want. If blood flows excessively, sprinkle powdered alum on the area and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then resume your work.
Until the stone is removed, prepare the instruments mentioned for removing stones in men to assist in your work. If excessive bleeding occurs and you know the bleeding originates from a severed artery, apply styptics to the area.
Tighten the styptics firmly and leave them in place. Do not remove them. Leave the stone and do not remove it, for the patient may die. Then treat the wound. If the intensity of bleeding subsides after a few days and the area becomes infected, resume your work until the stone is expelled.
Chapter Sixty-two.
In the cleft of the water ducts.
Watery ducts are a collection of moisture in the white peritoneum which lies beneath the scrotum's skin surrounding the testicle. This is called the scrotum. It may also be in a special membrane, which nature has prepared on one side of the testicle.
So that it may be thought to be another testicle. It is situated between the scrotal skin and the white peritoneum, which we have mentioned. This only occurs rarely. These ducts arise from weakness affecting the testicles; this material may also be caused by injury to the testicles.
This moisture has various colours: it may be yellowish, or blood-red, or dark-greyish-black, or whitish and watery – which is the most common. The signs by which it is recognised are:
Where the water collects, if it is in the white peritoneum we mentioned, the swelling will be somewhat elongated and round, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle will not be apparent, because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a separate membrane, the swelling will be round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, one might imagine it to be another testicle.  If the moisture is between the scrotal skin and the white peritoneum, then it can be felt.  To determine the colour of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe, whose image we have previously described.  Judge its colour by what comes out in the probe's path.
If we proceed to treatment with the lancet, the patient should be bled, if possible, and if you see the body is full, then the patient should lie on their back on something slightly elevated, with many cloths placed beneath, and you should sit on their left side.
Have a servant sit on their right, extending the patient's penis to one side of the scrotal skin, toward the flank. Then take a broad scalpel and make a longitudinal incision in the scrotal skin, from the center, down towards the pubic area.
The incision should be made straight, parallel to the line dividing the scrotal skin in half, until you reach the white peritoneum.  Carefully dissect this, avoiding cutting it.  Focus your dissection on the side closest to the testicle, and dissect as thoroughly as possible.
Then, widen the incision in the water-filled peritoneum and remove all the water.  Then, separate the edges of the incision with forceps, and pull the peritoneum upward, avoiding the scrotal skin.  Cut the peritoneum however you can, either entirely or in pieces.
Especially the thin side; if you don't thoroughly cut it, there is a risk that the fluid will return.  If the testicle protrudes from its covering while you are working, return it to its place after you have finished cutting the peritoneum, then stitch the edges of the scrotal skin together.
Then treat it as you would any other surgical wound until it heals.  If the testicle is already damaged by another condition, tie off the vessels supporting it to prevent bleeding, then detach the testicle from its supporting structure and remove the testicle. Then treat it as described above.
If there is fluid accumulated on both sides, understand that it is a double hydrocele.  Incise the other side in the same way as the first. If you prefer a single procedure, do so. Then wipe away the blood.
Insert wool soaked in oil or rose oil into the incisions.  Place another layer of wool soaked in a mixture of liquid and oil over the testicles and the abdominal area.  Then lay a folded cloth (the *rafā'id*) on top. Tie it down with a six-sided bandage, like this:
Then, take two cloths, stitch them together, and fill them with fluffy wool, in this manner.  Make the straps to fasten them with damp cotton or wool.  Tie them together, tightly, with a belt. One of the straps should go from the buttocks to the back.
The second strap should go towards the pubic area, and the other two should go down the thighs, and the remaining two down the upper thighs.  Tie all the straps together with a belt.  This hydrocele can also be treated with cauterization instead of incision with a sharp instrument.
This involves using a gentle, heated scalpel to incise the scrotum until the white membrane containing the water is exposed.  Take another cauterizing instrument in this fashion:
It resembles a Greek eye; then cauterize the membrane with this, while hot, until all the moisture is expelled.  Then, grasp the membrane with forceps and cauterize the surrounding layer, cutting it as best as you can to remove all of it, while avoiding burning the testicle.
Know that this method is safer and avoids blood loss, and better than the incision method.  If during the incision or cauterization, a burning swelling or blood flow, or anything else, occurs, you should treat whatever arises with the appropriate remedies, as previously described.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot tolerate this procedure due to its unpleasantness, then use cauterization as described; and if this is confirmed by the evidence presented before.
If the swelling is watery, the patient should sit on a high chair.  Then, cauterize the swelling below with a wide probe.  Make the cauterization lengthwise along the body, and make the opening wide enough for all the water to drain.  Then apply cotton to the area and bandage it.
Allow the remaining water to drain, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not drain during the cauterization, it is likely because the white membrane is obstructing the opening of the wound, preventing the water from exiting.
In that case, you should insert a goose quill, or the instrument used to extract the water of the beloved (whose image was previously shown), or slightly enlarge the opening of the wound.
Know that the water may return and accumulate after six months or so.  If it accumulates, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should observe his/her days (i.e., rest).
Sixty-third chapter.
Concerning the laceration of fleshy tissues and their treatment.
Know that laceration of these fleshy tissues is often a perilous condition leading to death.  Therefore, we advise against interfering with it, and for the safety of the patient.  However, I am about to describe the treatment and the types of tumors associated with it.
I say that many tumors may occur in the bodies composed of tissues of the same kind.  This arises from various causes, either from excessive heat directed towards the tissues, or from injury. The tumor's color is the same as the body's color, and there is no water present.
The tumor is firm, and it may be hardened, its color like mud, and it is insensitive. It may also result from the entanglement and swelling of arteries, as previously mentioned, or from the swelling of veins.  If it is from the swelling of the arteries, this can be identified by the dissipation of the tumor.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be interfered with at all.  However, if it arises from the swelling of veins, nothing will dissipate when pressed with the fingers. This type may be lacerated, and so may other flesh.
This is done by incising the scrotum, then pulling the testicle upward, and removing it from the white peritoneum.  The attachments are separated from the vessels, the vessels are tied off, and the attachments are cut after being freed on all sides of the testicle.  If the testicle has adhered to these growing tissues.
Then the testicle should be removed and cut.  If the adhesion is between parts of the peritoneum or between vessels, then all such adhesion should be freed and cut in a circular fashion.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion, which is behind, then all of it should be cut, and the testicle removed, as we have said.  When your work is complete, dress the wound with wet wool in rose-oil and drink, then treat it with all the other treatments until it heals.
Sixty-fourth chapter.
Concerning the treatment of dropsy associated with elephantiasis.
Elephantiasis is a twisted swelling, resembling a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the limbs.  It hinders the afflicted person from movement, exercise, and walking. In treating this ailment, the treatment is similar to what has been mentioned previously.
However, it is necessary to mention the treatment as done by the ancients.  I say that the afflicted person should sit on a high chair, then pull the affected limbs downward, and then grasp the skin of the testicles with your fingers, near the vessels that are close to the penis.
A servant of yours should hold it (the skin) and pull it strongly. Then, with a broad, sharp scalpel, make a slanted incision alongside the vessels until the vessels are exposed.  Then, peel it from every side, as I have described to you in the case of the arteries in the temples.  Then, insert a needle with a double-threaded string into it.
Tie it (the string) at the first place where the elephantiasis appeared, and also at its end.  Then, make a straight incision along the body in the middle, and remove the foul, putrid humours that have collected there.
Then treat the wound like other wounds that need time to heal, so that the vessels that feed the affected limb will fall away. There is no harm in that. If the elephantiasis affects all the vessels, then one limb must be removed with the vessels so that the egg does not lack nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, it will wither and be useless.
Chapter Sixty-five.
On the treatment of intestinal obstruction.
This obstruction occurs when a fissure appears in the peritoneum extending across the abdomen, near the groin.  The intestines protrude from that hernia into one of the limbs.
This hernia arises either from a fissure in the peritoneum or from its extension.  These two types result from various causes, such as a blow, a leap, a shout, or lifting a heavy object, and the like.  If it's from an extension of the peritoneum, the hernia develops gradually over a long period.
It does not appear suddenly.  If it is from the extension, the swelling gradually extends to a certain depth, because the peritoneum is constricting the intestines. If it arises from a fissure in the peritoneum, there is initially intense pain immediately, and the swelling is distinctly visible beneath the skin nearby.
The intestines protrude and exit the peritoneum.  Feces may also exit, and this obstruction is then termed intestinal and fecal.  It may also contain air.  The intestines may also contain stagnant matter, which causes the patient's demise.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially if constricted.  The treatment for these conditions using iron is risky; therefore, one should be cautious.  The procedure is to instruct the patient to push the intestines back into the abdominal cavity with their hand if it is possible to return them.
Then, the patient lies supine, with their legs raised.  Then, the skin around the groin is stretched upward, and the entire scrotum is incised lengthwise.  Hooks are inserted into the edges of the incision as much as needed to widen the opening.
The incision should be wide enough to allow the testicle to emerge.  Then, the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin is peeled back until the white, tough peritoneum is exposed on all sides.  Then, insert your index finger next to the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum underneath the testicle's skin and the second peritoneum, detach the adhesions behind the testicle.  Then, use your right hand to pull inward on the scrotal skin.  At the same time, use your left hand to pull the white peritoneum upward and lift the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
Instruct the assistant to pull the testicle upward.  You should fully detach the adhesions behind it.  Examine with your finger to ensure there is no twisted bowel within the tough white peritoneum. If you find any, push it back into the abdomen below.
Then, take a needle with a thick, ten-stranded thread.  Insert it at the edge of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin, near the incision.  Then cut the ends of the thread so that only four strands remain. Then, join the strands together tightly.  Tie the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin tightly on both sides with this.  Wrap the ends of the threads tightly as well, so that nothing can reach the nourishing vessels. So that a hot swelling does not appear.  Tie a second ligature, outside the first, at least two fingerbreadths away.  After these two ligatures, leave a portion of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin the size of a finger's breadth.
So that a hot swelling does not appear.  Tie a second ligature, outside the first, at least two fingerbreadths away.  After these two ligatures, leave a portion of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin the size of a finger's breadth. Cut the rest of it (the peritoneum) in a circle and remove the testicle.  Then, make a cut beneath the scrotal skin that bleeds and oozes, as described previously.  Use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound, and apply the ligature as described.
The white peritoneum, after being cut, may require cauterization to prevent bleeding, as this often happens.  Leave the ligature until it falls off naturally.  If it is slow to fall off, then apply warm water and anything that causes perspiration to encourage its removal.
Then treat the wound with the usual treatment for surgical wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-six.
In this type of spasmodic colic, I have seen no one dare to treat it with iron.  The ancients advised, as described in colic with a hernia, to tie the vessels gently after incision below.
Then, an incision is made in the center, and the swelling is treated to open it, until the vessels fall out. The wound is treated as previously described until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-six.
In the hernia of the groin.
A hernia in the groin may appear, as we said, but no portion of the intestines descends into the groin. If any descends, it is slight and returns at all times.
However, if it persists for a long time, the opening in the peritoneum increases, so that the intestines and the testicle descend into the scrotum. This is due to the extension of the peritoneum in the groin, as we said. This is because the peritoneum stretches, then relaxes and protrudes the area.  Treatment is by cautery, as its description has been given before.
Treatment may also be with an iron, in this manner: the patient lies on their back before you, then you make a transverse incision in the swollen, protruding hernia, about the width of three fingers, and then you cauterize the peritoneum beneath the skin.
Until the white peritoneum beneath is exposed, then take a strip, place it over the protruding peritoneum and press it into the depth of the abdomen, then stitch the two protruding parts of the peritoneum onto the end of the strip, and join one to the other with stitches.
Then pull the end of the strip, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, and do not touch the testicle or anything else, as I have taught you in the treatment of intestinal hernias. Then treat it with the treatment of surgical incisions.
When the threads are cut, clean it and bandage the wound until it heals.  Then the peritoneum contracts, and the hernia protrusion will not increase.
This is the best method in this location, because it is closest to safety.
Chapter Sixty-Eight.
On the relaxation of the scrotum's skin.
The scrotum's skin often relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and this makes the appearance unsightly.  Therefore, whoever wishes to treat this should lay the patient on their back.
He should cut all the relaxed skin from the tough skin, then bring the edges together.  If you wish, first stitch the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, secure the stitching, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the usual treatment for other wounds until it heals and the stitches come out.
Sixty-ninth chapter.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book, but I did so for two reasons: one to be knowledgeable in medicine when asked about it, and to know how to treat those who are affected by it, and the other because we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as sheep, oxen, and cats, and similar animals; I say that castration is of two types: either by crushing or by incision and cutting.  For that which is done by crushing, the method is that we seat the animal in hot water until its testicles relax and soften and hang down.
Then you crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer discernible to the touch.  As for castration by incision and cutting, it is necessary to hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with the left hand, then tie the ligaments, and make a single incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles protrude, then cut them after having peeled them, leaving no peritoneum on them except the thin peritoneum which is over the vessels.  This type of castration is better than that which is done by crushing.
Because crushing might leave some of the testicles, and the animal will desire mating.  Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of the hermaphrodite.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types. One appears as a space or a scrotum with a form like a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may drip from the scrotum.
In women, there is one type, located above the vulva on the pubic bone, like small, protruding male testicles. One of them is like a man's penis, and both are like female genitalia. Treatment for these three types.
The two types of men and the single type of women, those superfluous tissues must be cut away, then treated with the usual surgical care until healed. As for the second type of men from whom urine issues from the scrotum, there is no work to be done, and no cure at all.
Chapter Seventy-one.
In the cutting of the clitoris and the protruding flesh of women's vulva.
The clitoris may increase beyond the natural size, becoming unsightly and disfigured. In some women, it may enlarge to the point of resembling a male organ, interfering with sexual intercourse.
One should grasp the excess clitoris with one's hand or forceps and cut it off, but one should not make the cut too deep, especially not into the root, so as to avoid excessive bleeding.
Then treat it with surgical remedies until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the uterus, filling it.
And it may extend outward, like a tail. Therefore, some early physicians called it the "tail" disease.  It should be cut off, like the clitoris, and treated until healed.
Chapter Seventy-two.
On the treatment of ruptures.
A rupture is when a woman's vulva is either not pierced or the piercing is small, and it may be either natural, from birth, or accidental.  An accidental rupture may be from a prior ailment, and it may be due to excessive, newly grown flesh.
Or from thin or thick peritoneum, located either deep within the uterus, or along its sides, or at its top or bottom.  It prevents intercourse, childbirth, and possibly menstruation.  A midwife can determine this by observation.
If the obstruction is visible and near, but if it is not visible, then she should examine it with her fingers or with a probe. If the obstruction is from thin peritoneum and near the labia, then promptly pierce it; this involves making a kind of flap on the labia, then placing both thumbs on them.
The woman should lie on her back with her legs spread, then forcefully stretch the labia until the thin peritoneum is pierced and the obstruction opens.  Then, she should take wool soaked in oil and place it on the area. The woman should be cohabited with every day, so the area does not close again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, it should be cut with a wide scalpel, similar to a leaf-shaped blade. If the obstruction is from newly grown flesh, grasp it with forceps and cut it.  Have remedies to stop bleeding without cauterization, such as qaqia, sheyan, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube so the wound does not heal quickly. Hold it for several days and use a thread of dry linen. Then treat it with the standard treatment for abscesses until it heals.  Other growths of flesh may appear in the uterus, and these should be cut in the same way.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, the cancerous tumor that occurs in the uterus should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that appear on the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the veins' openings, to the point of constant profuse bleeding. If hemorrhoids progress, they become warts.  Their types and signs were mentioned in the classification, and we will mention them here.
Concerning those that can and cannot be treated: I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep within the uterus and do not present to the touch, have no treatment with iron.  Those that are in the opening of the uterus, where the touch can reach them, are the ones that can be treated.
The woman should enter a cool room, then scrape the warts with a lancet or a rough cloth and cut them from their roots.  Then, when blood flows from one of the blood-stopping instruments, without pricking, apply it to them.
Such as qaqia, shiyan, and frankincense, and similar things. Then elevate her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If bleeding continues, seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, and astringents, and similar things.  Then take barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar.
Apply it as a poultice to her back. If the bleeding stops, do so; otherwise, continue with the strong leeches on the chest and breasts, without restrictions. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of thorns or the juice of a shepherd's purse or the juice of the branches of liquorice with a drink of astringents, and apply it to the affected area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals.  As for the red sore, it resembles the heads of warts, rough in appearance.  It should be cut off, as described for warts, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the treatment of abscesses that appear in the uterus.
Many types of tumors can occur in the uterus, such as cancer, hardened tumors, ulcers, gangrene, cancerous ulcers, polyps, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and inflammatory tumors.  All of these diseases and their types and signs have been mentioned in the classification.
Therefore, in this section, we must discuss the inflammatory tumor that appears in the uterus, if it is one of the tumors that accumulate over time. How should it be treated with iron?  You must consider: if the pain of the tumor is initially severe, accompanied by throbbing, inflammation, and fever.
If you see it (the tumor) red, and if it is sensitive to touch, do not rush to treat it. Treat it with what aids maturation until these symptoms subside.  Then, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats, leaning on her back.
And she should bring her legs towards the lower abdomen, with her thighs spread apart. Her arms should be placed under her knees and tied with a suitable bandage.
Then, the midwife should sit on the right side and use the instrument to open the cervix, a depiction of which will follow later.  When using this instrument, you must estimate the depth of the woman's uterus to ensure that what enters the instrument does not exceed the depth of the uterus.
For if the instrument is too deep, it will harm the patient.  Therefore, pads should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the uterine depth.  The coil that runs within the instrument must be positioned from the upper side.
While you hold the instrument, the attendant should rotate the coil until the cervix opens. If the abscess is visible and soft, easily felt by hand, it should be incised at its head with a broad scalpel. If all the pus is discharged.
A soft wick dipped in rose oil or green oil with some astringent should be placed in the wound.  The wick should extend out from the incision in the cervix.  Pure wool soaked in boiled baker's water should be applied externally, over the uterus and pubis.
Then, treat with the appropriate ointment for two days until healed.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water boiled with the root of sassafras or long wormwood.  Then, return to the ointment, if the abscess is hidden and within the uterus.
Iron should not be used to treat it, but it should be treated as described in the classification.
Chapter seventy-five.
On teaching midwives how to treat living fetuses that emerge in an unnatural form.
The midwife should first know the natural form of childbirth.  Its signs include:  when the woman stretches downwards, desires to smell the air, and finds her postpartum state less burdensome.
If the birth of the fetus is swift, know that this birth is natural, with the head emerging first, and the placenta either with it or hanging from its umbilical cord.  If you see these signs, then you should massage her abdomen to facilitate the quick expulsion of the fetus.
If it descends head-first, the placenta descends with it, and is cleansed well of impurities.  Any birth other than this is unnatural and reprehensible, as the fetus may emerge with its legs, or with its arms and legs before its head.
One arm or one leg, or its head and an arm, or it emerges folded, or perhaps it turns upside down, and other such reprehensible forms.  The midwife should be skillful and gentle with all these things, and avoid errors and mistakes.
I am showing every form of this, and how to manage it, so that you will recognize it and understand it. If the fetus emerges head-first, in the natural manner, and the woman experiences difficulty and pain, and you see her strength weakening, then have her sit on a chair, and instruct women to restrain her.
Compress her uterus with boiled herbs and moist ointments. Then the midwife should insert a small scalpel between her fingers, and cut the placenta, or cut it with her fingernail, so that the moisture within it flows out, and massage the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then the woman should be injected with licorice juice mixed with shellac ointment.  Then, after the injection, she should be encouraged to cough and sneeze using a nasal syringe, and hold her mouth and nose. The fetus will emerge shortly. If the fetus's arms emerge first, gently return them little by little.
If entry is not possible, place the woman on a platform, raising her legs upward. Then shake the platform on the ground, while the woman holds on to prevent falling during the shaking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the remaining fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and gently pull them.  It will come out.  If the fetus emerges on its feet, push the feet upward, then turn the fetus gradually until it is straight, then take one of the feet and gently extend it.
If they emerge to the thighs, instruct her to cough and sneeze with a nasal syringe; it will come out.  If it does not come out as described, gently reposition the fetus until it is in its natural form.  It will then emerge smoothly. If it remains stubbornly resistant to all of these methods, ...
Take the juice of *Al-Khatmi*, licorice juice, and shellac ointment, and gum dissolved in a mortar.  Grind them well.  Then apply this mixture to the woman's vulva and lower abdomen.  Then have her sit in lukewarm water until the thighs are reached.
If you see that it has softened below, make a poultice of myrrh and apply it.
