If she keeps it on for an hour, have her sit on a chair, then sneeze her and block her mouth and nose, and gently squeeze her lower abdomen.  The fetus will emerge immediately.  The fetus will come out on its knees and hands.
If it emerges in this manner, gently insert your hands, then have the woman lie on her back, with her legs dangling downwards, lying on a bed, and her hands clasped together. Then, gently press on her upper abdomen. Then remove the cushions from beneath her legs so that they are positioned low. If the fetus does not emerge, grasp both her legs and move them vigorously.  Then press gently above the thighs until the fetus rises.  Then the midwife should insert her hand and gently reshape the fetus.
Tell the woman to strain until the fetus emerges. The fetus will emerge sideways, with one arm dangling.  Gently attempt to reposition the arm. If you cannot, stand the woman up and have her walk.  If she cannot walk, have her lie down.
Shake her legs vigorously.  Then use the ointment that I described from the saliva. If it is delivered this way, have her sit on a chair, then work to reposition the arm and straighten the fetus.  Have the woman sneeze and tell her to strain until it emerges.
The fetus emerges on its back, arms outstretched, face toward the mother's back.  The midwife should grasp the fetus's arm and gently turn and straighten it.  Once straightened, apply the fat and saliva as we described.
Then tell her to move slowly.  When she does, have her sit on a chair and instruct her to lean to the right side.  Then tell her to strain and sneeze her with the kuṇḍas (a type of instrument). This will cause it to emerge. The fetus will emerge upright on its side.
If it descends into the cervix in this manner with the placenta, slit the placenta with your fingernail until it is free of moisture. If it is attached to the face or neck of the fetus, sever it from the navel so it does not suffocate and die. Then push the fetus inward until it is in the proper position.
Tell the woman to strain repeatedly, and it will emerge easily.  The birth of twins or multiple fetuses: Know that twins are frequently born, and sometimes three or four may be born and live, but this is rare.  As for five, it is unnatural and they will not live at all.
And they are surrounded by a single placenta, even if numerous.  They are separated by a membrane partition attached to the navel of each.  They may emerge like a single fetus, in a natural, easy birth, or they may emerge in a difficult birth, as we have described.
If any of them have difficulty during birth and become in the unnatural forms that we have mentioned, treat them as we have described.  Let the midwife be gentle, and act with patience and cunning in any form, by whatever means seem appropriate to achieve safety.
There are concepts of fetuses in the womb which may be aborted: one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived.  It is true that I have heard that one woman miscarried seven, and another fifteen, all conceived.
By the decree of the Exalted and Knowing, all this should be within the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a case may occur to her someday, and she should deal with it appropriately.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
In the extraction of the dead fetus.
If, treating the dead fetus with the methods described in the division of treatment, it does not emerge and the situation necessitates manual intervention, then one should observe. If the woman is strong and healthy, with no signs of complications that might cause harm, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be tilted downwards, and her legs raised, with an attendant holding her at both sides, or tying her to the bed, so that her body does not shift when the fetus is drawn out.
Then, the mouth of her uterus is moistened with moist ointments, along with the saliva of the *khatmi* flower, fenugreek, and flaxseed.  Then the midwife anoints her hand with these ointments and saliva.  Then, gently, she inserts it into the uterus, seeking a place to insert the hooks from the fetus, in suitable locations.
Observe; if the fetus is positioned to descend head-first, insert the hooks into its eyes, or nape, or mouth, or palate, or under its chin, or collarbone, or near the ribs, or under the clavicles.
And if it is descending feet-first, then in the pubis and the middle ribs.  One should hold the hook with the right hand, its curve between the fingers of the left hand.  The hand should be gently inserted and a hook should be implanted in one of those locations we have mentioned.
Until it reaches an empty space, then another or a third hook is implanted opposite it, if needed, so that the pulling is even and does not tilt towards one side. Then, the pulling should be even and not just in a straight line, but moved to each side.
So that it comes out easily, as is done with a tooth when extracting it.  And in between, the pulling should be relaxed if it is obstructed in a direction.  Then the midwife should anoint some of her fingers with the ointment and insert them from the side to turn what is stuck. If part of the fetus comes out, the hooks are moved to other locations.
She does this slightly higher until the entire fetus is expelled. If a hand comes out before the rest and cannot be returned due to compression, then it should be wrapped in a cloth and pulled until, when it is all out, it is cut.
Either at the wrist joint or the elbow.  This should be done similarly for the other hand and feet.  If the fetal head is large, and faces pressure during its exit or has accumulated water in its head, then a pointed, narrow knife should be inserted between the fingers.
With it, the head is cut open so that the water comes out, or it is forcefully opened using the instrument called the *mashdakh*, which is depicted with the images of the other instruments in the following chapter.
Similarly, if the fetus is naturally large-headed, the skull should be cut open or cracked with the *mashdakh*, as we said, and then the bones removed with the forceps. If the head emerges and is compressed at the collarbone, it should be cut open so that the moisture in the chest drains out.
Then the chest will adhere. If the chest does not adhere and the extraction is not possible, then make a cut as best as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or has a tumor, it should be incised to allow the moisture to drain out.
If the fetus is on its feet, pulling it and aligning it toward the cervix is easy. If it is compressed against the abdomen or chest, it should be pulled using a cloth wrapped around your hand, and the abdomen or chest should be cut open to allow the contents to drain.
If the rest of the organs have been removed and the head has retracted and become lodged, the midwife should insert her left hand. If the cervix is open, the hand should be inserted into the neck of the uterus, searching for the head and pulling it with the fingers toward the cervix. Then, a hook or two hooks should be inserted and used to pull it out.
If the cervix has closed due to a hot tumor, it should not be forced. Instead, application of emollient liquids and bandages should be used, and sitting in relaxing, moist water.
If the fetus is on its side, if alignment is possible, use the methods described for a live fetus. If alignment is not possible, it should be cut and removed.  Nothing of the placenta should be left inside.  Once you are finished with the treatment, use the methods described for treating hot tumors in the uterus.
If bleeding occurs, she should sit in constricting water and use other remedies.  I have observed a woman who conceived, then the fetus died inside her, and she conceived again afterward.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a tumor appeared in her womb, swelling until it opened and discharged pus.  I was summoned to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not heal.  I then applied some strong, astringent ointment.
Then a bone came out of the area, and after several days, another bone emerged.  I was astonished by this, as the abdomen is not a location for bone.  I surmised that these were bones of the dead fetus, and I examined the wound, removing numerous bones from the fetal skull.
I continued this process until I extracted many bones, and the woman was in the best of health.  She lived for a time with a slight discharge of pus from the area.  I have presented this unusual case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skilled physician attempts to accomplish through his hands in the treatment.
Chapter Seventy-seven.
It contains images of the instruments needed for the extraction of the fetus.
Image of a spiral for opening the cervix.
This is the binder with which books are bound. It has two spirals at the ends of the wooden boards.  However, this spiral should be more delicate than the binder and should be either ebony or boxwood. The width of each board should be two finger-widths.
Their edges are about a finger's width, and their length is one and a half spans.  In the middle of the boards are two pegs made of the same wood. Their length is half a span or slightly more, and their width is about two fingers or slightly more.  These pegs.
They are the two that enter the cervix to open it when you turn the spirals.  Image of another tool for the same purpose, more delicate and lighter.
Made of ebony or boxwood, shaped like tongs, but with two pegs at the end, as you see. The length of each peg is about a span, and their width is two fingers.  If you want to open the cervix with it...
Then, place the woman on a bed with her legs spread apart, and insert these two pegs, together, into the cervix.  Hold the end of the tool between her legs, below. Then, open your hand with the tool, just as you would with tongs, as much as you want to open the cervix. So that the midwife may do what she wants.  Image of another spiral mentioned by the ancients.
Image of the instrument for dislodging the fetus.
Image of the instrument used to crush the fetal head.
It resembles scissors, with teeth at the tip, as you see.  It can also be made rectangular, like tongs, in this form, as you see.  It has teeth like a saw's teeth, used to cut and tear.
Image of another instrument.
Image of a hook.
This hook should be somewhat thick so that it does not break when pulling the fetus with it.  Image of a two-pronged hook.
Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All these instruments, the more types there are and the better they are made by the craftsman, the quicker the work and the greater the esteem among people.  Do not disdain any instrument; it may be needed.
Chapter seventy-eight.
In removing the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, the woman should be instructed to hold herself, then have her sneezed at with the instrument, and press your hand over her mouth and nostrils.  If it comes out this way, well and good; otherwise, use a suitable amount (of force).
Pierce a hole in the covering and place in it herbs that open the cervix, such as fennel, chamomile, dill, marjoram, rue, and costmary—any or all of these remedies.  Soak them in water, heat them over a fire, and then place a silver tube over the hole in the covering's opening, and the other end in the cervix.
Hold it until the vapor reaches the bottom of the uterus, and then make her sneeze as mentioned previously.  Then, if the placenta comes out quickly, well and good. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in the grease of a she-goat or the saliva of the hyacinth.
Then, she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta.  If she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out gradually, little by little. If it is stuck deep in the uterus, insert the hand as described until she finds the placenta, and then pull it out slowly and gently.
So that the uterus does not fall out from pulling too hard, it should be gently moved to the sides, right and left. Then, increase the amount of pulling, and it will respond and free itself from the adhesion. If the cervix is closed, we have described the treatment with sneezing, the pot treatment, and herbs.
If it does not come out with all the methods described, avoid force when pulling it again. Instead, tie what has come out to the woman's thigh near the cervix, and then inject her with the four-ingredient ointment.  It will cause it to decay after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out. However, if it decays, a foul odor will rise from it to the stomach and head, harming the patient.  Therefore, appropriate fumigation should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients tried the smoke of frankincense and dried figs.  The image of the device used to fumigate the woman when menstruation and the placenta are retained, and similar cases.
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or of copper. The narrow end is placed near the vulva, and the wide end over the fire. Incense is placed on the embers, held until it dissipates, and then more is added.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of the unperforated anus.
Many boys are born with unperforated anuses, often blocked by a thin membrane.  The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger, or else pierce it with a sharp lancet, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then place cotton soaked in wine and oil on it, and treat it with ointments until it heals. If you fear that it will close again, insert a lead tube into the opening for many days and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
The anus may also be blocked by a healed wound or a tumor.  It is necessary to dissect this healed tissue, then treat it with the remedies mentioned, and with cauterization.
Chapter Eighty.
On treating corns that occur on the soles.
Corns that occur on the soles are a thickening and hardening that occurs near the anus, externally or within the surrounding space on one or more sides.
If this thickening persists, it may open and discharge a whitish watery fluid or thin pus.  Some of these corns may be perforated to the rectum or intestines.  Those that are not perforated will be recognizable by what comes out of them with stool and wind when using the laxative.
Sometimes worms may emerge from them.  Some corns, if located in the surrounding space, may be perforated to the bladder and urethra.  They may also be perforated to the hip joint and tailbone.  The perforated corn that reaches the anus is also distinguishable from the non-perforated kind.
Insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe, made of copper or iron, into the corn if there is no bend in it.  If there is a bend, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair on your finger. If you don't feel it at all, and no stool, wind, or worms emerge from the opening, as we have mentioned, know that it is not perforated, and proceed with treatment.
If the corn is perforated to the bladder or urethra, the sign is the discharge of urine from it, and the inability of the area to heal with medication.  If it is perforated to the hip joint or tailbone, the sign is the probe reaching those areas.
If there is no bending towards the bone, and pain is present in a particular area, and pus constantly emerges from it, and no treatment works and it does not heal with ointment, then these perforated corns have no cure.
Treating them is a futile effort for those physicians who are ignorant, while those that are not perforated and not chronic, hope for healing with iron as I describe and have experienced.  This involves placing the patient on their back before you.
Raise his legs, with his thighs tilted towards his stomach. Then insert a copper or lead probe, if there is a bend in the corn, so you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the anus, you should then insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe has pierced through your finger, clear and without feeling any skin or flesh between your finger and the probe, then know for certain that it is perforated.  Do not exhaust yourself on it, as there is no cure, as we have said.
Some claim it heals in rare cases.  A treatment that may be helpful is to heat a thin cauterizing iron to a size appropriate for the corn, as previously mentioned, and insert it into the corn until it reaches near the anus.  Then repeat this two or three times.
So that you know that all the excess congealed flesh, similar to a bird's feather tube, has been burned.  Then treat it with a thread soaked in clarified butter until that burned flesh is removed.  Then treat it with healing ointments, if it heals.
Otherwise, it will not heal with any other treatment.  If you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh or membrane between it and the probe, and you see the corn below the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the corn. As you make the incision with the probe inside the corn, continue until the incision reaches where the tip of the probe ends, and the probe is removed and falls out.  Then clean out that congealed flesh, which resembles a bird's feather tube, leaving nothing of it behind.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals.  If blood prevents you from completing your work of removing that flesh, the best and quickest remedy is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two beneficial outcomes: burning away the excess flesh.
And cut away the blood and dry the moisture, then treat it with a thread soaked in clarified butter or sulfur powdered with oil until the area becomes purulent and that burned flesh is expelled in the pus.  Then treat the wound at that point with healing ointments that promote the growth of firm flesh.
These are the remedies that have a binding and drying effect.  If it is repaired, it has healed, and there is no need to return. If the corn has reached the depths of the rectum and is distant from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and search with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it there is a membrane or flesh, and it is close to passing through, then there is no work to be done other than by way of hope and expectation.  That is to say, you must use one of three methods: either cauterize it, as we have said.
Or to cut it until you reach its bottom to enable the thread and treatment to reach it closely. Do not proceed with the cutting too far, so as not to sever the surrounding muscle of the rectum and cause involuntary bowel movements. Then treat it as we have mentioned, for perhaps it will heal, as we have said.
Or to pierce that membrane and puncture it after cutting it close to the rectum, as we said, either with a probe or another sharp-pointed instrument, and then cleanse all the accumulated flesh within the cut as far as possible.
Then close the entire wound, including the cut and the upper opening, until it seals, and the corn remains open within the rectum, making it easier on the patient.  The corn may perforate in this manner.
If, when introducing the probe into the fissure, it is in a side of the rectum, near the surface of the body, along with the skin and the surrounding rectum, then take a probe with a pierced tip, like a shoemaker's awl, in this way:
Then insert a twisted thread, of five threads or so, into it; and introduce the probe with the thread into the fissure until you reach its bottom. If it is perforated along the edge of the rectum from the inside, close by.
Then remove the thread from that perforation by inserting your finger into the rectum and pulling out the end of the thread. Bring both ends together and tie them tightly. Leave it for a day or two. Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads breaks and falls off. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the fissure is not perforated, then perforate it as best as you can, unless it is very deep.  Do not do this to the muscle lest you sever it. Then proceed as we described until it heals.
The shape of the pointed probe used to divide the fissures should have a very sharp curve on one side and a blunt one on the other, so as not to cut what is unnecessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
Concerning hemorrhoids that bleed, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids come in two types: either they are within the anus, resembling red swellings or like grapes, sometimes small, sometimes large, and blood constantly flows from them.
They may be single or multiple, and they may be outside the anus, on its edges.  However, those outside the anus are usually less moist; a yellowish liquid or a little blood flows from them constantly.  They are the same color as the skin.
The treatment for those inside the anus is to have the patient defecate and strain until the anus is stretched and the polyps are exposed. Then quickly grasp them with forceps or your fingernail and cut them at their base. If forceps do not hold them because of their moisture and relaxation,
Then, grip them with a rough cloth or a piece of sharpened wood or pull them out with your fingers and cut them.  Then, after cutting, sprinkle some sharp powders on them to act as cauterization, or cauterize them as described in the section on cauterization.
Then treat them with further remedies until healed. If the anus does not yield, give the patient an enema with a little bit of stinging to cleanse what is in the anus and to cause it to come out quickly when the patient strains.  As for polyps outside the anus, their treatment is easy and simple.
It is to grasp them with your fingernail or forceps, cut them, and then treat them with the methods mentioned until they heal.  If one is averse to cutting with iron, one should use a thread-like needle.
Then pull the wart upward and pierce it with the needle at its base from the other side. Tie the ends of the thread below the needle, which is positioned across it, and firmly pull the wart. Then tie the thread and remove the needle.  Do this with all the warts, leaving one unpierced.
So that some blood may flow from it.  Then, place a cloth soaked in rose or quince oil on the anus, and tell the patient to remain still.  Leave it until it falls off.  Once the warts fall off, treat them with ointments and other remedies until healed.
Concerning the treatment of fissures, they frequently arise from dryness of the stool and obstruction of the body. If prolonged and no treatment is effective, it is necessary to scrape them with a scalpel or your fingernail until they become moist and the upper crust that prevents healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more forcefully than before until it bleeds and peels. Then treat it; it will heal.
The Eighty-second Chapter.
On the treatment of ingrown nails, both ingrown and non-ingrown, dried warts, and the nits.
The ingrown nail is a round, body-colored swelling resembling a nail head, found throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and toes. Pain arises from it during walking.
One should slit the surrounding tissue of the nail, and remove it from its root using a lancet or forceps. Then treat it.  Alternatively, one can cauterize it as described in the relevant section. As for dried warts, each one is a small, rough, hardened, rounded protuberance on the body's surface.
They often appear on children's hands. Treatment involves stretching the wart, cutting or piercing it with silk thread or hair until it falls off.  Alternatively, it can be cauterized with fire or a potent medicine as previously mentioned.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts can be removed with the instrument I described in the treatment of nits.  Nits are also small, hardened, thick protuberances on the body's surface, penetrating deeply.  If they are exposed to cold, they cause a sensation similar to stinging from ants.
They occur throughout the body, most often on the hands.  Treatment involves taking a strong goose or eagle feather quill, forming a tube around the nit, then turning the tube with your hand until it splits the surrounding tissue.
And inserting it into the flesh, the nit is removed from its root. Alternatively, you can do this with a brass or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be solid, twisted, and easy for the fingers to grip and turn. Alternatively, cauterize the nit as previously described in the section on cauterizing warts.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On the types of instruments used for injections in cases of rectal disorders, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, tin, or hollow or hammered copper.  Small and large versions of this instrument may be made, depending on the users' needs. So, those used for treating young children, or those with narrow or sore rectums, should have very thin syringes.  Image of a large syringe:.
Its upper funnel, where the bag is attached, should be wide, with a partition where the bag is tied as seen.  The lower tip, which enters the rectum, should be very smooth and seamless.  On one side, there are two small holes, and on the other side, a single hole.
The hole's width should be about the thickness of a reed or slightly thicker. The bag, in which the medicines are placed, is made from the bladder of an animal or the skin of a sheep, and shaped like a small tray, with a diameter of one span.
Many holes are pierced along its perimeter, with the holes being approximately a finger's width apart.  Then, strong thread made of doubled ten-thread yarn or similar is threaded through these holes, thereby gathering the bag like a small tray. When the medicine is placed inside, the syringe head should be firmly tightened between the stones.  Then, it is injected.  This is an image of the bag's circular shape and the holes.
How to inject with it: Fill the skin or bladder with the medicine, warmed to a temperature tolerable by the patient. Be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.  Then, the patient lies on their back with their legs raised.
A mat or blanket is placed underneath.  Then, the tip of the syringe is lubricated with ointment, egg white, or licorice saliva, or similar.  Gently insert it into the rectum.  The attendant holding the bag should squeeze it firmly with both hands until all the contents of the syringe are emptied into the bowels.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient lowers their legs.  If the medicine is urging expulsion, let the patient retain it as much as possible.  If the patient sleeps with it all night, it will be more effective in treating the condition.  If injected for diarrhea and expulsion is desired quickly, repeat the enema until the patient recovers.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the syringe should be very gentle and smooth, unlike the syringes used for children.  This is a picture of a gentle syringe, with its skin taut.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On the treatment of wounds.
I have previously mentioned in the division of treatment similar to cauterizations, and here I am describing their treatment by hand and medicines completely.  I say that wounds differ according to the thing causing the wound and the location of the wound.
The things causing wounds are numerous, such as striking with a stone, cutting with a sword or knife, stabbing with a spear, stick, or arrow, and similar things.
As for wounds according to locations on the body, they are like wounds on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and other organs.
I will describe the treatment of some wounds to make them a model and rule for all wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds specifically, because complex wounds will be mentioned in the first chapter of the third book of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head, and it is not a bone fracture, I examine it. If it is from a blow with a stone or similar, and only the skin has been bruised, and the wound is large, and I fear that the patient will develop a hot swelling, then quickly incise the scalp at the location. Do not delay this, and let blood out according to his strength, if there is no impediment to bloodletting, especially if the blood coming from the wound is slight.  Let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the injured area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinks, and thick foods. If there is an impediment to bloodletting, then he should reduce his food intake, avoid meat and drinks, and if a hot swelling occurs, apply a cotton pad dipped in rose oil, alone or mixed with a drink that has a binding quality, to the wound.
If you fear a hot swelling, apply to the wound, if it is fresh and still has its blood and the air has not altered it (as I will describe to you shortly), and if the air has altered it, apply some ointments.
Until the pus comes out, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large, and caused by a sword cut or similar, and its edges have not joined, bring them together with sutures as described in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin has exposed the bone and is hanging, and its attachment is only in a slight band, cut it and then treat the wound with remedies that will create strong flesh in place of the skin.  Tell the patient to eat strengthening foods such as heads and the like.
If a foulness occurs in the skin, but it is not adhering to the bone, cut away all the foulness and throw it away, then treat it.  If in these wounds there is an artery or vein that bleeds and the bleeding is not stopped by remedies, look for the artery.
If you find it, and it does not stop bleeding, sever it with a scalpel and tie it off.  If the situation demands it, and what we have mentioned is not helpful, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and simple, their treatment is easy and straightforward; it is sufficient to sprinkle this powder over them while they are still bloody.
Before the air changes it, the prescription for the powder is: one part frankincense, two parts myrrh, and three parts slaked or unslaked lime. Grind all of them, sift them, and fill the wound with it.  Bind it firmly so it adheres well.
It will clot with blood, then bind it from above with bandages.  Leave it undisturbed as long as it does not swell, for the remedy will not come off until it heals.  Lime alone may accomplish this if frankincense and myrrh are unavailable.
This action can also be done with lime in many large wounds, if its application is skillful and firm, and the wounds are fresh and bloody, provided the air has not altered them. However, if the air has altered them somewhat, especially if it is summer.
Applying this powder is not the only treatment; you should treat it as described previously by applying some ripening ointments or a barley flour paste made with water and honey, until the pus comes out.  Then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
If a minor fracture of the bone occurs in the wound, extract it with a cloth.  The treatment for head fractures has been mentioned elsewhere; the treatment for neck wounds is no different from that of simple head wounds.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, there is no remedy except to avoid treatment with strongly astringent substances like verdigris, alum, and the like, as these harm the nerve and cause spasms.
Nothing cold should be applied to the wound at all, because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  Use gentle remedies like moistened rose water or rose oil and ceruse, which are repeatedly washed with fresh water.
If also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner, and with moist ointments, if the wound is large, use sutures or bring the edges together with bandages, then treat until healed.
If the wound has a recess and pus has collected in the bottom, drain it from the lowest point.  If an artery has been severed and blood is flowing from the wound, either ligate it, tie it off, or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx, but the vessels are intact, bring the skin edges together with sutures along the larynx's cartilage. Do not touch the larynx itself, but smooth it and restore it to its natural shape, then tie it tightly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have a tightening and drying effect until healed. If the wound has affected the bone, explore it. If there are bone fragments, remove them all or as many as possible. If not possible, leave it until the wound becomes necrotic and heals, making removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powder we described. If bone in the wound has become necrotic and pus-filled, treat it as described in the section on chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see a recess, observe if air emerges when the patient breathes. If so, know that it is a fatal wound.  If there is no recess and the wound is fresh and bloody, do not apply the powder initially nor tightly close it, lest blood becomes trapped in the recess.
This will cause it to return to the heart and kill the patient.  Instead, apply a drawing ointment twice daily. If ointment is unavailable, place a moist linen compress in the wound to absorb the moisture, and position the patient to sleep with the wound uppermost.
To drain any accumulated matter. If three days or more have passed and the patient shows no spasms, abnormal heartbeats, or shortness of breath, and their general condition is otherwise good, consider the wound healthy. Treat it with bandages and other healing measures until healed.
If healing and the flow of air are continually prevented, know that it has become pus-filled. Treat it according to that section.  The patient should lie on their wound to drain any collected matter.  If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it as previously described with sutures.
If large, use the powder; if small, use the powder. If the wound has affected the bone and detached fragments, investigate the wound and promptly remove any loose fragments. If they are not loose, leave them until later.
If the wound becomes foul, this will make the fragments easier to remove.  The treatment for other wounds occurring in other parts of the body is the same as what has been mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Five.
On wounds of the abdomen, bowel protrusions, and their suture.
A wound in the abdomen may be large, small, or medium-sized.  A large wound may expose one or several intestines, making their replacement and suture more difficult and challenging.
Even a small wound may present difficulties in another way, making replacement even more challenging.  If the intestines are not promptly returned to their proper position, they will swell and thicken, making replacement difficult.  Therefore, the best type of wound is a medium-sized one, because it does not present the same difficulty in returning the intestines as do the other two types.
Know that there are four types of sutures for the abdomen. Two types are general-purpose, suitable for abdominal sutures and for suturing other bodily wounds. The other two are specialized, applicable to other wounds, but are particularly suited for abdominal sutures.
I will describe them to you one by one with explanation.  If the wound is small and something from the intestines has protruded, and its return is difficult, this is due to one of two reasons: either the smallness of the wound, as we mentioned, or because the intestines have been inflated by exposure to cold air.
If this is the case, it is necessary to warm them by dipping a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water, alone or with added boiled thyme, saffron, and anise, and applying it to the intestines until the swelling subsides.
A drink with astringent properties may also be used, and is better than water alone for dissolving the swelling. If you see the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestines with water that has been boiled with hyssop or cumin, as this will make their return easier.
If its return is still impossible after this treatment, make a small incision in the wound with the instrument used for lancing boils. The initial treatment, if successful, is preferable to incision, but incision is resorted to only as a last resort when all else fails.  This is a depiction of the instrument.
Its one curved side is limited, and the other is unlimited. The thin end is not as sharp as a scalpel, but slightly blunt.  It is a small, lance-like instrument, as you see.  If the wound is wide and the intestines have entered, it is necessary to return them to their natural shape.
The specific location of the treatment, if achievable, is best.  However, if the wound is wide and in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs elevated higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, the head and chest should be raised higher than the lower body.
Likewise, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to keep the side with the injury elevated higher than the other side.  This should be used in major and medium-sized surgeries.
For minor surgeries, position it as best you can. Then, summon a helpful assistant who will hold the entire wound in their hands, bring the edges together, and then reveal it gradually to the surgeon who will stitch it.
This is the basic method of stitching: take a needle or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Leave a space the width of a little finger on the edges of the wound and pierce one needle through the skin edges without threading a suture. Piercing it through the lining of the skin (the peritoneum) from inside, so that you have grasped both edges of the skin and of the peritoneum - four layers.  Then, tighten a doubled thread around the needle repeatedly from both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly joined.
Then leave a space equal to the width of a finger and insert another needle, threading it with the suture as you did with the first. Continue this with the necessary needles until the whole wound is closed.  Use needles of medium thickness.
Because very thin needles quickly cut through the flesh, and very thick needles have difficulty penetrating the skin, the needles should be of medium thickness.  The needle should not be inserted too close to the edge of the skin.
So that the flesh does not tear quickly and the wound does not open before it closes.  Nor should the stitches be too far apart, so that the wound does not prevent healing. Then cut the ends of the needles so they will not injure the patient while he sleeps, and attach pieces of linen to each end of the needles.
Leave it until you know the wound has healed. This type of suturing with needles is best suited for small surgical procedures, because one or two needles, or so, may suffice for stitching.
As for the method of suturing for a general wound, gather the four edges—that is, the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum—at once with a needle carrying a moderately thin and thick suture thread.  Then, after the needle has passed through these four edges, return the needle from the side from which you began.
So that the thread will be interwoven from above the wound, resulting in stitching similar to the stitching used to secure sacks, and keep a distance between each stitch equal to the thickness of a little finger.  This type of stitching is known by everyone.
With this method of stitching, a surgeon treated a man who had a knife wound in his abdomen. The surgical opening was more than a span, and about two spans of the midgut had been exposed. The opening was in the middle of the abdomen.
He repositioned the intestines after they had protruded from the wound for twenty-four hours.  The wound healed in about fifteen days, and he was treated until he was completely recovered.  He lived many years afterward, functioning normally in all respects.  The physicians had previously judged that he would not recover at all.
Surprisingly, I did not treat it with any ointment, as I was in a location with no available medications. I would place used cotton on the wound twice a day, and ensure it was washed with honey water until it healed.
Concerning one of the specific types of stitching, I mention it based on Galen's text: Begin the stitching from the skin, inserting the needle from outside to inside.  Once the needle has penetrated the skin and the muscle extending in a straight line along the entire length of the abdomen...
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side where I inserted the needle, and I then inserted the needle into its other edge from inside to outside at the other edge of the diaphragm.  If you have penetrated it, penetrate it again in this same edge of the diaphragm from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and insert the needle into its other edge from inside to outside.  And as you insert it into the peritoneum, insert it also into the edge of the diaphragm on its side, until you have penetrated them all.
Then, beginning from this same side, draw a line along the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, bringing the needle out from the skin near it, then returning it to that skin, and drawing a line along the edge of the peritoneum on the other side with this edge of the diaphragm.
Bring it out of the skin on its side, and do this again and again, until you stitch the entire wound in this manner.  The overall description of this stitching is that it is like the stitching of furriers for furs.
By stitching the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leaving it once from the other side until it is emptied; and as for the second type of special stitching that Galen also mentioned, this is his account verbatim.
It is to stitch it in the manner that some healers stitch, by joining each part to its corresponding part naturally, bringing the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the diaphragm to its other edge, which is as I describe to you.
The needle should be inserted into the edge of the diaphragm near you, from the outside, penetrating it to the inside, leaving the edges of the peritoneum, then return the needle and penetrate it from the outside to the inside of both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again.
And penetrate it from inside to outside into the other edge of the diaphragm on the opposite side.  This type of stitching is better than the common, easy stitching previously mentioned. This is Galen's account verbatim. Some have said that stitching the abdomen with two needles may be appropriate. That is, to insert a single thread into them, starting by inserting the needle from your side and penetrating it to the other side, and inserting the other needle from the opposite side, penetrating it from your side, according to the stitching of the leatherworkers, just the same.
Know that if the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, its stitching is more difficult than in other abdominal locations.  As for treating the wound, its treatment is like that of other wounds.  If you apprehend the wound fresh with its blood before the air alters it, replace the intestines, stitch and secure it, then apply the medicated dressings.
If the wound has been altered by the air, apply some of the ointments available to you until it suppurates, the sutures fall out, and the peritoneum and diaphragm adhere.  Then treat it as you would any other wound until it heals.
If you do not have medications, apply to it, from the beginning of suppuration, old cotton and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals.  If the wound is uncomplicated, you do not need further treatment in most cases. However, if you fear that the wound may cause pain in the major organs.
Therefore, you should dip soft wool in lukewarm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpit.  If he feels pain or foul odor in his intestines, which often occurs, inject him with a lukewarm, astringent black drink.
Especially if the foul odor has reached the intestines and the wound has pierced into its cavity. Know that the thicker parts of the intestines heal more easily, and the thinner parts heal more difficultly.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not heal from any wound that occurs in it at all, due to the abundance of large vessels within it, the thinness of its substance, and its proximity to the nerves.  However, if what has protruded from the wound is the omentum and you encountered it promptly, return it according to how you would return the intestine itself.
If it has been some time and it has turned green or black, you should tie a thread above the blackened area to prevent bleeding, because there are veins and arteries in the omentum, and then cut what is below that ligament.
And you should leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside of it, so it is easy for you to pull it and remove it when the omentum falls away and the wound becomes suppurated.  This describes a wound that occurs in the intestines. Now, if a perforation occurs in the intestines and is small...
It may sometimes be possible to repair it in some people, as I have seen a person who had a spear wound in their abdomen, and the wound was to the right of the stomach.  The wound became chronic, and pus, stool, and air were coming from it. I treated it, though I did not expect it to heal. I continued to treat him until he healed and the area joined.  When I saw that the area had healed, I feared that the patient might suffer a bad event in his abdomen.  But no such bad event occurred, and he remained in the best possible condition. Indeed, he ate, drank, had intercourse, bathed, and exercised, assisted.  Some experienced individuals have said that if a wound occurs in the intestines and is small, it should be treated in this way.
This is done by taking large ants, then bringing the edges of the wound together.  One ant, its mouth open, is placed on the wound edges.  When it bites down and closes its mouth, the head is cut off; then it sticks and does not separate.  Then another ant is placed near the first.
Continue doing this with several ants, according to the size of the wound, then close and stitch the wound.  Those heads remain adhered to the intestines until the intestines heal and recover, and no ailment occurs to the patient.  The intestines can also be sutured with fine thread.
The method of extracting the animal adhesive from the Egyptian reed is to take the end of this thread from the reed and pull it out.  Then, a thin, twisted linen thread is tied to its end, and this thread is inserted into the needle. The Egyptian reed thread is used to suture the intestines, and then the intestines are returned to the cavity.  This method of suturing with ants and the Egyptian reed is based on hope and expectation.
However, if the tear is large and extensive, especially in one of the small intestines, there is no remedy, and no cure is possible.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
On the treatment of the common cold and the nasal obstruction.
Know that any wound or swelling that becomes chronic, transforms into an ulcer, does not heal, and constantly discharges pus, is generally called a fistula (nasoor) in any part of the body; and we call it a cold (zukam) or fistula.
It is, in reality, a hard, compacted, white mass without pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather.  Sometimes it is moist, constantly discharging pus, and the moisture may sometimes stop.  This moisture may be abundant, small in amount, thick, or thin.
I have previously stated in the classification of surgical conditions that any ulcer that does not heal, in which flesh does not grow, is due to one of nine causes. One of these is either a deficiency of blood in the body, or poor quality of the blood, or because of hard flesh inside and on the edges that prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because the ulcer is severely affected by dirt and impurities, or because the ulcer itself is foul and the material it discharges is of poor quality, or because the treatment is not appropriate, or because there is a general epidemic in the city or a specific condition affecting the city.
Or because the ulcer is severely affected by dirt and impurities, or because the ulcer itself is foul and the material it discharges is of poor quality, or because the treatment is not appropriate, or because there is a general epidemic in the city or a specific condition affecting the city. As has been shown in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is slow, naturally, or because it involves one or several bones.  All these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medications have been described in the classification.
Here, I must inform you about the treatment of polyps and colds that are treated manually. Know that polyps can occur in all parts of the body, some leading to large veins, arteries, or nerves.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of many components, such as the hand or foot, and some lead close to a major organ or similar.
Those polyps of this type are difficult to cure, do not respond to treatment, and are a source of effort and ignorance.  If some polyps do not reach these aforementioned locations, there is hope and expectation for their treatment.
If you wish to ascertain this, take a probe of copper or iron.  If the polyp is straight, probe it with this. If the polyp has a curve, probe it with a thin lead probe, for lead is pliable.
If it (the probe) slips when entering and curves towards the curve, and if the polyp has many openings which you cannot determine with a probe, inject one opening with liquid. The liquid will flow to the other openings.
Then, investigate in any way possible to determine if there is bone or nerve, whether the polyp's base is distant or close, or whether it is a single polyp with multiple openings.  Ascertain all of this to the best of your ability by investigating and noting the pain when touching the area.
And similar to these are the signs and causes of the tumor.  If you have ascertained all this accurately, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  If...
If the polyp is visible, close to the surface, or in a healthy area distant from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the locations I have described, then dissect the polyp as described above and remove any hardened, decayed, or excessive flesh within.
And treat any flesh or wart that grows in its mouth until it heals. If the polyp is deep-seated and in a straight line, you should dissect it deeply as possible and then cleanse it of all its decayed flesh.
Then, use tightly wound, sharp medicated dressings and insert them into the polyp's base, which you locate with an instrument. Repeat this several times, so that the sharp medication eats away any remaining corruption in the polyp's base.
Then treat it with ointments that promote healthy tissue growth until it heals.  If it doesn't heal that way, cauterize it as described before. If the cause of the polyp is bone, and you are certain of that, probe and dissect it as I have instructed, unless something prevents you from doing so.
If it is a vein, nerve, or a major organ, as we mentioned, and you expose the bone with some corruption and blackness, remove the corruption, then treat it with something that promotes healing until it heals.  If it doesn't heal and the pus continues, as it was doing before.
Know that you have not completely removed all the corruption.  Expose all of it again, thoroughly scrape it, and cleanse it to the best of your ability, then treat it with a healing agent.  If it heals, well and good.  Otherwise, know that the corruption is deeply hidden.  Expose all of it again.
Thoroughly scrape and cleanse it to the best of your ability.  If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, remove it with delicate forceps suitable for the task.
If there are many bones, thoroughly remove them all.  Do not leave any behind.  If any ligaments obstruct their removal, and there is no danger in cutting them, cut those ligaments and the flesh, if present, and free the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones immediately, apply something to fester the wound and leave it for several days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones fester.  Protect the wound, so the opening you created during your procedure does not close.
If you fear that, apply a cotton swab soaked in crushed sulfur with oil, or a cotton swab dipped in Egyptian ointment, or one of the green ointments.  If these ligaments fester and the bones become loose, they will be easily removed and pulled out.
If it is a single large bone, like the tibia or femur, and only its surface has become corrupted, thoroughly scrape it until the blackness and corruption disappear, then close the wound.  If a large portion has become corrupted and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, it must be opened.
Cut it all the way to where the corruption ends, then treat it until it heals.  I will tell you of a case that happened to a young man in his leg to make it an example and help you in your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years old, he had pain in his leg, from a cause originating inside the body, until the materials reached the leg and a large swelling formed.  There was no external cause.  Then, time passed with the doctors' errors, until the swelling opened.
A great deal of material flowed from it, and he was poorly treated, until the leg became affected and had many openings, all emitting pus and bodily moisture. A group of doctors treated him for about two years, but none of them were skilled surgeons.
Until they came to me.  I saw his leg, and the materials flowed from those openings in a great stream. The man's body had wasted away, and his complexion had turned yellow. I inserted a probe into one of those openings, which led to the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
I found that some of them led to others from all sides of the leg.  I then opened one of those openings, exposing some of the bone.  I found it corrupted, decayed, blackened, putrid, and perforated, until the perforation reached the marrow. I reported what I discovered.
I removed the corrupted bone, and I believe that there was nothing but that corruption which I removed and reported. Then, I applied healing medicines to the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.  I then exposed the bone again, beyond the first exposure.
I found the corruption connected to the bone.  I reported what I saw of that corruption as well.  Then I attempted to heal it, but it did not heal or join together.  I exposed it again, and I continued to cut the bone piece by piece, trying to heal it, but it would not heal, until I removed approximately a span of bone.
I removed it with its marrow, then healed it with medicines, and it healed quickly and recovered.  This repetition of work and incision was necessary due to the patient's weakness and low tolerance, and my fear for his death, because he was experiencing fainting spells frequently due to excessive vomiting.  He recovered completely.
Solid flesh grew in place of the bone, and his condition improved in his body. His strength returned and he managed his affairs. He experienced no impediment to his walking that harmed him at all.  If a bone in any part of the body has broken.
Therefore, it is necessary to expose it in this manner: take a bandage and tighten it at the end of the protruding bone, and instruct someone to stretch it upwards.  Then, make another bandage thicker than the first.
Then, tie it to the flesh under the bone and stretch its end so the flesh pulls downwards.  You should expose the flesh at the location you wish to expose so the saw does not injure the flesh. Place a plank or board under the bone from below, securely.
Because if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted bone. The incision should be slightly above the affected area, so that there is no corruption within the bone cavity which will not appear on the surface, requiring a further incision.
If the bone is corrupted but not protruding, instead connected but with corruption in the middle or part of it, expose the flesh from all sides. Then place the board underneath, and then make the incision from the initial side where the corruption is.
Once you have finished the incision, make another incision on the opposite side.  Let the incision be a little distance from the corruption, as we stated. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself and incise the bone where it joins on the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but excision. If the corruption is in the hand or foot, treatment is extremely difficult, but you should scrape away the corruption however it appears to you and remove and clean it however you can.
By whatever method you can manage, provided no vein or nerve is encountered.  Know that the saws and instruments for cutting these bones vary according to the position of the bones, their thickness, thinness, size, strength and fragility. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare a tool suited to each type of work.

Chapter Eighty-seven.
In cutting off limbs and spreading bones.
The limbs may become putrid either from an external cause or from an internal cause.  If you treat this decay with medicinal remedies and the treatment proves ineffective, and you see the decay spreading in the limb and nothing can stop it,
Then you should cut off that limb where the decay has reached, so that the patient is saved from death or a great harm that is greater than losing the limb.  A sign that this has happened to a person is that the limb becomes dark, so much so that you think fire has burned it.
Or it will rot after darkening, so that the rot spreads to the limb's surrounding tissue and takes hold of the whole body.  So be quick to cut it off.  Likewise, if the cause of the decay is from a sting of certain venomous creatures, such as the sea scorpion, snake, or centipede, and similar creatures.
If the decay or the sting is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger and do not delay the decay from spreading to the hand.  Likewise, if it has reached the hand, cut it off at the base of the forearm, and do not delay the decay from spreading until it reaches the forearm.
Likewise, if it has reached the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow joint.  If the decay progresses and you see it spreading towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for that would mean the patient's death. Use other treatments to the extent of your ability.
Likewise, do the same with the leg. If decay reaches the toe, cut off the toe at one of the phalanges. If decay reaches the foot, cut off the entire foot. If it ascends to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If the decay has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave it and let the patient submit to death.  The method for cutting or dissecting the limb is to tie a bandage at the bottom of the place you wish to cut and tie another bandage above the place.
The servant of one bandage pulls downwards, and the servant of the other bandage pulls upwards. You, yourself, separate the flesh between the bandages with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is exposed. Then it is cut or dissected.  You should place linen cloths on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct place, causing the patient excessive pain and a hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, cauterize the place quickly, or apply some blood-stopping astringents, then return to your treatment until it is finished.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I am telling you about an example of a case in a man's leg, with the same condition I am describing: a blackening with a burning sensation like fire occurred in his leg.
This decay first appeared in his finger, until the man's entire body was affected.  The man despaired when he saw the decay spreading through the limb, and because of the severe pain and burning, he had it amputated at the joint and recovered.
After a long time, the same decay appeared in his index finger.  He came to me, and I applied the remedies I had on hand to his hand after cleansing his body, but the decay did not cease and began to spread to the other finger.
Until the decay spread to his entire hand, and he asked me to amputate it. I refused, hoping to prevent the decay, and also fearing that amputation would cause his death, as his strength was failing.  When he lost hope in me, he returned to his home.
I heard later that he eventually severed his entire hand and recovered.  I have related this story to be of help in understanding this type of illness and as an example to be followed.
Eighty-eighth Chapter.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject medications into them.
If a fleshy tumor forms in any limb and persists for a considerable period, then bursts or gradually resolves, emptying all the matter within it, leaving the site empty, like a container.
The skin over it, like a rag, becomes thin, and the damage is not severe enough to affect bone, nerve, or ligament.  Therefore, it is called an abscess, and not a fistula, unless the duration is so prolonged that the damage affects one of these tissues.
Then it is called a fistula or ulcer, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin, especially if it has become thin and like a rag, and you are sure it does not adhere to the site due to its corruption.
If you suspect the skin might still adhere, and the corruption hasn't reached that level, and if there's thick flesh on the skin, treat it with injections.  Observe the abscess. If it is large, and the pus it produces has a foul odor, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and alum, in equal parts, mixed in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens and resembles the consistency of honey.  Then take what you need, and dilute it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess.  Close its opening and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then extract it by squeezing. Do this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.  You may also inject it with ash water, if you do not have this medicine.  This is done by taking the ashes of olive wood or oak wood, and pouring water over it.
Then strain it and inject it until you are certain the abscess has been washed out. If the patient does not respond to the Egyptian medicine or ash water, inject it with water and honey mixed with some powdered alum.
Or inject it with honey and syrup mixed together, because honey cleanses and washes, while syrup adheres to the abscess, especially if the syrup is somewhat astringent and drying. If you do this repeatedly and are certain the abscess has been cleared of its corruption, then inject it with something that promotes flesh growth.
For example, take some palm ointment and dissolve it in rose oil and a binding syrup, or inject it with other medicines and ointments whose properties we have described in the chapter on ointments. If the opening of the abscess is narrow and does not accommodate the injecting tube.
If it is narrow, widen it slightly with a metal instrument, or place a soaked wick in Egyptian or Syrian ointment there until it widens. Similarly, if the opening is wide, bring the edges together by stitching, leaving enough room for the injecting tube without more.
Also, if the opening from which pus is draining is high up, cut it at the lowest point so that the pus drains downward, because if pus is accumulated in the depths of the abscess, it prevents the growth of flesh there.
If you cannot cut the abscess toward the bottom as desired, then apply pressure to the limb so that the pus drains easily as you find suitable, and ensure that no pus remains trapped within it at all.
As for the adhesive medications that are applied to the abscess from the outside and how to apply them, take one of the adhesive ointments that matches what you want and spread it on a linen cloth, ensuring the cloth covers the entire abscess. Then, using scissors, cut an opening slightly wider than the opening of the abscess.  Spread some soft ointment onto another piece of cloth, sized to fit the opening, and apply it, keeping it a distance from the abscess opening so the pus can easily drain.  Do not remove the larger cloth until later.
For several days, the one on the abscess opening should be removed at all times to allow the accumulated pus to drain, and to see if the discharged pus is copious or scant, or ripe or unripe.
Also, check the site of the abscess itself: does the patient feel pain, or not?  Is there swelling, or not?  If your treatment continues in this way, and you see the material that is discharged being meager at the abscess opening,
Know then that the abscess has adhered, or is close to adhering.  In that case, treat it again the next day or on the third day.  When you have treated the wound, change the cloths and renew the ointment. If after a long time, thin pus comes out of the abscess, do not despair of its adherence.
For healing is near, because some people have slow flesh growth in their wounds, while others have the opposite. If, after many days, unripe pus comes out of the abscess, know that it has not adhered.  If the abscess adheres slowly,
Increase the use of drying medications, and let them be moist in consistency and dry in strength, such as the pistachio ointment if more colocynth has been added.  The most effective treatment is using pistachio ointment.
It is treated with rose oil and sprinkled with moderate-strength old wine.  Then mix it and use it.  You can do the same thing if you do not have pistachio ointment.  Honey, if cooked until it becomes thick, can be used, or you can take myrrh, aloes, and kender, powder all of them and sprinkle them over the honey while it is on the fire. Then apply it to a cloth and bind it to the abscess, or apply the thickened cooked honey, sprinkle the medicines over it, place the cloth on it, and bind it.
Long-leaved zeraun, iris root,  chrysanthemum flour, and rue, either individually or mixed, can be used.  Grind and sift them, sprinkle them over the honey I described, and use it.  If the abscess persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
If it has affected the bones, or other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it with the treatment of the fistula as described previously.
Chapter Eighty-nine.
Treating the hangnail, injured fingernail, removal of excess finger, and separation of fused fingers.
A hangnail is excess flesh that grows under the fingernail of the hand or foot, and sometimes other fingers. If it persists and is neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it may fester and rot, even eating away the base of the nail, and possibly corrupting it entirely, and the rot may reach the bone, so it has a foul odor, and the end of the finger becomes wide and dark in color. If you treat it by the methods we described in the section and it does not succeed,
then you should cut away with the iron all the excess that grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound after that.  Cauterization is very beneficial in this case.  If the bone is sound and the nail is also sound,
If the edge of the nail has pressed the flesh inward, causing pain and irritation, then a thin strip should be placed under the edge of the nail that is irritating the flesh, raising it up, and that flesh should be gently cut away.
and apply to the remaining flesh the burning, corrosive medicines, until all of it is gone. Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the rot has affected the bone, then you must cut away and remove that bone, for it will never heal as long as there is rotten bone within.
If you see decay and rot spreading through the finger, amputate it at one of the phalanges, as previously described, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or injury and severe pain occurs, you should first bleed the affected area.
Then, using a sharp scalpel, split the nail with a slanted cut from top to bottom. Be careful not to reach the flesh beneath the nail with the split, as this will cause intense pain in the affected area and lead to the growth of excess flesh in that spot.  Then treat the area with something to alleviate the pain.
As for the extra finger that sometimes grows on some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may contain bone in some parts, or it may contain a nail;  and some of it may grow from the base of the joint of some fingers.
Or it may grow on some of the finger's phalanges.  Those that grow on some of the finger's phalanges do not move, and those that grow near the finger's joint may move.  If it is purely fleshy, cutting it is easy; cut it at its base with a wide scalpel.
However, if it grows from the base of the joint, treatment is difficult, and you should avoid cutting it.  As for the one that grows on the finger at one of the phalanges, it should first be cut flesh-wise, roundly towards the bone, then the bone should be chipped away with one of those saws appropriate for that.
Then treat it until it heals.  As for the adhesion that sometimes occurs between fingers, this often happens and is sometimes present from birth, or arises from a healed wound or burn, or similar things. It is necessary to split this adhesion.
So that the fingers return to their natural position.  Then, place between them a thin piece of linen soaked in rose oil, so that they do not rapidly fuse; or separate them with, or place between them, a thin sheet of lead until the area heals properly.
Likewise, if adhesion occurs between some fingers and the palm, split that adhesion as is suitable and improves the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On the severing of sinews.
Sinews are twisted, thick veins filled with dark, black fluid, found in most parts of the body; however, they occur most often in the legs, especially in the legs of those who wear heavy packs, carriers, and porters.  First, one should use vigorous rubbing to remove the dark matter.
Then, lance the affected area with a lancet.  Treatment with iron can be of two types: one where the dark blood is incised and removed; the other where the vein is cut and the entire substance removed.  Incision should proceed, first, by soaking the leg in hot water.
Until the thick, murky blood is loosened.  Then, the leg of the afflicted person is bandaged from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage.  Then, the vein is incised in one, two, or three wide cuts.
Then, remove the dark blood with your hand, from the lower leg upward and from the upper leg downward, until you remove an amount of blood that you see as sufficient, or as much as the patient can tolerate. Then, bandage it and instruct the patient to avoid foods that produce dark matter, and repeat the emetic and bloodletting.
When the veins become filled again and harm the patient,  as for its incision, it should be done as follows: Shave the leg of the patient if there is a lot of hair. Then, the patient should enter a hot bath and soak the leg in hot water until the skin is red and sweats or endure strenuous exercise.
If a bath is not available, heat the affected area. Then, cut the skin lengthwise opposite the vein, either near the knee or near the heel, and use hooks to open the skin and strip the vein from every side until it is palpable.
When it appears, you will see it red and distinct.  Once it is released from the skin, it looks like a tendon.  Then, insert a blunt probe underneath it, until it rises and comes out of the skin, and suspend it with a smooth, blunt hook.  Then, make another incision near that incision, about the width of three fingers.
Then, strip the skin off the vein until it is exposed. Then, lift it with the probe as you did before and suspend it with another hook as you did initially, then make another incision, or several incisions, if necessary.
Then, cut and sever the vein at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull and cut it until it emerges from the second incision. Then pull it to the incision above, and do this until you pull it out of the third incision, the highest of all the incisions, until all of it is out and then cut it.
If pulling and cutting is not effective, insert a needle with strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull. Insert the probe underneath and twist your hand in every direction until it comes out, ensuring it doesn't break, because if it breaks, it will be very difficult to remove it completely.
Leaving it untreated will cause harm to the patient.  Once you have completely removed it, place wool soaked in a drink and rose oil or oil on the wound site and treat it until healed. If the vein is twisted, contorted, or has turns in various directions.
If it is not straight, as we have said, you should cut it at each bend and twist. Then, suspend it with hooks to remove it completely.  Be careful when cutting it not to sever or injure the vein, as this will make it more difficult to remove.  Use caution in your efforts.
Image of the incision for removing the vein.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not have a bend like other hooks, nor should its tip be sharp to avoid injuring the vein.  It should be thick and smooth in its bend, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein with its thinness. Rather, it should have the advantage of thickness, as we said.
Chapter Ninety-one.
On the civil vein.
This vein originates in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and the Arab lands, and in hot, sparsely fertile countries.  It may also originate in other parts of the body besides the legs, arising from a festering wound beneath the skin.
As it occurs within the bodies of snakes, worms, the pustule of a wound, and the worms that grow between skin and flesh.  A sign that this vein is beginning to form is a severe inflammation in the leg, then the affected area swells, and the vein begins to emerge from that swollen spot.
As if the root of a plant or animal.  When its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing one to two dirhams, tied, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
Whenever something emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it.  If it grows too large, cut off a portion and wrap the remainder.  Do not cut it from its origin before it is all out, because if you cut it, it will shrink and enter the flesh, causing a tumor, festering, and a foul ulcer in the area.
Therefore, it must be treated and drawn out gradually until all of it emerges from the body, without leaving any part behind.  In some people, this vein may grow to five or ten cubits in length.
I have heard that it has been known to emerge from a man to the extent of twenty cubits. If it breaks off during your treatment, insert a reed into the hole and gently press it against the body for a long time until everything is emptied out of it and try to heal the area with remedies for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears on a joint or in the leg itself, producing many openings from which branches emerge. Treat it as described in the division, and as previously mentioned.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
Concerning the ulceration on the worms that develop under the tendon, called the cow's disease.
This disease is called "cow's disease" in some of our countries because it often affects cattle. It is a small, single worm that develops between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the body, moving up and down.
Its movement from one part of the body to another can be felt, until it pierces the skin at some point, exiting as a result of the putrefaction of some humors, as seen with worms, snakes, and the formation of squash-like lumps in the abdomen.
The danger from it is that if it crawls through the body and ascends to the head, reaching the eye, it might burst through it, causing its destruction. This is a frequent occurrence. If you wish to treat it and remove it, it must be done when it is crawling and noticeable by touch.
Therefore, one should tie tightly around the area above and below it with a strong bandage, then make an incision and remove it. If it has burrowed into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply cautery to the area with fire until it burns it out.  The greatest concern is its potential to damage the eye, as we have mentioned.
If you see it in the head near the eye, tie tightly beneath it on the forehead, then make an incision and remove it.  The patient should also be treated with laxatives to cleanse the body of bad, putrid humors and should avoid foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
On incision of the disease known as the "nafar".
This disease, called "nafar" in our country, is a pain that appears in some organs, then spreads from one organ to another.  I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a woman in a remote area who uncovered her arm, and I saw a slight swelling in the vein of the arm.  After an hour, I saw this swelling creeping up her forearm towards her shoulder as quickly as mercury.
If it moves from one place to another, the pain disappears from that spot and settles in the shoulder.  Then, after an hour, it spread throughout the rest of her body, eventually reaching the other arm.  She then recounted that, as far as I could see, the pain travelled around her entire body.
I was astonished by the speed of its transfer from one organ to another.  Before, I had only heard of this disease affecting different body parts, but I had not observed it firsthand like this in this woman.
I attributed this only to the fact that the woman was from the desert, with a lean body and exposed veins.  Therefore, this moving pain was apparent to the senses.  It should not manifest in this way in those who are well-off, with a supple and hidden venous system.
If you wish to treat it, and the patient feels the pain, if it appears visibly as we described, then quickly press upon it from above and below and cut into it so that the trapped wind comes out and the location is cleared.
If you do not see it, treat it by shaking the body and using remedies that disperse wind, such as rotten seeds and scab seeds, and similar medications.
Chapter Ninety-four.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows vary according to their types and the locations of their penetration in the body.  Their variations in types include large and small, hollow and solid glass, and those with three and four angles.
Some have tongues, others have splinters.  As for their location in the organs, they are of two types: either in major hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and similar organs. If an arrow strikes one of these organs and signs of death that I will describe to you later appear, you should avoid removing the arrow, as death will most often follow. If these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not lodged deep within the organ, remove it and treat the wound.  Signs of an arrow striking the brain, piercing the bone and wounding the meninges include severe headache, dizziness, and fainting.
