If these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not lodged deep within the organ, remove it and treat the wound.  Signs of an arrow striking the brain, piercing the bone and wounding the meninges include severe headache, dizziness, and fainting. Redness in the eyes and tongue, spasms, confusion of mind, vomiting, and possibly bleeding from the nostrils or ears.  Speech may cease, the voice may be lost, and white, porridge-like moisture, resembling the watery consistency of meat, may emerge from the wound.
If these signs appear, do not treat the patient or remove the arrow if it has not yet been removed.  As for the signs of an arrow striking the heart, if it is close to the left breast and feels as though it has lodged in something solid, not hollow...
The arrow might exhibit a pulsating motion, and black blood may flow from the wound, followed by cold extremities, cold sweat, and fainting.  Know that death is inevitable.  A sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the expulsion of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels next to the neck swell, the patient's complexion changes, breathing becomes labored, and he craves inhaled cold air.  If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be near the smaller ribs, and breathing will be labored with severe pain.
He sighs and all the muscles of the shoulders move.  If the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain follows, and blood resembling the liver in its redness emerges from the wound.  If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food may emerge from the wound.
The situation is obvious.  If the arrow strikes the abdomen and penetrates, and something from the bowels, or the intestines, or the bladder, emerges from the wound, there is no hope for treatment or arrow removal.  If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine emerges,
If something protrudes from the wound and the patient's pain intensifies, know that he is doomed.  As for the other organs, such as the face, eyes, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they are more likely to remain unharmed.
Unless the arrow struck an artery or nerve, and unless the arrow was poisoned, I will tell you something I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may use it as a guide for treatment. An arrow struck a man in the bridge of his nose, at the base of his nose.
I removed it from the other side, under the earlobe, and he recovered without any harm to his eye. I also removed another arrow for a Jew, which had struck his earlobe under the lower eyelid.  The arrow was concealed, and I only retrieved the small tip that was attached to the shaft.
It was a large arrow, from a composite bow, with square, smooth iron, and no ears.  The Jew recovered without further injury to his eye. I also removed another arrow from a Christian's throat; the arrow was Arabic.
It had two ears, and I cut between the uvula, as it had penetrated his throat. I gently worked on it until I removed it. The Christian recovered and was healed.  I also removed another arrow from a man's abdomen; we estimated he would die from it.
After about thirty days, or thereabouts, and with no change in his condition, I cut open the arrow, maneuvered it, and removed it. He recovered and suffered no ill effects. I saw a man with an arrow in his back; the wound had closed around it.
Then, seven years later, the arrow emerged from the base of his thigh. I saw a woman with an arrow in her abdomen; the wound had closed over it.  The arrow remained, and there was no change in her condition or any impairment in her normal activities.
I saw another man with an arrow in his face; the wound had closed. He experienced little pain.  Many such cases exist. I removed an arrow from a Sultan's commander; it was lodged in the middle of his nose, slightly deviating to the right.
The arrowhead disappeared entirely. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow lodged. I found the arrow wound very narrow. I probed it with a fine probe, but felt nothing. He experienced a prickling sensation and pain under his ear, on the right side.
I hoped that the prickling was due to the arrowhead, so I applied a dressing with drawing and ripening properties, hoping the area would swell and reveal the arrowhead, so I could then cut it out. However, no indication emerged that the arrow had reached that spot.
I continued applying the dressing for many days, but nothing happened.  The wound eventually healed, and the patient despaired of removing the arrow.  One day, he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me.  I then applied the strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it.  I felt the thin arrowhead, which adhered to the wood.  Then, I increased the application of the strong medicine, until the arrowhead itself was visible.  It took about four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I could insert forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it wouldn't come out.  I continued to manipulate it and use various tools until I finally grasped it with strong forceps, as illustrated at the end of this chapter.
The physicians had determined that his nasal cartilage could not be repaired, but I repaired it, and the wound healed completely, leaving the patient free of any further pain.  I am telling you how I removed this arrow so that you may use this as a model and guide for cases I have not mentioned.
Because the parts and details of this craft cannot be grasped by description or encompassed by a book.  Instead, the skillful artisan gauges the many from the few, the present from the absent, and invents new methods and tools in response to unusual cases.
Concerning this craft, I say that arrows are removed from the affected limb in two ways: either by pulling them out from the point of entry, or from the opposite side, which is where they should exit.
If the arrow is protruding through soft tissue, it can be pulled out. If it does not come out immediately, you should leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh becomes infected and it is easier to remove.  The same applies if it's lodged in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for a few days and try pulling and moving it each day. It will come out. If it still will not come out after a few days, you should carefully pierce around the arrow in the same bone from every side with a fine awl, widening the path for the arrow, and then pull it out.
If the arrow is lodged in the skull, deep within one of the brain ventricles, and some of the symptoms I described previously appear, refrain from pulling the arrow out. Leave it until the situation improves after a few days.
Then, proceed with pulling out the arrow.  If it is deeply embedded and will not come out, use the awls around the arrow as described previously. Then treat the wound until it heals.  And if the arrow has lodged in a hidden part of the body... If it is hidden from sight, probe for it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it out with suitable instruments. If you cannot reach it due to the narrow wound and the deep penetration of the arrow, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way,
then widen the wound to gain access to the arrow and remove it. If it has two prongs, grasp them and detach the flesh around them from every side with all possible methods. If you cannot remove the flesh, then fracture and twist the prongs until you can free it.
When attempting to remove an arrow from any location, use the forceps to twist it in all directions until you extract it.  Be extremely gentle so that the arrow does not break, making its removal more difficult. If you are unable to remove it after some time,
leave it for a few days so that the surrounding flesh decays. Then try again; it will be easier then. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments described in the relevant section.  Exercise caution to avoid cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon. Employ any method that works.
In every way possible, remove the arrow gently, carefully, and firmly, as I have described.  When pulling the arrow, it is best to position the patient as he was when the arrow struck him.
If this is not possible, use whatever positions you can.  As for an arrow exiting from the opposite side, either something of the arrow will be visible externally, or you can feel the arrowhead close to the surface of the skin.
Cut along it, making the incision wide enough for the forceps. Then pull it out; it will be easier to remove. If it is lodged in bone, twist the forceps in a circular motion until the arrow loosens its grip on the bone and makes room for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days and try again until it comes out. If the shaft of the arrow remains, push it out with it. If the shaft has fallen off and you want to push it out, insert a hollow instrument into the arrowhead and use it to push the arrow out.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that fits into the hollow. This will make removing the arrow easier.  If the arrow is poisoned, you should remove all the poison from the affected flesh if possible, and then treat the wound appropriately.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or flank, and is close enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision, make an incision, being careful not to sever a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if necessary, and treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps used to extract arrows:.
Its tips are like the beak of a bird, crafted so that, when it grips an arrow or something else, it will not let go.  Different sizes—large, small, and medium—are made, depending on the size of the arrow, the extent of the wound, and its width.
Image of the hollowed-out cannon:.
Image of the solid cannon.
This is solid at the end, like a rod, to easily enter the hollowed-out arrow and push it out.
Chapter Ninety-Five.
On the opening of veins.
The veins commonly opened in the body are thirty in number.  Among them, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the *hasisins*; the two arteries on the temples, visible; and the two veins at the corners of the eyes, known as the *naazirins*.
And the vein standing in the middle of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip, and these veins known as the *jayharak*, and the two veins under the tongue.
And as for the veins opened in the arm and hand, they are five veins. One of them is the *qayfal*, which is from the outer side, and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the *akhal*, which is the middle vein, is composed of a branch of the *basliq* and a branch of the *qayfal*, and the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the *basliq*, which is situated on the inner side, is also called the *abtar* and the common people call it the vein of the abdomen and the cord of the arm; it is situated on the elbow, and it is that which clings to it. And it is that which is clearly visible above the thumb, and the *asilim*.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, with two branches.  In the leg and foot, there are three veins: one beneath the knee bend on the outer side, the second is the *safin*, located at the heel on the inner side, and the vein of the women, located at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg, there are three veins like them.  As for the two veins behind the ears, their opening is beneficial for chronic colds, migraines, scabies, and chronic, bad head sores.  And the method of their opening, as I describe.
This is done by shaving the patient's head and rubbing its back at the location of the veins with a rough cloth, rubbing thoroughly. Then, the patient's neck is bound with a turban until the veins appear, their location being behind the ears, in the low parts of the head. Then, you feel for them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark it with ink.
Then take a scalpel, known as a *nishal*, and insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then, lift the vein and skin upward and cut the vein with the skin in a severed manner, the length of the cut being about two clenched fingers.
Or similar, and drain the amount of blood desired. Then tie them with ligatures and leave them until healed.  Their cutting and cauterization were previously mentioned at the beginning of the book.  As for the two arteries in the temples, their opening is beneficial for chronic migraines, severe headaches, chronic eye inflammation, and sharp discharge of humor into the eyes.
Their opening, as I describe to you, involves binding the patient's neck with a turban until the veins appear clearly to the touch, and their pulsation becomes apparent under your finger.  At that point, mark it with ink.  Then, lift the skin from above the vein with your index finger.
Insert the *nishal* scalpel from below, lift the vein upward, and sever it as you did with the other veins. Drain the blood as needed. Then, loosen the patient's neckband and place your finger on the vein for an hour.
Then apply a piece of cloth and a ligature, and tie tightly from above. Leave it until healed.  Their mention, cutting, and draining were previously discussed in the beginning of the book.  As for opening the forehead vein, its benefit, following the opening of the temporal veins, is for chronic facial conditions such as sores, ulcers, and persistent redness.
Its opening, as I describe to you, involves the patient binding their neck with a turban until the vein appears.  Then, take the instrument called the *fas* (axe).  Here is its image:
Place the pointed end of the axe on the vein, strike from above with a mallet or similar object, and allow the blood to flow as desired.  Then, loosen the patient's neckband and secure it tightly until healed.
One may also open it with a broad scalpel, but it should not be a sharp-edged scalpel like other scalpels, rather slightly broad-edged.  Open it along the groove, because the bone is close by.  A thin scalpel might break there.
As for the veins in the eye sockets, their benefit is for eye ailments like scabies, redness, and sores, and facial diseases.  The method of opening them is for the patient to bind their neck with a turban, then to open them while standing over their head.  Open them along a slight longitudinal groove with a small, slightly broad scalpel.
If the area has little flesh, a thin-edged scalpel might break.  Take as much blood as you need, and place a bandage over them.  Secure them for one night, then release them.  As for opening the nasal vein, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
Severe headaches and facial diseases such as the red rash that appears on the nose, especially if chronic, are treated by this method.  The procedure involves binding the patient's neck, holding their nose with your left hand, and taking a long, thin scalpel.
Insert it into the center of the nostril itself between the nasal partitions in a straight line, because the vein is not apparent to the touch there.  Blood will flow immediately.  You should push the scalpel slightly and take the desired amount of blood, then bandage it for a night.  It will clot quickly.
As for the jugular veins, their opening is beneficial for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and melancholic diseases that appear on the skin's surface, such as black vitiligo, scabies, foul ulcers, and cankers.  The procedure for opening them is to bind the patient's neck beneath them with a bandage, and the surgeon should stand over the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair, then the vein is opened lengthwise, with a somewhat wide incision.  Then, the moderate amount of blood, or as much as needed, is drawn.  The same procedure is performed on the other vein.  Finally, the bandage is released.
The veins are bound with a moderate pressure to prevent suffocation, and the patient is left for the next day.  The wound will heal.  As for the veins in the neck, opening them after opening the jugular veins is useful for sores in the mouth, gum disease, foul ulcers, cracked lips, and foul sores.
Those that are in the nose and around it.  The procedure for opening them is to have the patient sit facing you, and tie their neck with a turban.  Then, turn their lips and look for the veins; one will be seen to the right of the lip, the other to the left.  Identify them by their blackness.
Around them are also thin black veins, so cut them off completely.  If it is unclear and you do not know what they are, then aim for the largest and clearest ones.  Do the same with the veins in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, opening them after opening the jugular veins is useful for the constrictions that occur in the throat due to the disease of the larynx and diseases of the mouth.  The procedure for opening them is to have the patient sit before you facing the sun, raise their tongue, and look under the tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for a vein. Their color is towards black.
Open them, and be cautious. Do not cut them deeply, as there are arteries beneath them. Excessive bleeding from those arteries might occur.  The three veins opened at the elbow are those customarily opened in all people. Opening them can be done in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, aromatic lancet or olive-shaped lancet, towards the soft tissue, or by cutting with a scalpel, which is the scraping [and this is its image].  Image of the wide, aromatic lancet:
It should be wide, as you see, to be suitable for opening the hollow, full, prominent, visible, thick veins that contain thick, dark blood. And this is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
This lancet is narrower and has a thinner tip, suitable for opening thin veins that contain thin, yellowish blood.  And this is the image of the scraping lancet.
This scraping lancet, which is suitable for cutting, comes in various widths and thicknesses, according to the width or narrowness of the veins.  One may infer other things from this as well, and it is well-known among craftsmen.
And concerning the jugular vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the ailments that are beneath the throat and neck, extending to the chest and abdomen. The phlebotomist should be cautious when opening it, and should be aware that there is an artery underneath.  If he makes a mistake and inserts the lancet too deeply, he will sever that artery.
This will cause bleeding, therefore, it should not be opened with a pointed lancet, but rather opened with a scraping lancet.  If the jugular vein is not clearly visible, it should be avoided, and one should turn to another vein or try to find a branch of it.
Or open the arm's vein instead, for it is clear and can be opened with the scraping lancet, as we said.  If you wish to open it specifically, then before tightening the arm, you must feel the area until you locate the pulse point and mark it with ink.
Then the arm is tied and the vein is opened with a slanted cut using the scraping lancet, as we said.  Carefully ensure that the incision is made away from the artery's location.  Whenever you see inflation at the marked spot after tightening the bandage, that inflation is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood during the phlebotomy surging like a child's urine, and the blood is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood.  Immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger; if the bleeding stops, it often stops, and tighten the arm.
Leave it be, and warn the patient about neglecting it.  Let it be on a pillow, and do not move it for days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and overwhelms you and you do not have a remedy at hand, cut the artery if it appears to you, for its extremities contract and the blood stops.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it, and take one half, and tightly bind it over the vein with a bandage and a splint until the next day. If the bleeding stops, then do so; otherwise, treat it with the previously mentioned methods of applying cauterizing instruments to stop the bleeding. Stopping the bleeding is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the small size of the wound and the tightness of the bandage on the arm, be aware of it.  As for the black vein, the benefit of its opening is to draw blood from the top of the head and the lower part of the body, since it is composed of a branch of the basilic vein and a branch of the cephalic vein, as we said.
The phlebotomist should be careful when performing the procedure.  Underneath, there are nerves.  If the lancet is driven too deep and strikes a nerve, numbness will result, making it difficult to treat, and possibly will not heal at all.  This nerve is often perceptible to the touch.
If in some people it is hidden and thin, not discernible, then you should make your incision a shallow one with the lancet, and avoid the nerve to the best of your ability. If the vein is between two nerves, then cut the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of its opening is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and in this vein especially, if you wish to open it, make the incision with the olive-shaped lancet or the wide, rue-shaped lancet, because it is the safest of all veins, as there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
However, when performing the phlebotomy, you should avoid striking the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft spot.  If it is not struck in the first attempt, it is not harmful to repeat the phlebotomy multiple times, although it may swell in some people if not opened in the initial attempt.
But that swelling will not harm him.  As for the method of phlebotomy, its symptoms, and what should be done to treat them, first you must know that phlebotomy is used either to preserve health only.
Or it may be used in diseases.  The one used to preserve health and maintain it and to guard against diseases should be done on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, the basilic, and the median cubital vein.  Phlebotomy should be performed at the beginning of spring, if signs of fullness appear.
Phlebotomy should be performed on Sunday or Tuesday, after three hours of daylight have passed.  As for phlebotomy used in diseases, it does not have a fixed time, but whenever necessity and urgency require it, whether night or day.
At all hours and at all times.  Boys should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should elderly men who have passed sixty.  If anyone intends to perform phlebotomy for any reason...
He should cleanse his bowels before phlebotomy with a gentle enema if there is much retained phlegm, lest foul matter from the bowels be drawn into the veins during phlebotomy and harm the major organs.  A feverish person, a drunkard, or an inebriated person should not be bled until these conditions subside.
One should also beware of phlebotomy after dysentery, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual activity, exertion, exercise, late nights, fasting, and all that weakens the body, physically or mentally.  Then, if the blood is thick, one should look into thinning the humors beforehand.
With foods, drinks, and medications if possible.  Then he should enter a bath if nothing prevents it, or do some light exercise, so that the blood is thinned, and he should perform the phlebotomy in the middle of the day as we have said.  He should also try to clear his mind of all bad thoughts that day.
He should avoid worries, anger, and fear, and all physical ailments such as extreme fatigue, exhaustion, and sexual activity, and similar things.  He should prepare a pleasant environment, including perfumes, herbs, and amusements, in keeping with the customs and abilities of each person.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion higher than the one the patient sits on, and the phlebotomist should rub the patient's arm with his hand two or three times, then tighten the bandage with a ligature and twist it two or three times, keeping the tightening moderate.
Because if the tightening is excessive, it will prevent the flow of blood, or if it is too loose, it will also prevent the flow of blood.  Then, after tightening, the patient should rub his hands together.
Until the veins swell and become noticeable.  Then the phlebotomist should lightly oil the lancet, especially with old oil.  Then he should place his left index finger on the same vein, slightly below the spot where he intends to make the incision, so that the vein is not displaced during the puncture. Because some veins are like tendons, pulling away during the incision, and others are full of air, and when the lancet is placed on them, they recede under the lancet, deceiving the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein. If it does open it, it only does so to a small degree.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be cautious and careful in all these matters. Then he lowers the lancet; if it opens the vein on that first attempt, well and good; otherwise, he tries again slightly below or above that spot, quickly if the area has not swollen.
If the patient swells or feels pain, leave it for a day or two without tightening the bandage, as it might cause a hot swelling. He should not enter a bath. Then he may repeat the incision if he wishes, if the lancet wound was small, and the blood flow was thin.
Fearing that not enough blood will be drawn, the phlebotomist should reinsert the lancet into the same puncture, gently and straight, and slightly enlarge the opening.  Do this quickly before the area swells, as a small opening can cause swelling in many people.
If you see it has swollen, do not return to that spot; it will not help. Apply some clarified oil; it facilitates blood flow, and is superior to plain oil and other ointments in this case.
Use clarified oil in all your vein-opening procedures when blood flow is difficult.  This remedy, and also shukhznaya, can achieve the same result if applied to the affected area. The blood will thin and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the incision site, which often happens especially for those who have never been bled before, or if the vein opening was small, immediately apply a sponge dipped in slightly warmed salt water to the swelling and bandage it for an hour. It will dissolve.
This should be done after the blood from the vein itself has completely drained or from another vein. If a dark or greenish residue remains at the site after a few days, it is not harmful. If you wish, apply a poultice of powdered myrrh and gum.
Or something from the juice of fudhanj, and similar things.  A swelling or lump often occurs when bleeding basilisk veins. If, upon pressing on it, you find it pulses, that is a bad lump. Beware of applying anything to it that we have mentioned.
For it may bleed arterial blood. However, bandage it with something that constricts to solidify the area, and then treat it with other remedies until it heals.  One should draw from each person according to their strength and the predominant color of their blood.
If the blood is dark, let it flow until it turns red.  Likewise, if you see it thick, let it flow until it becomes thin.  And if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness is gone.  A strong, full individual needing a single, large bloodletting should...
...widen the vein opening and use a wide lancet.  A weak individual, however, should do the opposite.  And it is necessary to remove the blood in stages.  The opening should be narrow.  The best tool for opening a vein is a curved, slanted incision, not a pointed one.
This type of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and nerve damage. It is better and safer than bloodletting across or lengthwise.  If fainting often occurs during bloodletting, it is necessary to give the patient something to eat before the procedure, such as bread soaked in pomegranate juice or sweetened water.
If he is feverish and has his blood drawn three or four times, if his constitution is cold, let him eat bread soaked in myrrh liquid, or honey-based drink scented with perfumes, or a pleasant herbal drink before the bloodletting.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting due to excessive blood loss, he should be given meat broth and a light herbal drink.  Perfume the patient with musk and wash the patient's chest with it.  Use all the treatments mentioned in the section on fainting.
For those who have had extensive vomiting, and whose strength is weak, if they wish to release their arm's blood again, the blood should be released slowly, little by little, according to their strength over several consecutive days.
For those who want to release their arm's blood again and whose body is strong, let them do so after seven or nine hours from the first bloodletting.  If one wishes to draw blood from their body to the opposite side where it has accumulated, then blood release should occur on the second or third day.
If the patient has excessive, heated, and inflamed blood in their body causing fever, blood should be released in one session, removing a large quantity, widening the opening until fainting occurs, after careful consideration of all bloodletting requirements. Place your hand on the patient's pulse while the blood flows to prevent death instead of fainting, as this often occurs if the bloodletter is unaware and careless.  This should not be done if you intend to release the arm's blood again.
If the vein opening has closed and blood flow is difficult, do not forcefully poke or twist it, as this is very harmful. Instead, either leave it until a subsequent bloodletting, or remove the clotted blood from the vein opening with a lancet.
Or apply something dissolved in water, like salt, or a healing remedy like Faruq or Shakhzanaia, and gently poke it until blood flows. If the vein is swollen, leave it.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides.  If further bloodletting is absolutely necessary, either perform it above the affected area or in the other arm or a different vein.
Bloodletting from the arm's artery is a substitute for bloodletting from the eyesocket or the temples if those sites are not available or are difficult to access, as it contains elements from both.  To do this, the patient should put their hand in warm water until the vein in their forearm becomes red and clearly visible.  Then, apply a moderate bandage pressure just above the area.
Then, cut the vein slightly off-center, neither horizontally nor vertically, and make the incision wide.  Do the lancing just above the wrist joint. If blood flow is difficult, re-immerse the arm in the warm water container and allow the blood to flow into the water.
Until you have taken the amount you need. If it is summer, you may omit the step of returning the arm to warm water. The practice of letting the blood flow into the warm water is most often used in winter.  Bloodletting from this vein is the safest of all the veins. Because there is no vital vein or nerve underneath.  Bloodletting from the ulnar vein of the right arm is beneficial for liver ailments.  To do this, tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand, after first immersing the arm in warm water until the vein swells.
Because there is no vital vein or nerve underneath.  Bloodletting from the ulnar vein of the right arm is beneficial for liver ailments.  To do this, tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand, after first immersing the arm in warm water until the vein swells. The vein will be clearly visible.  Cut it slightly off-center.  If you sever it completely, it will do him no harm.  Be careful; do not press too hard with the lancet, as there is a nerve supplying the fingers beneath, and the area is devoid of muscle. Then, return the arm to the warm water.
Leave it to continue flowing. If you do not return it to the warm water, the blood will coagulate in the opening of the vein and cease to flow. Once you have removed the required amount of blood, apply ointment and salt to the vein to prevent rapid clotting.
You should also do this with every constricted branch.  As for the benefit of bloodletting from the left arm, it is beneficial for spleen ailments.  Do the same as you did with the second vein.  As for bloodletting from the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower body, such as uterine ailments.
And for menstrual retention, kidney ailments, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar illnesses.  The method of bloodletting is to immerse the leg in warm water and apply massage until the veins dilate.  Then, tie above the joint of the leg with a bandage. The vein is located near the heel, visible near the big toe.
From it, many branches extend along the leg.  Therefore, cut into the widest branch, or near the heel where it joins, as this is the best and safest option.  If you cut into the side of the leg, be careful of the nerves beneath it.
Make your incision with a twisting motion, as if you were intending to sever it, and the lancet should be sharp.  If the blood is slow to flow, return the leg to warm water and let the blood flow until it is emptied. If the practitioner misses the vein on the first attempt.
If the practitioner misses the vein on the first attempt, return to a point slightly higher on the leg.  The location is safe; there is no danger if you avoid the nerve, as we said before. The same procedure applies to the saphenous vein on the other leg.  As for the femoral vein, it is located, as we stated, at the heel on the outer side.
The benefit of bloodletting is for hip pain if caused by hot blood.  The procedure for bloodletting is to have the patient enter a warm bath, then quickly and tightly bandage the leg from the hip to above the heel with a thin, long bandage. Only then will it be apparent.
If it appears, then lance it in any manner, either twisting (which is better), or severing, or slitting.  The location is safe; and in most people it is quite hidden, if not found.
If it does not appear at all, then lance some of its branches, which appear on the back of the foot near the pinky and ring fingers.  Be mindful of the nerves, and release the desired amount of blood.
Then loosen the bandage, place a bandage on the site of the bloodletting, and bandage the area.  It will quickly heal.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On Cupping and its Use.
Cupping vessels may be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass.  Cupping can be of two types: one with a specific purpose, involving the application of a cup and the removal of blood; the other without a specific purpose.
And this cupping without a specific purpose is of two types.
And the cups of the "deceivers," which are the sides of the neck on both sides, and the cups of the chin, which are below the lower jaw of the mouth, and the cups of the shoulders, and the cups of the coccyx on the rump, and the cups of the forearms, which are the middle of the arms, and the cups of the legs, and the cups of the ankles.
Cupping draws blood from the fine veins spread throughout the flesh.  For this reason, it does not weaken the strength like bloodletting, and cupping, whether with or without fire, should not be used for any disease arising from fullness until the whole body is emptied.
If cupping is needed, whether due to illness or custom, we use it at all times, at the beginning, end, and middle of the month, and at any time.
For some people, when there is an excess of blood requiring removal by cupping, they experience heaviness and headaches in their heads. Some experience fullness, redness in their face, head, and neck. Some experience itching in their face, cheeks, and darkness and heaviness in their eyes. Some scratch the area where they were cupped.
Some laugh excessively, some taste blood in their mouths, their gums throb, and they spit blood. Some sleep excessively, and some see blood, redness, killings, and wounds, and the like, in their dreams. Whenever we see any of these things...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order cupping after two or three hours have passed during the day.  The benefit of cupping the neck is that it is helpful for heaviness in the head and water running down into the eyes.
However, it is necessary that this be done after the entire body has been emptied. This cupping may be a substitute for venesection of the temples. One should beware that those with a cold head or a cold, should not use it, as it will greatly harm them.
Therefore, it is not appropriate for the elderly or those with cold diseases of the head to use it.  And those who use it habitually may become forgetful.
Therefore, the cupper should lower the cupping vessel slightly, for fear of forgetfulness.  Cupping the shoulder is a substitute for venesection of the temples and the veins at the base of the skull.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, disruption of the respiratory apparatus, coughing, and fullness.  The cupping of the shoulder should be raised slightly, because if it is lowered, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping the temples is beneficial for headaches, ophthalmia, migraine, suffocation, and toothache.  It is a substitute for venesection at the base of the skull.  The cupper should be instructed not to insert the instrument deeply lest a vein be severed and bleeding result.
Cupping under the chin is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum disease, and similar mouth ailments.  It can replace venesection of the veins on the lips.  Cupping the shoulders is beneficial for palpitations due to fullness and heat.
Cupping the forearm pit is beneficial for what the venesection of the three veins—the veins at the base of the skull, the temples, and the neck—is beneficial for, because it draws blood from all those thin veins in the flesh and draws those thin veins from other, thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to push the instrument too far, as the area is lacking flesh and has nerves and arteries underneath.  A single cupping on the coccyx is beneficial for hemorrhoids and sores of the lower part of the body.
The cupper should be instructed to use a large, brass cupping vessel, as the area requires strong suction; a glass cupping vessel may break.  A considerable amount of pressure should be applied.  Cupping the legs clearly reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, and promotes menstruation.  It is also beneficial for boils and abscesses and acts as a substitute for venesection of the veins in the inner thigh and the ankles.  However, it greatly weakens the body, and fainting occurs in most people.  Cupping the ankles is similar in benefit to cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping is to first place the empty cupping vessel and apply moderate suction.  Do not prolong the application, but place and remove the cupping quickly so that the humors flow evenly to the area.  Repeat this until the area reddens and opens.
When the redness of blood appears, then apply pressure and resume the suction gradually.  Then observe the condition of the body.  For those with delicate, porous flesh, a single pressure is sufficient, to avoid causing an ulceration.
The cupper should widen the pressure, deepening it slightly, and regulate the suction with gentle movement. If the blood is thick, then the pressure should be applied twice.  The first time to open a path for the thin and watery blood, and the second to remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very thick, the pressure should be applied a third time to achieve the desired result.  In general, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, a single pressure is sufficient. If we want to remove a large amount of blood, we apply more pressure. If the blood is thick, we apply more pressure.
The pressure should be deep.  The moderate limit of the pressure is just the depth of the skin.  What ointments should be used when applying cupping?  And what water? What should the cupper beware of?  For those with thick, hard, dry skin, and narrow pores...
The cupping sites should be anointed with opening, softening, and dissolving ointments.  In the summer, examples include the ointment of Khwari, or the ointment of violet, or the ointment of sweet almond, or the ointment of pumpkin seed.
In winter, examples include the ointment of narcissus, or the ointment of lily, or the ointment of chamomile, or the ointment of hyacinth, and the like.  If the excess is thick and cold, use the ointment of marjoram, or the ointment of cumin, or the ointment of frankincense, or the ointment of dill, and similar ones.
If the cupper has wide pores and soft flesh, then the use of ointment should be avoided.  These should wash their cupping vessels after the procedure with rose water, or cold water, or elderberry water, or pumpkin water, or water from the *rajla* plant, or similar.  As for those with excessively moist blood...
They should wash their cupping vessels with vinegar, or with rue and sumac water, and the like.  As for those with thick excess, they should wash their cupping vessels with old wine, or marjoram water, or dill or chamomile broth, and similar things.  Cupping should be avoided in the bath and immediately after the bath.
Rather, it should be used one or two hours after leaving the bath.  One should not sleep after cupping.  This is what the cupper and the leeches should manage before and after cupping.
One should first observe if the cupper or leech is bilious, and if their blood is predominantly characterized by sharpness and inflammation.  Then, they should take cooling things like pomegranate, dandelion, vinegar, lettuce, sicken, and the like.  Their food should consist of poultry, lamb, and other similarly lean meats.
And for those whose temperament is cold, they should be given honeyed drinks, drinks made from *mi'ba* or *sakanjbin* (a specific type of syrup), and moderate aromatic wine, which is between old and new.  A reduced diet is prescribed, with food consisting of poultry, partridges, sparrows, and doves.  Food should be less than the drinks on the day of cupping and leeches.
In some cases, and for certain individuals, a potion of antidotal remedy or musk medicine or *shiltha* may be administered before cupping or bloodletting, or afterwards, to strengthen the major organs and thin the blood.  However, it should not be given to those who are feverish.
As for cupping without a specific condition, these are the cuppings placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and groin.  These organs do not tolerate the condition, but they are used to draw blood from one organ to another.
For example, placing a cupping on the breasts in cases of blood-letting; or we use it to remove cold air that has collected in an organ, such as placing a cupping on the abdomen and navel.  This loosens the organ, warms it, and alleviates the pain by dissolving that air.
It may be placed on the kidneys if blockages or stones are present, as its strong drawing action may open the blockage or dislodge the stones.  Similarly, it is done on the liver and spleen when wind troubles them. These cuppings can also be used empty, by suction only.
It may be used with fire, or filled with lukewarm water in cases of paralysis.  This involves filling a large cupping with hot water alone, or water in which certain suitable herbs have been boiled.
Then, filled, it is placed on the affected area, held, and removed, repeated as needed.  This is the form of cupping that is used with fire.
Its opening is two open fingers wide, as illustrated, and its depth is half a span.  A small hole, big enough for a needle to enter, is made on one side, about halfway down. It is made of thick-edged Chinese or brass.
It is smooth, flat, and polished so as not to harm the organ when placed upon it. A crosspiece of brass or iron is in the middle to hold the lit candle.  This cupping can be made larger or smaller, according to the illnesses.
The user must be skilled, for cupping for children and thin people is different from that for men and those with large bodies.  As for how to place this cupping with fire on the organ, one should light a wick made of strong linen or a small candle made of pitch, and place it on the center of the fixed rod in the center of the cupping.
So that the fire ascends towards the bottom of the cupping, so that the patient's body is not burned.  Then, it is placed on the organ, and the finger is placed on the hole we mentioned so that when we hold the cupping, we don't need to remove the finger.
Steam will emerge from that hole, and the cupping will be applied to the site.  Then the wick is relit, and repeated if necessary.  As for the cupping used for dropsy, it does not have a fixed rod or a hole.
Instead, it is used by filling it with water and placing it on the organ only.  This is its image.
The larger this cupping is, to hold much water, the better.
Chapter Ninety-seven.
On the application of leeches.
Leeches are used most often on parts of the body where cupping cannot be applied, either because they are small, like the lips and gums, or because the part is bare of flesh, such as the finger and nose.
To use them, select leeches from clean fresh water free of impurities, then leave them in fresh water for a day and a night to starve and clear their stomachs. Then, the body is first bled by venesection or cupping, and then the affected part is cleansed.
Until it turns red, then the leech is placed on it.  If it fills up, it will fall off.  If the affected area can be suctioned by cupping, that is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and massage and squeeze it. If the bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and it is a trickle, then soak a linen cloth in cold water.
Place it over the area until the bleeding stops. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered alum or oak galls or similar astringents over it until the bleeding stops, or place peeled halves of chick peas on the area.
Leave it until the chick peas adhere to the area; the bleeding will stop. If the leech needs to be replaced, if possible, do not use the same leech. If the leech refuses to attach, wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or, insert a needle into the area to draw out some blood; then place the leech there.  If it senses any blood, it will attach to the spot. If you want to remove it, sprinkle some dates, salt, or ashes on it and it will detach from the spot.
Chapter Three.
On fractures.
This chapter also deals with the necessary skills in the medical art, namely the setting of fractures and dislocations occurring in the bones.
Know, my children, that many ignorant physicians and common people, those who have never examined the writings of the ancients, nor read a single letter from them, may claim mastery of this chapter.
For this reason, this science has become extinct in our land, and I have never encountered a skilled practitioner in it. I have only gained what I have gained through extensive study of the works of the ancients and my diligence in understanding them, extracting knowledge from them, then through dedicated practice and experience throughout my life.
I have presented to you in this chapter everything that my knowledge encompasses. And after presenting it to you, having refined it from lengthy digressions, and condensed it to the utmost brevity, and elucidated it thoroughly, I have illustrated for you in it numerous depictions of the instruments used therein.
This is an added explanation, as I have done in the preceding two chapters.  There is no strength except from God, the Most High, the Great.
The First Chapter.
It contains a summary of the matter of fractured bones, and it is necessary to present them before we begin to describe the fractured and dislocated members one by one.  Before starting this chapter, it is necessary to provide a summary of the subject and its divisions. This will first compel you, and whoever else is eager to learn this noble craft, to understand and grasp its true meaning.
So I say, if a person suffers a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall, one should first promptly reduce the injury, or apply laxatives, or both. Unless prevented by factors such as weakness, or the individual who suffered the injury is a child or an old man, or the weather is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, his diet should be limited to cold legumes, poultry, and venison.  Drinks, fatty meats, overeating, and any food that causes the veins to fill with blood should be avoided until the swelling subsides and the possibility of fluid buildup at the site is lessened.
Then, the patient should return to his usual regimen. If the broken bone begins to knit, the patient should eat foods that are nourishing, dense, and strong, with a viscous quality, such as dates, rice, heads of grain, and ox tripe. Eggs, soft fish, thick drinks, and similar foods will speed up and improve the healing of the fracture.  Be aware that fractured bones in strong men and elderly individuals may never fully rejoin as they were originally.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, and even though bones that were very flexible, like those of young children, might knit together, nature produces a substance resembling glue on all sides of the fractured bone.  This thick substance adheres to and holds the pieces firmly together.
Thus binding the pieces together until it reaches the original strength and firmness, so that nothing hinders its function.
For this reason, the patient's diet should consist of foods that are strong, viscous, and thick, as previously mentioned.  Know that fractures vary according to the affected bone, since a fracture in the leg is different from a fracture in the head, and a fracture in the chest differs from a fracture in the back.
Likewise, all other organs differ from one another.  We will discuss each type of fracture in detail in its own section, and the type of fracture itself may also differ.
Because the fracture may be a simple break without fragments, or it may be lengthwise with fragments, some detached, some not.  The fracture may also occur with a wound and skin tearing, or it may be a minor crack.
Each type has a specific method of treatment, as will be detailed in its proper place.  Signs of a broken bone include its crookedness, unevenness, visibility, and the grating sound when touched with the hand.
If there is no visible crookedness, no grating sound, no pain or discomfort when the bone is touched, and the patient does not experience much pain, there is no fracture.  It could be a sprain or a minor break or crack, and it should not be moved at all by pulling or probing.
Instead, apply appropriate medicines, as will be mentioned later, to the area, then gently bind it.  Know that if the bone is fractured and splintered in two, but without fragments, then each part has moved away from its companion.
You should promptly realign and straighten the bone before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling does occur, leave it for a few days until it subsides, then realign it by any gentle means possible.
Know that setting and straightening a bone without splinters is easier.  Then it is bound, as will be described later. If the bone has splinters, the fractured limb (either an arm or a leg) must be stretched from both sides.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with both ropes and hands. Position the limb on a flat surface in its natural shape. Once the fractured bone parts have been stretched, gently return the protruding parts to their places by any means possible.
Make every effort to avoid causing pain or suffering to the patient.  Strive to join the two bone parts together in the best possible position.  At that time, you should touch and feel the bones with your hand.  If you find something amiss, correct and straighten it to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive stretching and forceful prodding, as many ignorant people do.  Often, they cause a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb, as I have observed repeatedly from their actions.
After setting, perfecting, and binding the limb, keep it still and quiet. Warn the patient not to move it during waking or sleeping hours, or when turning over, or while experiencing discomfort, or during bowel movements, or with any other movement to the greatest extent possible.  Ensure that the limb's position prevents pain.
If the patient feels pain or discomfort in a particular position of the limb, he should change that position to one where he feels no pain.  In doing so, he should also ensure that the new position is straight and level.
So that no crookedness occurs in the limb if it is forcibly straightened.  Now, regarding how to bind a fractured limb, as I am describing it to you, know that fractured limbs vary in size, shape, and configuration.  Small ones, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and similar, are...
The bandages should be gently applied for small limbs, such as the arm and fingers.  For larger limbs, like the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be firm and wide, since a broad bandage is necessary for a large limb, firmly binding it on all sides equally, without any gaps.
Then, after completing the setting, apply the appropriate plaster. Gently apply the plaster to the fracture site.  Then, begin wrapping the bandage around the fracture three or four times, depending on the limb's needs.  Slightly tighten the bandage, then continue wrapping towards the upper portion of the fracture.
Tighten the bandage less than you tightened it on the fracture site.  Then, move the wrapping away from the fracture a little, and gradually loosen the tension until the correct alignment is achieved.  Then take another bandage and wrap it also around the fracture site.
Wrap further, moving the wrap towards the lower portion of the fracture.  Maintain the tension and looseness of the wrapping as described for the initial upper wrap.  Then, place padding of soft material or cloth between the bandages to correct any misalignment of the fracture.
If there is misalignment, then do not add anything.  Then, wrap another bandage around it.  Then, apply firm splints to these bandages, if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or inflammation, apply something to reduce the swelling and inflammation. Leave it for a few days. Then, apply the splints at that time. The splints should be cut from wide, flat reeds, skillfully prepared, or from a kind of wood.
Made from pine, palm leaves, reeds, or similar readily available materials.  The splint should be of a uniform size and shape in this manner.
However, the splint placed directly on the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others.  The length of the splint should be appropriate to the size of the fractured limb.  Image of the splint:.
Then, tighten the splints with another bandage, in the same way as the first. Then, tie them securely from above with strong threads, as described previously, ensuring the tightening is concentrated on the fracture site. The tighter the wrapping, the closer it is to the fracture.
The threads should be of medium thickness and should be made of damp linen, in particular.  For threads to be thick, as observed with the actions of the ignorant who use twisted linen strips, is a serious error.
Because the tightening with them deviates from the proper level, and thin threads are also unsuitable, as you cannot achieve the desired tightening with them.  There should not be less than a finger's width between splints.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after tightening in the correct places, place soft padding, or fluffy wool, underneath them so that nothing causes him harm.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and a skin tear has occurred, we will address this separately.
Know that not every fractured limb should be splinted from the very first day.  If the limb is large, splints should not be applied until five or seven days, or longer, depending on your assurance that inflammation will not occur.
This is a description of the bandages used by the ancients to treat fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  It is a general, concise bandage that sets fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, as it is neither overly hot nor cold.
Take flour dust, the residue from the millstones, knead it as it is without sifting it with egg whites. Make the dough neither thick nor thin, then bake it.
Another bandage for fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take ten dirhams each of gum, frankincense, acacia, raisin, mastic, and myrrh; five dirhams each of aloes and senna; twenty dirhams of ebony; and twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman clay. Grind all the ingredients together and mix with ebony water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot.  This bandage, then, is well-made, quickly sets broken bones, and is suitable for most people due to its moderate qualities.
Another bandage useful for fractures and sprains: take ten dirhams each of mastic, gum, and white hellebore; five dirhams each of myrrh and senna; six dirhams of acacia; and twenty dirhams of Armenian clay. Grind all together finely, sift, mix with water or egg white, and use.
A bandage for dislocated joints and bones, relieving the pain that accompanies them, and for bone fractures: take prepared wool, dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and place it on the affected area. This bandage does not have the strength to set a fracture.
However, it is excellent for relieving hot swelling and pain, particularly. Another bandage to set a broken bone: take fig leaf and wild poppy leaf, grind them together, and apply the paste. Another concise bandage for the repair of a bone fracture.
If you want to treat the remaining swelling, take parts of hellebore root, chamomile, violet, and quince flour, grind them all together, and mix with ointment if the limb is not inflamed. If it is inflamed, mix with coriander water or water and apply.
Another stronger resolving bandage than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs after a bone injury: take parts of hellebore root, flax seed, fenugreek, rosemary, violet, and chamomile.
Grind all together and mix with vinegar water, or fresh water, or ointment, depending on the heat of the limb and the calming of the heat.  As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that this should be applied to a fractured limb during setting.
Except for the plaster made of wax and oil only, which should be of medium thickness.  Galen believed that things with dryness and some heat, such as myrrh, licorice, and frankincense, should be applied to the fractured limb during setting.
A remedy for weakness and pain: take mugwort, chickpeas, shaved human hair or bird feathers, hellebore, and salt, all equal parts. Grind, sift, mix, and apply as a bandage.  As for the amount of bandaging necessary...
Then, it should be removed.  Observe; if the patient experiences no pain, no rubbing, and no shifting of the fractured bone from its place, do not remove it for many days. However, if severe rubbing, bothersome pain, or swelling occurs at the site,
Then immediately remove it and do not delay.  Remove the bandage. Then, take a soft cloth or a damp sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area until the itching and pain subside, and then allow the limb to rest for an hour.
Then, apply softened wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, and bandage it overnight.  This will alleviate the hot swelling, reduce the limb's swelling, and ease the pain.  Then, gently reapply a light bandage, without the initial tight pressure.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and puffiness, and all the symptoms have disappeared completely and you need a bandage and pressure, then reapply it as you did originally.  If nothing of what we have mentioned happens in the limb,
Then do not remove it until after three, four, five, or seven days.  Sometimes it can be left for twenty days.  All of this depends on the condition of the limb, as we said, until the fracture is fixed and the flesh has begun to heal over it.
Then, at that time, increase the pressure beyond your original pressure. Also, increase the nourishment for the sick person, as previously mentioned.  If you see that the fracture site has become more dry and thin than is appropriate, then know that nourishment is not reaching it.
Then moisten it with lukewarm water every time you remove it, and do this every three days.  Slightly lessen the pressure.  By doing this, nourishment will reach the limb, and it will heal quickly.  As for what the ignorant among the fixers do, in breaking the limb again...
For if it is not first set properly, and is set crooked, this is a mistake on their part, and a great deception.  Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it.  I have seen no trace of it in any of them at all. The correct approach is not to do it.
The second chapter.
Concerning fractures occurring in the head.
Fractures of the head are numerous, their shapes diverse, and their causes varied. Some fractures occur from a sword blow, and these may be such that the entire bone is broken down to the peritoneum, which is beneath the bone. As is done to wood by carpenters.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a carpenter's fracture.  Alternatively, the sword might only sever part of the bone, and only its surface is broken, without penetrating through the bone completely.  This type of fracture is called a simple fracture.  The wound of these fractures may be large or small.
Some fractures are caused by a blow from a stone or a fall onto a stone or similar object, resulting in a contusion or abrasion. This fracture may be penetrating, having approached the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be on the surface of the bone.
The wound of these fractures also may be large or small.  Some fractures are hidden within the bone, subtle hairline cracks.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture.  A fracture can also result from a fall or a blow from a stone or similar object, where a portion of the bone is driven inward, creating a depression, much like how a blow to copper can cause a portion to be driven inward.
This is most common in the soft, spongy bone of children's heads.  All these types of fractures may have detached or non-detached fragments.  We will discuss the treatment of each in its appropriate place.  All these fracture types are recognizable through examination.
Their examination involves probing and removing any decayed flesh.  A hairline fracture is recognized by examining the bone, rubbing and staining it with ink; the fracture will appear black.  Treatment of the fracture first involves looking at the patient's symptoms.
If you see evident symptoms of danger, such as vomiting of bile, prostration, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, red and strained eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat them.  In most cases, death is inevitable with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that are not alarming and hope for recovery, then proceed with treatment.  If the injured person comes to you soon after the injury, particularly in winter...
In any case, you should diligently remove the bone before the fourteenth day. If it is in the summer, you should hasten the removal of the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane beneath the bone decays and causes the symptoms we described.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is also crushing and laceration, you must remove the crushed and lacerated portion, as I am describing to you, which is to shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in any way possible, according to the shape of the wound and the patient's condition.  If during the exposure you encounter bleeding or a hot swelling, address it appropriately.
This involves packing the area with rags dipped in a mixture of rosewater and ointment, and cleaning the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding is controlled.  Then proceed to manipulate and remove the bone, using one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a thin, narrow blade, in this manner:
Then use another blade, slightly wider than the first, in this manner:
Then use yet another blade, wider still than the second.  You should have a set of blades of varying widths and lengths, with extremely sharp edges, made of Indian steel or good quality tempered steel.
Use gentle pressure when using the blades, so as not to jar the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and solid, it is necessary to pierce around it with awls, called "non-dipping awls," before using the blades.
Because they do not penetrate beyond the skull's edge, as the awl's head, below its sharp point, is round, like a ring or small circle, preventing it from sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
You should have a large selection of these awls, each suitable for the thickness of the bone, so that for each skull, you have an awl whose sharp point is the correct length and short for the thickness of that skull.  Here is a picture of three types. Of awls: large, medium, and small.
To pierce around the fractured bone, place the awl on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know the bone has been pierced. Then, move the awl to another location, keeping the distance between each hole about the thickness of a reed or similar.
Then, with the cutters, cut between each pair of holes, doing so as gently as possible, as we said, until you remove the bone, either by hand or with some other tool you have prepared for the purpose.
Such as splints and delicate forceps.  You should be very careful that the awl or cutter does not touch the peritoneum. If you have smoothed the bone and separated it from the peritoneum, if it was adhered to it, and removed it, then it should be cleaned.
Smooth any roughness remaining on the bone with another tool similar to a cutter, but it should be finer and more delicate than the other cutters.  If any small pieces of bone or fragments remain, remove them gently using the tools you have.
Then treat the wound afterward with the dressings and ointments that are mentioned later. The other approach to the work is very straightforward, free of risk; Galen mentioned and praised it greatly.  This is his statement. First, you should begin by exposing the part of the bone in the area where the fracture is most severe and prominent.  Once you have exposed that part, place the tip of this lens-shaped cutter beneath it.  This is its form:
The lens-shaped part of it should be smooth, not cutting anything, and the sharp part should have its sharp edges extended lengthwise, as you see, so that the lens-shaped part rests against the peritoneum and the sharp edge of the cutter faces the bone.  Then strike the cutter from one side.
With a small hammer, so that all the bone is cut gently as it rotates, and you are safe from injuring the membrane, nothing frightening will happen, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were asleep.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in certain places, scrape it off using the tip of the lens-shaped cutter itself, removing it gently.  It will detach without harm or fear.
If the fracture of the bone did not reach the membrane, and the broken part of the bone was its surface, remaining rough and with splinters, it is necessary to remove that roughness and those splinters with rasps of different sizes that you have prepared, so that you can use the most suitable one for each location, according to what the work indicates.
The same fractured bone and its shape, and you should first use the widest rasps on the bone for scraping, then use those that are narrower, and continue doing this systematically until you reach the use of the smallest and thinnest ones.
As for the other small, hairline fractures and minor breaks, each one should be treated according to what will lead to its healing, something that is not hidden from anyone with even a minimal degree of proficiency in this craft, and who has studied what we have written and explained about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, you should take a linen cloth sized to the wound, soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound.  Then take another cloth, folded or triangular. Soak it in rose water and rose oil, place it on the first cloth, and do this with the least possible weight to avoid pressing on the peritoneum. Then, use a wide bandage over this, tightening only as much as needed to hold the wound in place.
Leave it with this treatment for one or two days until you are sure the inflammation subsides, then remove the bandage and subsequently apply remedies that have a drying effect, such as iris roots, caraway powder, kender and zeraوند powder, and similar ones.  These remedies should be made into a paste.
Apply the paste, as it is, to the wound.  In general, use a remedy that cleanses but does not sting.  During your treatment of the wound, make every effort to ensure that it is clean, free from oiliness, dirt, or pus. Do not allow pus to accumulate in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the brain membrane, it corrupts and rots it, causing a great affliction for the patient. This can occur in the meninges of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if treatment is neglected.
If a dark discoloration appears on its surface, and if the patient presents with the symptoms we mentioned, know that he is doomed. If the discoloration was caused by a remedy applied to the area, and the potency of that remedy was such that it produced this discoloration, then...
You should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them thoroughly, and then apply the mixture to a cloth.  Place the cloth on the meninges, and then treat it with the appropriate methods of treatment until it heals.
Chapter Three.
In the setting of a fractured nose.
Know that only the upper portions of the nose, either both or one, can break, because they are bones. The lower part is cartilaginous and does not break, but may be bruised, bent, or perforated.
If one of its parts breaks, you should insert the little finger into the nostril to straighten the fracture from inside. Use your index and thumb to correct the alignment from outside, until the nose is restored to its natural shape. Be gentle and careful not to cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, it should be straightened with a blunt-ended instrument.  If the fracture is in both sides, do the same.  He should address the fracture on the first day after the injury.
If possible, or else after seven to ten days, when the hot swelling has subsided, insert a linen wick into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two wicks if it is on both sides.
The wicks should be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some early surgeons recommended soaking the wicks in oil, changing them daily.  I, however, do not agree with this; the wicks should be soaked in beaten egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the wicks in place until the bone has set and the cartilage has hardened.  After the wicks, one may insert goose feather tubes wrapped in soft linen into the nasal cavity where the wicks were placed.  This will hold the nose fracture more firmly and prevent the patient from having difficulty breathing.
This is not essential. You may choose to use the wicks, or not. If a hot swelling appears on the nose during your treatment, bandage the nose with a linen cloth or a cotton pad dipped in vinegar, rose oil, or a diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling appears, then bandage the outside of the nose with a paste of fine semolina and finely ground kender seeds mixed with beaten egg white.  Then apply a soft bandage.  Do not tie the nose at all, if the nasal bones are fractured in small pieces.
Or if the bones have splintered, one must carefully remove them with the appropriate instrument, then suture the cleft and treat it with ointments that promote healing and suppuration. If a wound occurs inside the nose, one should treat it with wicks and use lead tubes. Until healed.
Fourth Chapter.
On the setting of a fractured lower jaw.
If the lower jaw is fractured, but the fracture is not accompanied by an open wound, and if the fracture is only from the outside, without a double fracture and the jaw bending inward, its identification will be easier.
If the fracture is in the right side, the index finger of the left hand should be inserted into the patient's mouth.  Likewise, if the fracture is in the left side, the index finger of the right hand should be inserted, and gently lift the prominence of the fracture from the inside to the outside.
With your other hand outside the bone, you control its alignment. If the jaw fracture has fractured in two places, then traction should be applied from both sides in a straight line until its alignment is achieved. If it has occurred in the teeth, it should be treated with gentle force.
Or if it has splintered, tie together the fragments that you hope will remain intact with a gold, silver, or silk thread.  Then place a bandage over the fractured jaw, then fold a cloth over that, and place a large, tight splint, or a piece of leather the length of the jaw over it. Then tie it from above according to how you manage to tie it and to match its closure so that it does not dislodge, and instruct the patient to rest and be quiet.  Make his food soft.  If you suspect that something has changed in its shape in any way, promptly reopen it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to how you manage to tie it and to match its closure so that it does not dislodge, and instruct the patient to rest and be quiet.  Make his food soft.  If you suspect that something has changed in its shape in any way, promptly reopen it on the third day. Then correct what you have bandaged with mill dust mixed with egg white or semolina flour, after removing the bandage. Place a soft compress on the dressing, and as long as that dressing adheres to it and the bone's condition has not changed, leave it.
Do not remove it until it heals and the fracture strengthens.  Often, this fracture strengthens in three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during that time, use the remedies we have mentioned repeatedly to alleviate it until the swelling subsides.
And if the fracture is accompanied by an open wound, then if a fragment or fragments of bone have separated, gently remove those fragments with the tools that are suitable for you. If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
Then, if you have removed the fragments and there is nothing left, suture the wound opening if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for it. And close the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of the collarbone if it is fractured.
The collarbone is most frequently fractured from the front, from the end of the shoulder blade. Its fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks and splinters into two pieces without producing fragments, which is the easiest to set;
Or the fracture may produce fragments, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture may occur with a wound.  If the fracture is without a wound, two assistants should be brought. One should hold the arm adjacent to the fractured collarbone, and the other should extend the neck in the opposite direction.
Then straighten the fracture with your fingers until it is properly shaped and free of protuberances or irregularities. If you need more extension, you should place a ball of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit.
Adjust the bone as needed, extending and elevating the collarbone, and pressing the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned. If you cannot pull the end of the collarbone outward because it has sunk inward, the patient should lie on their back.
A medium-sized cushion is placed under the patient's shoulder blade. The assistant presses down on the patient's shoulder blade to elevate the recessed portion of the collarbone.  Then, correct and straighten the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the collarbone has broken off and is moving, you should carefully remove it. If the fragment is embedded in the bone, you must carefully cut it out using one of the instruments prepared for this purpose. After placing the instrument that protects the diaphragm beneath the collarbone, an instrument made of wood or iron, which is illustrated as follows:
It resembles a spoon, without a curve. Its width should be adjusted according to the size of the bone, and its length should also be determined according to the work you need to do. It should have two ends, as you see one end wider, and the other narrower.
If the wound you made when removing the bone fragment is wide and inflammation is present, suture the edges of the wound. If the wound is minor or if you fear severe inflammation, close the wound with sutures and dressings appropriate for the size of the wound.
If a hot inflammation appears, soak the dressing in rosewater and vinegar or a drink, and apply it.  As for bracing a bone, if there is no wound or incision, apply a dressing made from mill dust and egg white to the bone. Place a soft pad over it, and then place a ball under his armpit if needed.  Then take a very long turban, with a width of approximately one span, or so.  Then apply a folded bandage over the pad and dressing.  Then take a splint made of thin board. Make it [the splint] three fingers wide and the same length. Then wrap it in a cloth, and then wrap that cloth and splint around the part of the turban that covers the fracture site.  Then wrap the turban around the fracture, just as it is wound around. And tie it around his neck, and under his healthy armpit and under his injured armpit, returning it repeatedly to each side.  How you see that the tightening secures the fracture firmly is clear to you.
And the entire purpose is that the splint should not move from the fractured bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day.  Whenever the ligament relaxes and you see the splint has moved, correct it and tighten the bandage.  Then make the patient sleep on their back, and place a small pillow under their armpit while they sleep at night.
To elevate his arm from his side, so the fractured clavicle rises with the shoulder or tie his arm to his neck.  Do not loosen the bandage unless a wound or swelling occurs at the site within twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see cause for it. Maintain the tightening and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone sets and becomes firm, strengthening further in twenty-eight days. And in some people, it may be in less than that.
Chapter Six.
On the reduction of a fractured shoulder.  The shoulder rarely fractures in its wider portion, but rather its parts fracture.  If a part or the middle is fractured, this is determined by touch; then, according to the nature of the fracture, apply a splint.
Reduce it and return it to its natural shape by any means possible, then apply mill dust, egg white, and soft plaster to the area. Place a folded cloth bandage over that, and then apply a wide splint of wood.
