Make the splint as wide as the entire shoulder or slightly wider.  If, under the splint, there is a protrusion on any part of the shoulder, smooth that protrusion with soft plaster so that the splint lies flat.  Then secure it from above with a long bandage.
Tighten the bandage firmly and secure the splint so it doesn't shift.  Examine the bandage daily; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it moves.  Let the patient lie on their sound side.  The shoulder will be healed in twenty days.
One or twenty-five days. When this time is complete, remove the bandage and you will be secure, for it is among the bones that one need not fear its splintering or displacement. If a fragment of bone protrudes, and it is piercing the skin, then cut over it and remove it. Do as described for a fractured collarbone in order to relieve any inflammation, if such a thing occurs.
Chapter Seven.
In setting a fractured rib.
The chest may break in the middle, though this is rare.  Its extremities, however, are more likely to fracture and splinter.  Among its symptoms, if the middle breaks, it will tilt downward, and severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing will occur.
Perhaps blood will be coughed up, and the depression of the fractured bone will become apparent to the touch.  To set it, the patient must lie on their back, with a cushion between their shoulder blades.  Then, their sides must be compressed, and the ribs gathered together with the hands from both sides.  Care must be taken to straighten them out on every possible aspect.
And hold until the bone returns to its proper shape.  Then apply bandages and splints and place a splint of thin, light wood from poplar, willow, or the like, wrapped in cloth, on top. Then, carefully tie it to the fractured bone so it does not shift, and wrap the bandage around the back several times, firmly tightening it.  The bandage should be checked frequently, and tightened whenever it loosens.
If the injury requires it, such as due to ulceration, pain, or swelling, immediately remove it, take off the bandage, and treat any condition that has developed. Then replace the bandage if it is deemed appropriate. Apply this to the affected area and keep it firm until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
In the setting of fractured ribs, know that fractures typically occur in the thick parts of the ribs adjacent to the spine and along their anterior edges.  This is because these areas are cartilaginous and easily injured.  Recognition of this is obvious to touch when palpating.
To treat it, align the fracture with the fingers in the appropriate manner until the shape is restored. Then bandage and splint the fractured bone if necessary. If the rib fracture is inward, it will cause severe pain.
And the pain is like the pain experienced by someone with pleurisy, for the bone punctures the diaphragm, causing difficulty breathing, coughing, and significant spitting of blood.  This is a difficult condition to treat, and many solutions have been tried in the past.
Some have said that the patient's diet should include foods that produce gas and wind, to inflate the abdomen and push the fracture outward. We do not support this, for fear of exacerbating inflammation if it has not already occurred, and worsening it if it has.
Some have said to apply a cupping glass to the area and then vigorously suction it. While this seems logical, there is concern that the cupping glass might inadvertently draw more fluids to the weakened area.  Others have said that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
The ribs are then stitched together until they fill out, creating an even band when wrapped around.  Thereafter, the patient is treated for pleurisy, through diet and medication.  If the patient is severely afflicted, it is unbearable. The bone was piercing the diaphragm painfully, and we feared for the patient.  Therefore, we should incise the area, expose the fractured rib, then place beneath it the instrument that protects the peritoneum, which we have previously described.  We should gently cut and remove the bone.
Then, if the wound is large, we stitch the edges together and treat it with ointments until it heals.  If inflammation occurs during this time, immediately apply rose-oil-soaked bandages to the affected area and treat the patient internally as well to relieve the inflammation.
He should lie on the side on which he sleeps more easily until he is healed.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Concerning the bones of the neck, if a fracture occurs, this is infrequent.  More often, the neck is injured by contusions.  The same is true for the vertebrae of the spine.  If such an injury occurs to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not... If you observe that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and lifeless, and he cannot move, extend, or close them; and if you pinch or prick them with a needle, he feels no sensation or pain, then know that in most cases, he will not recover, and he is likely to die.
However, if he can move them and feels the pinching and pricking, know that the spinal cord has been spared, and the patient will recover with treatment.  If a similar injury occurs to the vertebrae of the back, and you want to know if it will also recover...
Then examine his legs. If you see that they have become relaxed and show the same signs as the hands, and when he lies on his back, urine and feces are expelled involuntarily, and when he lies on his stomach, urine is expelled involuntarily... And if he lies on his back and attempts to urinate, he cannot do so, then know that he is doomed, and do not bother with treatment.  If none of these symptoms occur, the matter is less serious.  Treatment for such cases involves trying to relieve the inflamed swelling.
By applying rose oil, alone or mixed with roasted egg yolks, to the affected vertebrae three times a day, until the inflammation subsides.  Then apply one of the strengthening, dry dressings to the area and bind it with a bandage.  Instruct him to rest and remain still.
He should sleep only on the side that does not cause pain until he is healed. If a splinter or something else detached from the bone during the injury, it is necessary to cut through the skin and remove that detached bone. Then, if the wound is large, stitch the edges together.  Then treat with healing ointments until healed.  If the last bone of the coccyx (tailbone) is fractured, then it is necessary to insert the little finger of the left hand into the rectum.
Align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible and achieve alignment.  Then apply a dressing and splint, if necessary, and bind it tightly.
If you feel a fractured splinter within it, cut open the area and remove it.  Treat the wound with the previous methods until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a hip fracture.
Hip bones rarely fracture. If they do, the fracture involves fragmentation at the extremities, longitudinal splitting, and inward tilting.  The affected individual experiences pain, stabbing, and numbness in the leg on the fractured side.
To set it, run your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture.  If the fracture is only at the extremities, then adjust the fracture to resemble its original shape as best as possible.
If the fracture is longitudinal or has tilted inward, lay the patient on their stomach to facilitate setting the fracture.  Once you have adjusted it, apply a bandage, then a splint of wood or leather and secure it tightly.
Do not fear the fracture shifting or the splint loosening.  Fill in any hollows in the lower back with padding to ensure the splint lies flat.  Instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their sound side, if a hot swelling appears.
Refrain from manipulating or setting it until the hot swelling subsides.  Apply what is appropriate to soothe it, as previously mentioned.  Then resume setting and securing it properly.  If fragments or splinters are present in the bone, they should not be removed.
Do not touch them, but adjust them from the outside, as we said, and leave the securing in place until healing occurs.
Chapter Eleven.
On the setting of a fractured humerus.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder.  If it is fractured, set it by one of two methods: one is to take a curved, smooth stick of medium thickness, in this form:
And tie straps to its ends.  Then, suspend it from a high point. The patient sits on a chair, and then places their fractured arm over the stick so that their armpit is positioned at the middle curve of the stick. Then, hang a heavy object from above it.
Or, an attendant lowers it (the arm) and the physician straightens the fracture with his hands until the fracture is set correctly.  The other method is that the patient lies on their back and their arm is suspended from their neck by a strap.
Then, instruct two attendants: one to hold the upper part of the fracture in their hands and the other to hold the lower part, and each of them to pull in their respective direction.  If you want the pulling to be stronger, tie a strap beneath the fracture and one above it, and have each attendant pull in their respective direction.
If the fracture is near the shoulder, the middle of the strap should be under the armpit, and the other under the fracture towards the elbow; likewise, if the fracture is near the elbow, the straps should be in that location and at the elbow itself.
Then, gently set the fracture without force, until the fracture is properly aligned and well-united.  Then tighten it, unless a hot swelling arises.  If a hot swelling arises, leave the tightening until the seventh day. Apply soft, dampened wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides.  Then tighten it.  The method of tightening is to place the bandage over the fracture, then place a new cloth wrap over the bandage. Then bring the arm up to the upper arm and place the hand open against the shoulder.
Hold the cloths and the tightening on the upper arm and the arm, so that the arm acts as splints if nothing prevents you from doing so and if nothing of the fractured bone has changed for you. If you fear that something of it will change for you, then use splints.
Place a splint over the fracture itself, one that is wider and stronger than the other splints.  Let there be a finger's width between each splint.  Let the length of the splints be according to the fracture, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tightening mentioned in the first section.
The tightening should be on the fracture site, the strongest.
The farther from the fracture, the less the tightening.  If you see the placement of the splints and the tightening as we described in the moment of setting the limb, then do it.
If you fear a hot swelling, leave the tightening and splints until the seventh day, as we said. Then examine the ligaments every three days, lest a rash or puffiness occur, or food be prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightening.  Correct all of that as we mentioned.
If you are certain that none of that will occur, do not loosen the bandages until many days later.  The patient should lie on their back, their hand on their stomach.  A padded, moderate arm sling filled with wool should be placed under the armpit.
Examine at all times, night or day, lest the fractured limb's shape shifts or the bandage loosens.  Correct all of this with your effort.  Make the patient's diet as we previously described, first gentle, until the bone is strong.
Then the patient's diet should be made thicker.  The arm and leg usually become strong in forty days. At that time, the bandages should be loosened, and a bath used, and treated with ointments suitable for this, if the fracture is a severe one.
Do not loosen the bandages until fifty days or two months.
The Twelfth Chapter.
On the setting of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the radius.  One is small, being the one nearest the thumb, and the other is large, being positioned under the smaller one, at its lower end.  Sometimes only the larger radius is fractured, or the smaller one alone, or both may be fractured.
When the upper small radius is fractured, its setting is easy and its healing quicker. When the lower radius is fractured, the fracture is bad, and healing difficult. The worst case is when both bones are fractured. If the upper small radius is fractured,
Therefore, the physician, when setting it, should gently apply a slight pull to align it.  If the large radius is fractured, a stronger pull is necessary. If both radii are fractured, an exceptionally strong pull is necessary.
The hand should be positioned, when setting and pulling, stretched out on a cushion. The thumb of the hand should be positioned higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers.  The patient should sit upright.
The cushion should be at a height so that the patient does not exert himself.
Then, one attendant supports the arm from below, either by hand or with a bandage, and another attendant supports it from above.  Then, the physician aligns the bone until it is restored to the best possible shape.  If the fracture has splinters, they are carefully removed.
Return each splinter to its place to the best of your ability.  If a splinter protrudes, pierces the skin, and cannot be set, make an incision over it and remove it using the method described previously.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, a separate section has been dedicated to this.
Treat any resulting inflammation from the initial setting with a poultice of rose oil and white wax, applied on a linen cloth of medium thickness.  Apply gentle pressure until the swelling subsides.
Remove the poultice and apply a bandage made from mill dust and egg white.  Then apply the splints.  The splint over the fractured area should be wider and stronger.  Know that the arm usually requires six splints.
The fracture may be in one radius or both radii.  Then, make the traction over the fracture site stronger and tighter.  As you increase the traction upwards or downwards, gradually lessen the tension, as described at the beginning of this section.
Use soft, damp linen bandages to wrap the fracture; they should not be excessively stiff.  The thread used for traction should be linen, of medium thickness as previously described.  Inspect the limb and ligaments after several days.
If any irritation develops on the limb, such as itching, it should be soothed with warm water.  The limb should be left untensioned for a night to rest, then traction resumed.
If the traction has become slack and the bone has shifted, or something similar has occurred, correct it to the best of your ability.  Also, if the limb is deprived of nourishment due to excessive traction, reduce the tension slightly and leave it for several days to allow nourishment to reach it, then re-apply traction.
If no problem mentioned above arises, the splint should not be removed for twenty days or thereabouts. Then, suspend the patient's arm from their neck, keeping the arm straight.  The patient must avoid strenuous or erratic movements.  The patient should sleep on their back.
Know that this arm fracture is typically set within thirty, or thirty-two days, perhaps even twenty-eight days, all depending on the individual's temperament and strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On setting fractures of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpals and phalanges rarely fracture, but are often bruised.  When the hand suffers a fracture or a bruise...
The patient should sit on the ground, with a chair in front of him/her, level with the ground.
Then, placing the hand outstretched on the chair, the bone-setter extends the fractured bone and the physician straightens it until it is properly aligned.  Then, if no hot swelling occurs, the bandage and splint should be applied, followed by a splint to cover the area.
It should be enclosed in soft cloth. If the fracture is towards the palm of the hand, make a ball-shaped form of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold it with their fractured hand, and then tie it with a long cloth.
Let the splint be of soft leather, so that the skin can heal while the entire hand is bent; and tie it as is necessary.  If the fracture is outwards, then a splint should be placed above and another below the hand to keep the hand open and upright.
Then, the splint is tightened as the hand turns, and the fingers are bound together with bandages. If the fracture is in one of the fingers, if it is the thumb, it should be straightened correctly and tied with the hand, or if desired, a small upright splint should be made to support the fracture.
It should not be moved. If the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, index, ring, or pinky fingers, they should be straightened and bound with the adjacent healthy finger, or all bound together, which is better, or a small upright splint placed on them.
As we said regarding the thumb, and examine during and after the setting of your fracture, and treat any swelling if necessary, as we have repeatedly described.
Fourteenth Chapter.
On the setting of a fractured femur.
The thighbone often fractures and is easily felt, as it shifts back and forth.  Its setting involves tying a bandage above the fracture and another below, with the patient lying on their face.
Then each attendant pulls the bandage taut in its direction, ensuring that the bandage is straight if the fracture is in the middle of the bone, but if it is near the origin of the thigh, a soft bandage made of wool or similar material should be used.
Near the thigh's origin, towards the pubic area, the pull should be upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Likewise, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be placed near the knee to pull downwards, then the physician aligns the bone with both hands, restoring it to its natural shape.
The bone will heal well.  Then, you should maintain the dressing and tension, unless a hot swelling develops in the limb. If a swelling occurs, leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then resume treatment.
For the tensioning, a wide, firm bandage should be wrapped around the fracture two or three times, leaving any excess.  Then the leg is wrapped until the heel is at the origin of the fibula.  A long thread is inserted between the thigh and the leg, near the knee from below, and the ends of the thread are brought to the top on both sides.
Then wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.  Next, place splints on the thigh at the fracture site itself, and make a single splint on the leg bone.  Then fill the space between the thigh and leg with soft padding to ensure even tension.
Then begin with strong tension at the center of the fracture site, wrapping three or four times.  As you move the bandage away from the fracture site, the tension should be less, gentler, and looser.
Then, take the ends of the thread you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie them to the overlying splints.  Then, bring the thread ends down to the ankle of the leg, and use them to also tighten the ends of the splints on the other side.
So that the bandage does not shift from its place, then maintain the tension as long as the limb shows no pain, swelling, or inflammation, or similar.  If anything of the sort occurs, promptly loosen it and rectify whatever happened as we have described repeatedly.
If there is a splinter of bone protruding, then smooth it out if possible; otherwise, split the skin over it and remove it. Treat the wound as previously described until it heals.  Sometimes, this thigh fracture can be treated without including the leg in the splints.
As we have mentioned for the humerus and forearm, however,  treating it in this way will not cause the patient to limp.  If you only splint the [affected] limb without including the leg, the patient will certainly limp.
Know that the thigh takes fifty days or a little more or a little less to heal, depending on the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
In the fracture of the kneecap.
The kneecap rarely fractures, but it is often bruised. If a fracture does occur, it is either a crack or a fragmentation of its parts, and this may occur with or without a wound.
All of this is determined by examination.  The fracture is treated by aligning the separated pieces with the fingers until they come together as well as possible through gentle and careful repositioning.  Then a bandage and a circular splint are applied if necessary.
Further compression is applied according to what is appropriate.  Then all cases, like the one described in other fractures, such as inflammation and the like, are managed, each condition being addressed with the appropriate treatment until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the treatment of a fractured leg.
The leg has two bones: one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the ulna.  They are subject to the same types of fractures as the arm bones. Therefore, its treatment is like that of the arm, and the procedure is the same.  If both bones are fractured, the leg is displaced in all directions.  If the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is displaced forward.  If the thicker bone is fractured, and this happens from below, that is obvious to you.
So use traction and alignment, and bind the splints accordingly, but if the leg fracture is severe, with many fragments, the traction should be less and gentler, and apply less force to the reduction.
In treating the leg, there is something extra compared to the arm.  After aligning the splints and completing all your work, take two pieces of pinewood, similar to those used for smoothing walls or palm leaves, and place them between the gaps of the boards.
Choose pieces that are slightly thick, not thin, and make them the length of the leg from the knee down.  Then wrap each one with two layers of cloth along its length, placing one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let the length be from the knee to the bottom of the foot.  Then tie the two pieces at three points, at the ends and the middle.  With this arrangement, the leg will be prevented from tilting sideways and will be well-supported.  A wooden trough along the leg might also be used.
Place it [the leg] in this [trough] to prevent movement, and this is especially important if there is a wound as well.  Then examine the leg each day carefully for swelling or puffiness, or any other such issue. If any of this occurs, address it appropriately until it heals.  The leg bone will be set in thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
In fractures of the leg and fingers.
The heelbone is rarely fractured.  The bones of the leg may be fractured, and the fingers are also seldom fractured; rather, they are usually bruised.
If a fracture of the leg bones occurs and you see the bones protruding, have the patient place their foot flat on the ground, like walking. Then, place your foot on the protruding bones and press them down to align them.
Until they return to their proper position. Then, take the bandage and splint, place a small, flat board with two ends under the sole of the foot, and firmly bandage the foot with cloth and whatever else you need.
After three or four days, release the bandage. You will find the bones aligned, whether they were fractured or dislocated. The procedure is as described.  If some of the fingers are broken, set them and align them as described for setting the fingers of the hand.
Then, make a splint for the broken finger that is the length of the finger and slightly wider.  Place the board you described under the foot and bandage it tightly. If two, three, or more bones are broken.
Make a splint for each finger, appropriate in size and wrapped in soft cloth.  Bandage the board to the bottom of the foot, ensuring that each end of the board extends outside the sole of the foot for proper support.
You should not forget to treat all fractures with the symptoms we described, and address each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
In fractures of the woman's pubic bone and the man's pubic bone and penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, have her sit in a squatting position, then gently lean her back slightly, holding her from behind.  The midwife should then pack the vulva with cotton until it is full and feels like a ball inside.
Then, gently rock the woman and raise her pelvis slightly, and the cotton will gradually come out, forming a ball at the entrance of the vulva, and the broken bone will mend.  Then, place a pillow under her back.  When she wishes to urinate, gently remove the cotton to allow her to urinate.
Return it to its original position as it was first inserted, then return to her original pillow arrangement.  Do this for seven days or so, and it will heal. If you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube of reed to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva.
Then blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder inflates inside the vulva, and the fracture will mend. Afterward, pack it with cotton and continue the care as described until it heals.  Similarly, if the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, the same procedure is employed in its repair.
And the treatment is similar to what we described for fractures of the hip bone.  The proper approach to these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, is readily apparent to someone skilled and knowledgeable of this book, since the fracture itself often provides guidance.
For the correct method of treating a fractured penis, understand this:  If a man's penis is fractured, take a goose's trachea, insert the penis into it, then wrap a bandage around it.  Bind it tightly and leave it for three days or so until healed.
Chapter Nineteen.
On setting broken bones when there is a wound.
For someone who presents with a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur or humerus, or similar, one should immediately perform venesection if the conditions for venesection, as previously mentioned, permit.
If the wound is bleeding, then one should quickly cauterize it with powdered alum, if nothing else is available.  Then, begin the reduction of the fracture on that very same day, and do not delay it.
If no inflammatory swelling occurs, then if inflammatory swelling does occur, leave the reduction until the ninth day, so that the inflammatory swelling subsides.  Do not approach it at all on the third and fourth day, for it will expose it to bad symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin, exposed, then you should carefully try to return it to its place and smooth it with your hands, with gentle, slight extension. If you cannot return it and smooth it with your hands, then return it using this instrument, which is made of iron, and its length is about seven or eight fingers. And its width should match the wound.
Therefore, the physician should make three or four of them, according to the need for treatment in each type of fracture, and let them be round, with a slight thickness so that they do not bend when struck during use.
And they should be sharp-pointed, with a bend at one end, thicker at the top and gradually thinner towards the bottom.  This is its shape:
And it is called in Greek "perem", meaning a small lever.  The sharp, bent end of the instrument should be placed on the protruding bone end and used to push it back until the bone is properly aligned and the fracture ends are brought into contact.
If the fractured end is thin and the instrument does not grip it well, then the end of that bone must be cut so that the instrument can hold it.  If the bone cannot be realigned as described, cut it with similar instruments as mentioned.
Or, saw it with one of the saws, as you can. Then remove any remaining roughness or thin scales from the bone.  If, after realignment, the patient experiences intense, painful discomfort, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can realign it to its natural position, do so, as you will greatly benefit the patient. Once the bone is properly set, dip a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer.  Do not place any ointments or anything greasy on the wound.
So that putrefaction and decay do not occur. Then use splints when you are finished setting the bone, and leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, to fit the size of the wound.
Beware, be extremely cautious, not to tighten the wound with the fracture.  Often, unskilled physicians do this, causing their patients either death, or ulcers, or colds.  Your tightening should be gentle and relaxed, opposite to the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we described, and the patient is experiencing distressing pain in the area, then you should not apply splints.  Make bandages of tough cloth for the area of the splints, and secure it with them.
If, after a day or two, you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth soaked in the drink that you placed on it. Then use the dressings and ointments we customarily use to treat wounds, such as the four-part ointment, and the like.  You should also loosen the bandage.
Examine the wound every evening and morning until it heals and is cured.  The limb should be positioned so that pus drains downwards easily.  If the wound takes many days to heal and the pus does not stop, know that there are small fragments of bone.
You should probe the wound with a probe.  Remove any such fragments that are loose.  If any fragments are not loose, and are piercing the limb and causing pain, cut them and remove them in any way possible.
If the wound develops a cold, or eating, or any other type of corruption or putrefaction, you should counteract each symptom with the corresponding treatment described in its chapter.
If a large, prominent bone such as the femur, humerus, or similar large bone is fractured, you should not attempt to pull it out or remove it. Often death results from this.  Leave it until it becomes infected; perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On the treatment of complications arising from some fractures.
This complication often arises after the fracture heals, especially if it is near a joint. It spoils the appearance of the limb and may prevent the limb from its natural function.
If the complication is recent, use astringent remedies such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, rue, acacia, and similar substances, taking some or all of them and mixing them with an astringent potion or egg white or vinegar.
Apply it to the complication, tightly bandaging it.  Leave the bandage on for several days, then remove it and repeat the process until the complication subsides, God willing, or apply a firm lead plate over it.
Lead has the property of removing all growths on the limbs. If the complication has hardened and become severe, and necessity dictates its removal, cut it from above and remove the excess growth or scrape it away with a suitable instrument until it is gone.  Treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of fractures, if the limb is, after being set, still thin and not as it was originally.
If a fractured bone is set and the limb remains thin and weak afterward, this may be due to many reasons. One reason is either excessive loosening and binding of the ligaments improperly, or excessive tightening of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb, or excessive poulticing, or excessive movements at inappropriate times, or insufficient and weak blood supply in the patient's body.
The treatment for this involves nourishing the patient and strengthening his body to increase the blood supply, using baths, bringing joy and happiness, and similar measures.  Then, applying asphalt to the limb to attract much nourishment to it, or continuing to poultice with lukewarm water. So that nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-two.
On the treatment of fractured bones, if they are reduced crookedly, preventing them from functioning properly.
So when a limb, after being reduced, shows crookedness, or a protrusion of the fractured bone, or a twisting, and the appearance of the limb is thus disfigured. However, if the limb is not prevented from its natural function, it is not appropriate to accept the assertion of those who claim to break the limb from the head, and many ignorant physicians and bone-setters in our country have done so.
This action is highly reprehensible and leads to great risk, possibly resulting in injury. However, if the crookedness and twisting are recent, it is appropriate to apply water in which relaxing herbs such as plantain leaves and root, and king's crown, and the like, have been boiled.
And apply relaxing dressings such as meticulously crafted diachylon, or take plantain root sap and mix with chicken fat and shea butter, and apply that.  Or, take ripe figs, grind them with pigeon droppings, and similar medicines called resolvents.
And the twisting will resolve with gentle, constant massage with the hands, and the limb should be moved in every direction at all times. However, if the crookedness is old, severe, and rigid, and treatment with iron is necessary.
Then, it is necessary to split it open, release the bone connection, and cut away any remaining crookedness or excess bone using delicate incisions.  Use care and attention in this process. Afterward, treat the wound with the previously mentioned remedies until healed.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is the displacement of a joint from its position, hindering movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing the afflicted intense pain and suffering.  Therefore, when a person experiences a dislocated jaw, one must promptly attempt to relocate it.
Delay this not at all, for if delayed, swelling will occur at the affected location, forming a mass that makes relocation of the jaw difficult.  Therefore, it must not be delayed; it should not be moved or stretched while swollen, as this often leads to severe spasms and painful distress in the patient.
However, if this occurs, the patient should first be bled, then allowed to rest until the swelling subsides somewhat.  Then, the affected area should be applied with warm water and ointment.  Finally, the jaw should be gently relocated, and each affected area treated as described in its respective section.
The chapters on the jaw are also arranged, as previously discussed, from the upper to the lower parts of the body.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of the lower jaw.
The jaws dislocate rarely.  Their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they move slightly from their positions, relaxing a little; or they completely and entirely dislocate, relaxing completely. Toward the chest, so that the patient's saliva drips and he cannot retain it, nor can he close his jaw or speak coherently; but if the dislocation is slight, it will often return to its place easily.
However, if the dislocation is complete, it is necessary to return it quickly and not delay.  A servant should hold the patient's head, and the physician should place the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
Or if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, he should place both thumbs inside, and the rest of his fingers outside, to adjust it. He should instruct the patient to relax his jaw and allow it to go in every direction. The physician then adjusts the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its position.
If returning them is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, apply warm water and ointment compresses to make their return easier. Do not delay their return, as we have said.  If they return to their proper position and the patient's mouth is closed but still relaxed, then apply linen dressings, and a mixture of wax, ointment, and rose.
Then gently tie with a loose bandage.  The patient should sleep on their back, with their head raised between two pillows so that they do not move it to either side, nor chew anything.  Their diet should consist of soft, easily digestible foods.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is healed, they may eat as they please.  They should use gentleness and not force their mouth open when eating, drinking, or yawning, until the jaw is healed and recovered.
If the jaws, once dislocated, do not return to their proper positions, significant fever, persistent headaches, and possible abdominal distress, and perhaps repeated vomiting, may occur.  If you observe this, know that it is serious.
Many die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the reduction of the clavicle and shoulder joint.
The clavicle does not usually dislocate inward due to its connection to the chest. It may dislocate outward, which is evident to the touch.  To reduce it, the patient should lie on their back with their arms extended.
Then press the area firmly with your hand; it will return.  Then apply bandages and splints, and secure them.  As for the part of the clavicle near the shoulder, it rarely dislocates. If it does dislocate, it should be reduced as described above, to the best of your ability.
Then apply bandages, splints, and dressings, and instruct the patient to remain still and quiet until healed.  Using this same treatment, the shoulder joint can also be reduced if it is displaced.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On reducing the dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the shoulder joint dislocates in three ways: one, downwards towards the armpit; two, towards the chest; and three, upwards over the shoulder, although this last is rare.
It does not dislocate to the back of the shoulder blade, nor to the front where the nerve is located.  Most commonly, it dislocates downwards towards the armpit, particularly in those with less muscle, because in them, it quickly exits and quickly re-enters.
However, in those with abundant muscle, the opposite is true; that is, it exits with difficulty and re-enters with difficulty.  Sometimes, a person may experience an injury or fall, resulting in a swollen, warm shoulder, which may be mistaken for dislocation.  It is necessary to examine this thoroughly to determine the correct diagnosis.
Then, in treating it, if the dislocation is downwards towards the armpit, when you compare the dislocated shoulder to the healthy one, you will find a noticeable difference.  You will find the head of the dislocated shoulder is tilted, and under the armpit, upon palpation, the head of the dislocated shoulder will feel like an egg.
And the injured person will not be able to raise their arm to their ear, nor to perform any other movements of the arm.  Likewise, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, this will also be evident upon palpation.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  To reduce it, have an attendant raise the arm upwards. Then, place your thumb under the patient's armpit and pull the joint forcefully upwards, back into place, while the attendant raises and extends the arm upwards.
Then, lower it downwards, and it will return quickly. If it does not return as described, and the dislocation has been present for many days, the patient should bathe in warm water and use remedies that relax and soften tissues, such as boiling fennel, fenugreek, and king's crown in water, and apply it.
Then the patient lies on their back, and a ball of wool of moderate firmness is placed under the armpit.  The physician places their foot on the ball, grasps the patient's arm with their hand, and extends it downwards.
And the physician pushes the ball with their foot, and the joint will return to its place.  Alternatively, if you prefer, have a man taller than the patient stand beside the patient on the affected side.  The taller man should place their shoulder under the patient's armpit and lift the armpit upwards, so that the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant pulls the patient's arm downwards towards their abdomen. If the patient is light-weight, something should be tied to them to make them heavier, which will cause the joint to return at once.  The dislocation can also be reduced in other ways.
Another method is to place a long, round-headed wooden beam on the ground, like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin.  A soft cloth is placed on the top of the beam, and the patient stands along the beam with the beam placed under the patient's armpit.
Then the patient extends their arm downwards on the opposite side, and also extends their body forcefully on the other side, and the joint will return to its place quickly.  If the joint is difficult to reduce with all the methods mentioned previously, then use this treatment.  Take a piece of wood approximately two cubits long. And its width should be about four fingers, and its thickness about two fingers.  It should have a rounded head so that it can easily be inserted into the armpit in this manner:
Then a soft cloth is tied to the rounded head so that the wood does not harm the patient.  Then it is placed under the armpit, and the entire arm and forearm are extended along the wood downwards. The wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and wrist.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbar.  Extend the hand downwards, leaving the rest of the body hanging from the other side. The joint will then enter its place. If it enters in any way possible, a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then a bandage prepared from mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the entire shoulder area, circling over it.  Then the ball is tied firmly under the armpit. The bandage is wrapped around the ball from above and the arm is suspended from the neck.
The arm is left undisturbed for seven days.  The patient's diet should be light, to strengthen the limb and promote healing more quickly.  Then, after seven or five days, the bandage is removed and the arm is exercised. If it is firm and does not relax, then it is healed.
If the joint frequently dislocates due to moisture or another cause, cauterization with three strips of linen should be used, as described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint still does not hold,
If the bandage and binding are repeated several times, but the joint does not hold, and it slips and relaxes, and the patient cannot raise the arm, then know that the nerve in the shoulder head has been severed, stretched, or relaxed.  In that case, know that the joint will never hold in place.
For a dislocated jaw, located near the chest and breast and towards the back, it should be pushed and pulled with the hands until it returns.  All other binding and treatment should be used until it heals. If, after healing, there is a feeling of soreness in the area and slow movement,
The patient should use a hot bath repeatedly until the soreness subsides and returns to its normal state.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of a dislocated elbow.
The elbow joint is difficult to dislocate and also difficult to relocate.  It can dislocate in all directions, especially forward or backward.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, as it is visible and palpable, regardless of how it has dislocated.
If you compare the dislocated elbow to the healthy one, the obvious depression of the joint will be apparent.  The arm cannot be bent, nor can the shoulder be brought to the elbow.  You should immediately relocate the dislocation before a hot swelling appears.
If a hot swelling appears, its relocation will be difficult, and it may never heal, especially if the dislocation is backward. This is the worst type of dislocation, the most painful, and often leads to death.
If it is possible to relocate it, the attendant should extend both of his arms, the physician's hand should be placed above and below the elbow, and the joint should be pushed back into place using all thumbs or the base of the hands.
If the dislocation is forward, the hand may be bent once so that the base of the palm strikes the corresponding shoulder.  If this doesn't relocate the dislocation, use very strong and forceful extension, having two attendants extend the arm.
The patient is also held by two attendants to prevent movement during the extension. Then the arm is rotated in every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or a wide bandage around it.  If the physician uses both hands to grasp the joint, he should apply ointment to make the joint easier to slip back into place.
Then the joint is forcefully pushed back into place. After relocation, a bandage containing a binding and drying agent, along with egg white, should be applied tightly. The arm should be suspended from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is untied.  If the joint is firmly in place, the bandage is removed and the patient is released. If you see that the joint has not become strong, repeat the bandage and the binding and leave it for some days until it is firm, then untie it.  If the patient experiences pain after the joint is returned and untied,
If movement is difficult, use moist heat in the bath, gentle massage, and probing until it becomes flexible.  Alternatively, place a soft, fat-like pad on the joint, bind it, and leave it in place for one day and one night, then remove it and take the patient to the bath. Once the patient sweats, gently massage the joint.
Then repeat the pad application a second and third time, along with bathing, until it becomes flexible. If you prefer, place moist, ground cow dung mixed with ghee on the joint and bind it. Repeat this several times, as it will help it become flexible and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easier than other joints, but it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the moment of dislocation before inflammation or a hot swelling develops in the area.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board, have an assistant extend the arm, and the physician should place their hand on the bony prominence of the joint and push until it returns.  However, it is important to check if the dislocation has shifted to the inside of the hand.
Let the patient place the outside of their hand on the board during the extension and reduction. If the dislocation is outward, the inside of the hand should be on the board so the physician's hand is over the same bony prominence.  If it returns immediately, do so; otherwise, secure it with a dressing that relieves the swelling.
Leave it alone; do not try again, as it cannot tolerate further attempts.  After several days, reduction is impossible, and the joint will remain crooked.  This does not harm the patient, unless the hand becomes weak and cannot grip anything.
In that case, you know that the nerve is severed or bruised, and there is no remedy but to apply cauterization.  It may or may not help.  After reducing the wrist, apply the dressing we described, then bandage it and leave it for five days.
Then it is removed, and the hand is exercised.  If its movement is impaired and there is some stiffness, soften it with heated water and massage repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction.  If a finger dislocates towards the palm's surface or its underside, stretch the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumb until it returns.  Then tie the finger's head and suspend it towards the direction it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and stretch it until it stands upright that day. If it is night, tie it in the same way.  Continue to loosen it during the day and exercise it with movement, and tie it at night.  Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Likewise, if it dislocates to the underside of the hand, tie it towards the same direction and do to it your initial procedure until it heals.  Likewise, do this for any other direction of dislocation.
Chapter Thirty.
On treating a dislocated vertebra.
When a vertebra in the back or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no cure, for death quickly comes to the patient.
A sign of this is that the patient's stool is expelled involuntarily; he cannot control it.  Often, some of his limbs, either his legs or arms or one of them, become relaxed.  However, if only one vertebra is displaced, it often returns to its place.
And its displacement can be in four directions.  That which displaces to the rear is called a hump.  If the hump developed from childhood, there is no cure for it, and it will never heal.  However, if it developed from a fall, a blow, or something similar...
The ancients described various treatments with lengthy explanations, much of which is ultimately useless.  I have shortened this to a concise summary, sufficient to replace their extensive methods. I have clarified the meaning, explained it, and illustrated the mechanism differently from how they described and explained it.
So I say that a hump that forms in the front of the chest cannot be treated, nor can one that forms to the sides.  Only the one that forms on the back can be treated, and the method I am describing is for the patient to lie on his face.
On a flat surface near a wall, spread a damp cloth beneath him so that his chest is not harmed.  Then place a vertical plank, embedded in a hole in the ground, near his head, and another plank near his feet, at the opposite end of the surface.
A servant holds one plank, which is not fastened in the hole, and another servant holds the other plank in the same position.  Then a soft, strong bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under their armpits.  The end of the bandage is stretched to the plank near the patient's head and tied to it.
Then another bandage is tightened around his hips, knees, and ankles.  All the bandages are gathered and tied to the other plank near his feet.  Each servant pulls on the plank in their direction, and the planks remain in their embedded positions.
However, the planks are not secured, as we said.  The physician places his hands firmly on the hump, until it returns, or places a board on it and then leans on the board with his legs until it returns. If this treatment is unsuccessful, take a board about three cubits long.
A hole is dug in the wall, near the patient, into which the end of the board is inserted. The middle of the board is placed on the hump.  The physician places his feet on the other end of the board and pulls firmly to compress the hump.
And it returns to its place.  If you wish, you can do this with a hand-wound spiral; that is, you drive two planks into the ground, one near the patient's head at the end of the shop, and two near his feet at the other end of the shop, each a cubit long.
Let there be a span between each plank.  A hole is made in each plank through which the spiral is passed. All the wood is securely fastened to the ground so that it does not move at all. A curved stick, the spiral, in which the bandage is wound, is inserted into the holes in both planks.
At its end is a hole secured with a stick a span long, wound in, and the same is done in the other two planks. Then the bandage which was tied around the patient's chest is tightened on the spiral near his head, and the bandages tied around his legs are tightened on the spiral near his feet.
Then a servant stands by each spiral, turning the hand-crank that winds the spiral. The physician adjusts the hump as previously described. This is a picture of the spiral, the shop, and the patient.
Then, after the swelling subsides and the area is level, the dried bandage should be applied, moistened with egg white, then a compress.  Then, place a splint made of a board over the bandage, with a width of about three fingers, and a length covering the affected lump.
And over some healthy beads, then tied with the necessary bandage.  The patient should eat a soft diet until healed.  If some swelling remains in the area after healing, then the medicines that relax and soften the tissues should be used, along with the board we described, for a long time.
A lead plate may be used in this case.  If a lump appears at the end of the spine beads, one might suspect a dislocation, but it could be an extra bone that has protruded.  Do not apply this treatment, as death may result.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and not to the slight displacement and subluxation that occur in other joints.  The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inward, outward, forward, and backward.  It dislocates inward most often, and rarely forward or backward.  A sign of an inward dislocation is that if you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg will appear longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient will be unable to bend their leg at the groin.  The area adjacent to the groin will be swollen and noticeably swollen, indicating that the head of the thigh bone has moved there.
The signs of a dislocation outward will be the opposite of these signs.  A sign of a forward dislocation is that the patient can fully extend their leg, but cannot bend it without pain, and if they try to walk, they cannot move forward.
The patient will retain urine and the groin will be painful.  When walking, they will place their weight on the heel.  A sign of a backward dislocation is that the patient cannot straighten their knee, nor can they bend it before bending the groin.  The leg will also be shorter than the other.
The groin will be relaxed, and the head of the thigh bone will be clearly visible at the hip area.  As for the reduction of these dislocations, look; if the dislocation is longstanding and the patient has not attempted reduction, and it remains in its state, then there is no treatment at all.
Therefore, it is not advisable to encounter it.  However, if the dislocation is recent, and of any of the four types, immediately rotate and extend the joint in and out, and move it right and left.  Perhaps...
If it returns without further treatment, then if it does not return this way, it is necessary to prepare a strong, capable servant who will extend the leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied above the knee, and another servant to extend it from above.
By inserting their hands under his armpits, then tightening a soft bandage around the base of the thigh, and another servant holding the end of the bandage, and the extension is either from the front from the groin toward the collarbone, or from behind towards the back.
All of them should extend at once, until the patient is lifted from the ground and remains suspended. This type of extension is common to all four types. If the dislocation returns by what we have described, otherwise, each type requires its own specific treatment, as described.
If the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on their sound side, then a bandage is placed on the base of the thigh, between the head of the thigh and the area below the groin.
Then, the bandage is extended upward from the groin towards the upper part of the body, toward the collarbone.  A strong servant then takes hold of the thick part of the affected thigh with both arms and pulls it outward with great force, and it will return to its place.
This type is easier than other methods of restoring this limb. If this method fails, and the dislocation does not respond to this treatment, then the patient's legs should be tied together with a strong, soft bandage around the heels and knees.
Each bandage should be approximately four fingers apart. The affected leg should be extended further than the other, by about two fingers.  The patient should then be suspended from a wooden beam in the house, approximately two arms' length above the ground.
Then, a strong servant is instructed to hold the head of the thigh, and another servant is to grasp the patient, and the first servant pushing on the thigh with force, the joint will quickly return to its place.  Now, for its specific reposition if the dislocation is outward.
The patient should lie on a bed, as described for the humpbacked patient, and the bandage is tightened specifically on the affected leg and the chest.  Then, two boards are placed, one at the patient's feet and the other at the head, with an additional, strongly secured board in the middle of the bed.
Damp cloths should be wrapped around it so as not to injure the patient, and the board should be placed between the thighs to prevent downward displacement during stretching.  Then, each servant pulls in their direction and the physician straightens the hip with his hands. If the hip returns to its place.
Otherwise, place the board on it and press it as described for the humpback, except that the patient should lie on their healthy side. If the dislocation is forward, the affected leg should be stretched once while in this same position on the bed.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with the other hand.  At the same time, the pressure should be extended downwards towards the knee.  If the dislocation is backwards, the patient should not be stretched downwards.
He should be elevated from the ground, but rather placed on a firm surface, and the leg with the dislocated hip should also be positioned outward, as described in the patient's posture on the bed, lying on their face with the bandages tightened.
As we stated previously, the board should also be used to apply pressure to the area where the joint has dislocated.  When the hip joint returns properly, a sign of which is easily observed, the patient's legs should be stretched.
If you see the legs are level and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, then know that the limb has returned to its proper position.  At that point, bring the thighs together, apply the bandage, and secure it with a turban-like wrap so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days. Then, remove the bandage and splint the leg alongside the other. If you find them equal in size, know that the joint has stabilized, and allow the patient to walk. If you see any weakness,
Return and bandage it again, tightening it as you did initially. Leave it for three more days. Then remove it, and have the patient walk slowly for a number of days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On the treatment of the knee joint.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (i.e., backward).  It does not dislocate forward.  A sign of dislocation is to instruct the patient to pull their leg towards their thigh.
If the leg does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated.  To treat all types of dislocation, have the patient sit with their legs extended, if able.  A helper should sit behind them to hold their waist and gently turn it backward.  Then sit upon their thighs.
Then place your back against his body, and position his leg between your legs.  Hold his knee with both hands, interlocking your fingers around it.  Then firmly press the sides of his knee with your hands, while another assistant extends the leg, until the knee returns to its place.
A sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh without force, smoothly.  Then bandage it, firmly securing the leg to the thigh.  Then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then remove it.  Only light walking should be done for several days, until strength returns.
If you cannot return it using the method described, then use strong traction with bandages, as described previously in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of fractured heels.
The heel may dislocate slightly or completely.  Dislocation may occur inward or outward.  A sign of dislocation is that the heel is swollen and prominent on the side to which it has dislocated.
As for treating its slight displacement, it is easily returned by gently pulling with the hands and straightening it until it returns.  If it is completely dislocated, the patient should sit, and a strong attendant should grasp it from behind, in the middle of his back.
Then, you grasp the foot with your right hand from the top and your left hand from the bottom, at the heel area.  Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand, twice. Then extend it with your left hand and push the top of the foot toward the leg with your right hand, without force.
Do this twice as described.  Then, in the third instance, push the top of the foot toward the leg while pulling on the heel. If it returns in one or two attempts like this and you see the foot is aligned, then do the procedure again. If not, redo it, as it will return.
If it does not return with the method described, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground.  Securely fix and dig a peg into the ground, placing it between their thighs, wrapped in cloth so as not to harm the patient. Then, an attendant holds the patient's thigh.
Then another attendant stretches the leg, either with their hands or with a bandage tied around the patient's neck. Each attendant stretches in the opposite direction of the other, and the peg remains between the patient's thighs, holding it so the patient's body does not pull downwards during the stretching. Then the physician straightens the joint with their hands.
Another attendant holds the healthy leg below.  If the joint returns quickly, then take the bandage and splint, and secure them tightly with bandages.  Bind the foot with a bandage to the bottom, taking care to avoid the nerve located above the heel from behind.
So that the bandage is not too tight and causes him pain.  Then leave it for two or three days.  If the bandage loosens, tighten it, then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this period, there is a risk that the joint will dislocate and not be treatable afterward. If a hot swelling appears, then use the treatments and ointments previously described in other sections of the text to resolve it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of fractured fingers.
One should gently align the fractured parts with a slight stretch, without force.  This is not difficult but easy. If the fracture is in the toes, the patient should place their foot on a flat surface.
Or, while standing, on a board.  Then, place your foot on the protruding joint and press down firmly until it is aligned and no protrusion remains.
Then place a board under the sole of the foot, encompassing the whole foot and having two ends.  Tightly bind it for three days. Then unbind it and keep the patient from walking for many days until it is strong and a return of the fracture is prevented, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On the types of fracture that occur with a wound, or with a break, or with both.
When such a thing occurs and you attempt to treat and set it, death often follows.
Therefore, one should not undertake such treatment unless they are skilled in the art, experienced, gentle, compassionate, careful, and not rash or reckless.  One should initially use medications that relieve only hot swellings, and leave the patient alone.
O God, except that which you hope for to be free from harm with a slight illness, and some hope appears in it, then quickly return it in the beginning before the hot swelling occurs.
If the limb returns to the desired state, then use the treatment that relieves hot swellings and treat the wound with suitable drying ointments. If the jaw is fractured and pieces of bone have become detached, then remove them. Follow in this regard what we have mentioned concerning simple illnesses in the preceding passages.
Strive diligently and purify yourself from entering the path of uncertainty, as I have previously advised.  This will preserve your reputation and safeguard your standing, God willing.
